The Syllogism

Reducing the Third Figure to the First Figure via Conversion or Reductio
Explains reduction of the third figure first mood to the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce a third-figure conditional syllogism to the first figure using conversion or reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
Reduction transforms the mood into the first figure, the most evident form, to verify validity.

The first figure is the benchmark of clarity. The author shows two ways to reduce the third figure's first mood: by converting the minor premise, or by assuming the conclusion false and deriving a contradiction. Both confirm the mood's validity.

Guided Path
1
Recall the first mood
Premises: Whenever A-B then J-D, and whenever A-B then H-Z. Conclusion: It may be that if J-D then H-Z.
2
Method 1: Conversion of minor
Convert the minor premise 'Whenever A-B then H-Z' to 'It may be that if H-Z then A-B' (the converse). Then combine with the major 'Whenever A-B then J-D' in the first figure to get 'It may be that if H-Z then J-D'. Finally, convert this conclusion to obtain 'It may be that if J-D then H-Z'.
The reduction via conversion involves converting the minor and then converting the resulting conclusion.
3
Method 2: Reductio ad absurdum
Assume the conclusion 'It may be that if J-D then H-Z' is false, so its contradictory 'Absolutely not if J-D then H-Z' is true. Combine this with the major premise 'Whenever A-B then J-D' to form a first-figure syllogism: Whenever A-B then J-D, and absolutely not if J-D then H-Z, therefore absolutely not if A-B then H-Z. This conclusion contradicts the given minor premise 'Whenever A-B then H-Z'. Therefore the assumption is false, so the original conclusion is true.
This method uses the principle of reductio: assuming the negation leads to a contradiction with a true premise.
4
Compare methods
Conversion is direct but requires an extra conversion step. Reductio is indirect but straightforwardly uses the given premises. Both confirm the validity of the mood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٣] وأما في الشكل الثالث فالضروب فيه كما مر، والشرائط كذلك؛ والضرب الأول وهو قولنا كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د»، وكلما كان «أب» فـ «هز» فإنه ينتج قد يكون إذا كان «ج د» فـ «هز» بالرد إلى الأول؛ وذلك بعكس صغراه، أو بالخلف مثلا كما يقال لو كذب قولنا قد يكون إذا كان «ج د» فـ «هز» لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان «ج د» فـ «هز». فنضمه إلى الصغرى ونقول كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د»، وليس البتة إذا كان «ج د» فـ «هز» فليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فـ «هز»; وقد صدق قولنا كلما كان «أب» فـ ...
[3] As for the third figure, the moods are as have passed, and the conditions likewise; the first mood is our saying 'Whenever A-B then J-D, and whenever A-B then H-Z' yields 'It may be that if J-D then H-Z' by reduction to the first figure; that is by converting its minor premise, or by reductio, for example, as is said: if our statement 'It may be that if J-D then H-Z' were false, then its contradictory would be true, namely our statement 'Absolutely not if J-D then H-Z'. Then we combine it with the minor and say: 'Whenever A-B then J-D, and absolutely not if J-D then H-Z, so absolutely not if A-B then H-Z'; and our statement 'Whenever A-B then ...' is true.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage explains the first mood of the third figure of conditional syllogism. The premises are two universal conditional propositions sharing the same antecedent (A-B). The conclusion is a particular conditional: 'It may be that if J-D then H-Z.' The author explains that this mood can be reduced to the first figure in two ways: first, by converting the minor premise (i.e., from 'Whenever A-B then H-Z' to 'It may be that if H-Z then A-B'), then with the other premise forming a first figure syllogism. Second, by reductio (al-khalf): assume the conclusion is false, i.e., 'Absolutely not if J-D then H-Z', combine with the minor premise to derive 'Absolutely not if A-B then H-Z', which contradicts the given premise 'Whenever A-B then H-Z', thus proving the conclusion. This shows the validity of the mood.

Six Moods of the Third Figure of Conditional Syllogism
Author enumerates and validates the six moods of the third conditional figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the six moods of the third figure of conditional syllogism and determine the valid method of proof (reduction to first figure or reductio) for each.

Core Idea
The third figure's six moods are validated by either reduction to the first figure or reductio ad absurdum.

Conditional syllogisms, like categorical ones, have figures and moods that determine their validity. The third figure of conditional syllogism features six valid moods, each defined by the quantity and quality of its two conditional premises. Mastering these moods equips you to handle hypothetical reasoning in Islamic jurisprudence and theology, where conditional rules are common.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the structure of the third conditional figure
Both premises share the same antecedent (أب) as the subject, and have different consequents (ج د and هـ ز). The conclusion relates the two consequents.
2
Understand the six moods' premise combinations
Each mood is a pair of conditional premises varying in universality (كلما vs. قد يكون) and affirmation/negation (affirmative vs. ليس البتة).
Refer to the logical variables: أ, ب, ج, د, هـ, ز.
3
Note the conclusion for each mood
The conclusion expresses a relationship between the two consequents (ج د and هـ ز) using modal operators like 'قد يكون' (it may be) or 'ليس البتة' (it is absolutely not).
4
Learn the two proof methods
Reduction to first figure is done by converting the minor premise; reductio ad absurdum assumes the conclusion false and derives a contradiction.
Most moods can be proven by either method, except the sixth which requires reductio.
5
Apply the proof methods to each mood
For moods 1-5, practice both conversion and reductio. For mood 6, note that conversion fails because two particular premises do not produce a syllogism.
Use Islamic examples to solidify understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٣] وأما في الشكل الثالث فالضروب فيه كما مر ، والشرائط كذلك؛ والضرب الأول وهو قولنا كلَّما كان «أب» فــ «ج د»، وكلما كان «أب» فـ «هـز» فإنه ينتج قد يكون إذا كان «ج د» فـ «هـز» بالرد إلى الأول؛ وذلك بعكس صغراه، أو بالخلف مثلا كما يقال لو كذب قولنا قد يكون إذا كان ج د فـ هـز لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان «ج د» فـ «هز». فنضمه إلى الصغرى ونقول كلّما كان «أب» فـ «ج د»، وليس البتة إذا كان «ج د» فـ «هـز» فليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فـ «هـز»؛ وقد صدق قولنا كلما كان «أب» فـ «هـز»، هذا خلف. والضرب الثاني وهو قولنا كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د»، وليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فـ «هـز» ينتج قد لا يكون إذا كان «ج د» فـ «هـز» لما مر؛ والضرب الثالث وهو قولنا قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ «ج د»، وكلَّما كان «أب» فـ «هـز» ينتج قد يكون إذا كان «ج د» فـ «هـز» لما مرَّ؛ والضرب الرابع وهو قولنا كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د»، وقد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ «هـز» ينتج قد يكون إذا كان «ج د» فـ «هـز»؛ والضرب الخامس وهو قولنا قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فــ «ج د»، وليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فـ «هـز» ينتج قد لا يكون إذا كان «ج د» فـ «هـز»؛ والضرب السادس وهو قولنا كلما كان «أ ب» فـ «ج د»، وليس كلما كان «أب» فـ «هـز» ينتج ليس كلما كان «ج د» فـ «هـز» لما مرَّ من طريق الخلف. فإن طريق العكس لا يصح في هذه الصورة لما أنه لا قياس عن جزئيتين؛ وقد مرَّ في الأقيسة الحملية أنه يتبين بالافتراض أيضًا، وذلك يعرف بالتأمل.
[3] As for the third figure, the moods are as previously mentioned, and the conditions likewise. The first mood: our statement 'Whenever A is B, it is C D' and 'Whenever A is B, it is H Z' yields 'It may be that when it is C D, it is H Z' by reduction to the first figure; and that is by converting its minor premise, or by reductio ad absurdum, for example as is said: If our statement 'It may be that when it is C D, it is H Z' were false, its contradictory would be true, namely 'It is absolutely not that when it is C D, it is H Z'. Then we combine it with the minor and say: 'Whenever A is B, it is C D' and 'It is absolutely not that when it is C D, it is H Z' so 'It is absolutely not that when A is B, it is H Z'; but our statement 'Whenever A is B, it is H Z' is true; this is a contradiction. The second mood: our statement 'Whenever A is B, it is C D' and 'It is absolutely not that when A is B, it is H Z' yields 'It may not be that when it is C D, it is H Z' as previously. The third mood: our statement 'It may be that when A is B, it is C D' and 'Whenever A is B, it is H Z' yields 'It may be that when it is C D, it is H Z' as previously. The fourth mood: our statement 'Whenever A is B, it is C D' and 'It may be that when A is B, it is H Z' yields 'It may be that when it is C D, it is H Z'. The fifth mood: our statement 'It may be that when A is B, it is C D' and 'It is absolutely not that when A is B, it is H Z' yields 'It may not be that when it is C D, it is H Z'. The sixth mood: our statement 'Whenever A is B, it is C D' and 'Not whenever A is B, it is H Z' yields 'Not whenever it is C D, it is H Z' as previously by the method of reductio. For the method of conversion is not valid in this form because there is no syllogism from two particular premises; and it has previously passed in categorical syllogisms that it is also shown by supposition, and that is known by reflection.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a classification and validation of the six moods (ḍurūb) of the third figure of conditional syllogism. The author first restates that the moods and conditions are as previously mentioned (likely in an earlier section). For each mood, he provides the two premises and the conclusion, along with a method of proof: either by reduction to the first figure (using conversion of the minor premise) or by reductio ad absurdum (al-khalf). The first mood is illustrated in detail: both premises are universal conditionals ('Whenever A is B, it is C D' and 'Whenever A is B, it is H Z'), yielding a particular affirmative conclusion ('It may be that when it is C D, it is H Z'). The proof uses conversion of the minor premise or reductio: assuming the conclusion false leads to a contradiction with the major premise. The second through sixth moods follow analogously, with variations in quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative). The sixth mood requires reductio because conversion would involve two particular premises, which yield no syllogism. The author also notes that in categorical syllogisms, the supposition method (al-iftirāḍ) can be used. An Islamic example for the first mood: 'Whenever an action is obligatory (fard), it is required (wājib); whenever an action is obligatory, it is rewarded (thawāb); therefore, sometimes when it is required, it is rewarded.'

Key Terms
الشكل الثالث
الضرب
الخلف

Key Takeaway
The six moods of the third conditional figure are each proven by either reduction to the first figure or reductio, with the sixth mood requiring reductio exclusively.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the premises: 'Whenever a ruling is abrogated (naskh), it is repealed (mansukh).' and 'Whenever a ruling is abrogated, it is temporally limited (muqayyad).' What conclusion follows, and which mood of the third conditional figure is this? Prove it by reduction or reductio.
?
Identify the mood of the following: 'It may be that when an analogy is valid, it is sound. Not at all when an analogy is valid is it speculative.' What is the conclusion? Use reductio to prove it.

Moods Two to Six of the Conditional Syllogism
Author enumerates six moods of conditional syllogism with examples and reduction methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and validate each of the five additional moods of the conditional syllogism (moods 2-6) as described by the author.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms allow inference between two conditionals sharing the same antecedent.

After learning the first mood (modus ponens and tollens), we now explore five more moods that combine universal and particular conditionals to derive conclusions about the relationship between the consequents. These moods extend the power of conditional reasoning in Islamic logic, often used in theological and jurisprudential arguments. The author uses Arabic letters as placeholders, so we will instantiate them with concrete examples from Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major premise
The major premise is the first conditional in the mood, which can be 'kullama' (whenever) for universal or 'qad yakun' (it may be) for particular. For example, in mood two: 'Whenever A is B, then C is D' is a universal conditional.
Universal conditionals assert the implication holds in all cases; particular conditionals assert it holds in at least one case.
2
Identify the minor premise
The minor premise is the second conditional or its negation. It may be 'laysa al-batta' (absolutely not) for a strong negation, or 'laysa kullama' (not whenever) for a negated universal. For mood two: 'It is absolutely not that if A is B then E is Z' is a negated conditional.
The minor premise often contradicts the implication of the second consequent.
3
Apply the rules to derive the conclusion
Based on the quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of premises, a conclusion is inferred about the relationship between the two consequents. For mood two, from a universal major and a strong negative minor, we conclude 'It may not be that if C is D then E is Z' (a negated possibility).
Each mood has a specific rule; the author lists them systematically.
4
Understand the methods of proof
The author notes that some moods are proved by reduction (khalf) or conversion. For mood six, conversion fails because it would require two particular premises, which is invalid in categorical syllogisms. Instead, reductio ad absurdum is used.
Assumption (iftirad) can also be used, as in categorical syllogisms.
5
Instantiate with Islamic examples
Replace the abstract variables with terms from Islamic sciences. For example, in inheritance fiqh: Let A = 'father dies', B = 'son inherits', C = 'daughter inherits'. Then mood two gives a valid inference about these rulings.
This bridges abstract logic to practical Islamic reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والضرب الثاني وهو قولنا: كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د»، وليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فـ «هـ ز» ينتج قد لا يكون إذا كان «ج د» فـ «هـ ز» لما مر. والضرب الثالث وهو قولنا: قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ «ج د»، وكلَّما كان «أب» فـ «هـ ز» ينتج قد يكون إذا كان «ج د» فـ «هـ ز» لما مر. والضرب الرابع وهو قولنا: كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د»، وقد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ «هـ ز» ينتج قد يكون إذا كان «ج د» فـ «هـ ز». والضرب الخامس وهو قولنا: قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ «ج د»، وليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فـ «هـ ز» ينتج قد لا يكون إذا كان «ج د» فـ «هـ ز». والضرب السادس وهو قولنا: كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د»، وليس كلما كان «أب» فـ «هـ ز» ينتج ليس كلما كان «ج د» فـ «هـ ز» لما مر من طريق الخلف. فإن طريق العكس لا يصح في هذه الصورة لما أنه لا قياس عن جزئيتين؛ وقد مر في الأقيسة الحملية أنه يتبين بالافتراض أيضًا، وذلك يعرف بالتأمل.
The second mood is our saying: Whenever 'A is B' implies 'C is D', and it is absolutely not the case that if 'A is B' then 'E is Z', so it is inferred that it may not be that if 'C is D' then 'E is Z', according to what has passed. The third mood is our saying: It may be that if 'A is B' then 'C is D', and whenever 'A is B' then 'E is Z', so it is inferred that it may be that if 'C is D' then 'E is Z', according to what has passed. The fourth mood is our saying: Whenever 'A is B' then 'C is D', and it may be that if 'A is B' then 'E is Z', so it is inferred that it may be that if 'C is D' then 'E is Z'. The fifth mood is our saying: It may be that if 'A is B' then 'C is D', and it is absolutely not the case that if 'A is B' then 'E is Z', so it is inferred that it may not be that if 'C is D' then 'E is Z'. The sixth mood is our saying: Whenever 'A is B' then 'C is D', and it is not the case that whenever 'A is B' then 'E is Z', so it is inferred that it is not the case that whenever 'C is D' then 'E is Z', according to what has passed via the method of reductio (al-khalf). For the method of conversion (al-'aks) is not valid in this form because there is no syllogism from two particular premises; and it has been mentioned in categorical syllogisms that it is also clarified by assumption (al-iftirad), and that is known by reflection.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage presents five additional moods of the conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i muttasil), following the first mood described earlier. Each mood combines a major premise (a conditional with 'whenever' or 'it may be') and a minor premise (a contradicting or affirming statement about the consequent) to produce a conclusion about the other conditional. The author uses Arabic letters as placeholders (أب for antecedent, ج د for first consequent, هـ ز for second consequent). The second mood uses a 'whenever' major and a negative absolute minor ('absolutely not') to conclude a negated possibility. The third uses a 'may be' major and a 'whenever' minor to conclude a possibility. The fourth uses a 'whenever' major and a 'may be' minor to conclude a possibility. The fifth uses a 'may be' major and a negative absolute minor to conclude a negated possibility. The sixth uses two 'whenever' conditionals with a negative minor to conclude a negated universal conditional, and it relies on reduction (khalf) rather than conversion, because conversion would require two particular premises which are invalid in a categorical syllogism. The author also mentions that assumption (iftirad) can be used, as in categorical syllogisms.

كلما
ليس البتة
الخلف

D) It is not that whenever A is B then E is Z

Key Takeaway
The conditional syllogism has six moods, each combining different quantifiers to produce valid conclusions about the relationship between consequents.

Analyzing Objections to Conditional Syllogisms
Author presents four objections to conditional syllogisms and refutes each with logical reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the four objections against conditional syllogisms and evaluate the author's refutations by identifying hidden assumptions about necessity, circularity, and universality.

Core Idea
Objections to logical forms often misinterpret necessity and universality.

This passage presents four dialectical objections (shubahat) to the validity of conditional syllogisms, followed by systematic refutations. As you work through this exchange, you will learn how a skilled logician defends a logical form by exposing hidden assumptions. The objections target issues of time unity, circular dependence, necessary consequence, and a concrete counterexample. By the end, you will be able to evaluate similar objections in Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four objections
Read the Arabic and translation to locate each objection marked [1], [ب], [ت], [ث]. Write down the core claim of each.
The objections are numbered but not all have explicit answers in the passage.
2
Extract the logical challenge
For each objection, state what the critic claims is wrong with conditional syllogisms. For example, the first objection says time unity is unknowable, so the middle term cannot be fixed.
3
Examine the author's responses
For the first three objections, the author gives answers. Note how he reframes the issue: e.g., reinterpreting universality to include time unity, distinguishing absolute dependence from circularity, and showing that the alleged necessary consequence does not obtain.
The fourth objection lacks an explicit answer; consider whether the previous responses implicitly cover it.
4
Evaluate the strength of each objection
Based on the refutation, decide if the objection is successfully defeated. What assumption does the objector make that the author rejects?
For the fourth, the example uses numbers; think about whether the premises are truly universal.
5
Apply the method to an Islamic example
Construct a similar objection and refutation using an example from fiqh or kalam. For instance, a conditional syllogism about the validity of qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يمتنع أن يورد عليهما من الشبه كما يورد في الضرب الأول؛ وذلك من وجوه: [1] منها أن يقال: إنه لا ينتج إلا وأن يكون الأوسط متحدا، والأوسط لا يكون متحدا إلا وأن يكون الزمان متحدا. والاتحاد في الزمان من جملة ما لا تعرف حاله أنه ما هو وكيف هو فكيف يكون منتجا؟ [ب] ومنها أن يقال: إنه لا ينتج فإن صدق النتيجة مع صدق إحدى المقدمتين إما أن يفتقر كل واحد منهما إلى صاحبه في التحقق؛ وإنه من جملة ما يلزمه الدور، وإما أن لا يفتقر؛ وإنه مما يحقق الافتراق بينهما في الجملة وذلك لا يمكن بصدق النتيجة دون صدق إحدى المقدمتين فيكون بالعكس؛ وإنه هو المنافي لكونه منتجا. [ت] ومنها أن يقال: إنه إذا كان منتجًا فلا يخلو من أن يتحقق أحدهما وهو صدق النتيجة أو صدق إحدى المقدمتين، أو لا يتحقق. فإن لم يتحقق فظاهر، وإن يتحقق فكذلك ضرورة تحقق نقيض هذا مع وجود ذلك وإلا لكان النقيض مستلزما لوجود ذلك، ومنافيًا إياه؛ وذلك محال. [ث] ومنها أن يقال: إنه إذا كان منتجا كان منتجا في هذه الصورة وهي قولنا كلما كان الاثنان فردًا فهو أولي وكلما كان فردًا فهو غير زوج؛ ولا يكون منتجا فإنه لا يصدق أن يقال قد يكون إذا كان أوليًّا فهو غير زوج. وإنه أولي دائما وزوج كذلك؛ والمعنى من الأولي عدد لا يعده إلا واحد. وأما الجواب عنها فنقول [1] في الأول الاتحاد في الزمان من الضروريات فيما نحن فيه وإلا لا تكون الصغرى كلية على التفسير الذي مر ذكره؛ ولا الكبرى كذلك. وكلامنا في كون كل واحدة منهما كلية على ذلك التفسير وهو أن يكون التالي متحققاً عند تحقق المقدم في جميع الأحوال والأوضاع. [ب] وفي الثاني نقول: إن من اللوازم أن يفتقر كل واحد منهما إلى صاحبه وإلا لا يكون القياس منتجا لكن افتقار أحدهما إلى الآخر مطلقًا لا يكون دورًا. ألا ترى أن الافتقار متحقق في سائر الأمور الإضافية؛ ولا دور فيها بل الدور فيما يفتقر كل واحد منهما إلى الآخر افتقار المسبب إلى السبب، وفيما نحن فيه ليس كذلك؛ وذلك يعرف بالتأمل. [ت] وفي الثالث نقول: إذا تحقق أحدهما ولا يتحقق نقيض هذا مع وجود ذلك وإلا لكان هذا المجموع من لوازم أحدهما؛ وذلك لا يمكن لا متناع ارتفاع أحدهما مطلقا عند ارتفاع هذا المجموع مطلقًا. ولا يقال يمكن بأن يكون المجموع متحققاً على ذلك التقدير، ولا يكون من لوازمه فإنه إذا لم يكن من اللوازم لا يكون تحققه ضروريًا على ذلك التقدير.
And it is not impossible that objections be raised against them (the conditional syllogisms) just as they are raised against the first mood; and that from several aspects: [1] Among them is that it is said: It does not produce a conclusion unless the middle term is unified, and the middle term is not unified unless time is unified. And the unification in time is among the things whose state is not known—what it is and how it is—so how can it produce a conclusion? [B] Among them is that it is said: It does not produce a conclusion, for the truth of the conclusion together with the truth of one of the two premises either each of them depends on the other in actualization—and this is among the things that entail circularity—or it does not depend; and this is what establishes the separation between them in general, and that cannot be by the truth of the conclusion without the truth of one of the premises, so it would be the reverse; and that is contrary to its being productive. [T] Among them is that it is said: If it is productive, then it is inevitable that either one of them (the truth of the conclusion or the truth of one premise) is actualized, or it is not. If it is not actualized, it is obvious; and if it is actualized, then likewise, by necessity the contradictory of this occurs with the existence of that, otherwise the contradictory would be entailing the existence of that and contradictory to it; and that is impossible. [Th] Among them is that it is said: If it is productive, then it would be productive in this form: our saying 'Whenever two is odd, it is prime, and whenever it is odd, it is not even'; but it is not productive because it is not true to say 'It may be that if it is prime then it is not even', since it is always prime and even as well; and the meaning of 'prime' is a number that only one counts it. As for the answer to them, we say: [1] Regarding the first: The unification in time is among the necessary things in what we are discussing, otherwise the minor premise would not be universal according to the interpretation that has passed; nor the major premise likewise. And our discussion concerns the universality of each of them according to that interpretation, which is that the consequent is actualized upon the actualization of the antecedent in all states and conditions. [B] Regarding the second: We say: It is among the necessary consequences that each of them depends on the other, otherwise the syllogism would not be productive; but the dependence of one upon the other in an absolute sense does not constitute circularity. Do you not see that dependence is actualized in all relative matters? And there is no circularity in them; rather circularity is where each of them depends on the other with the dependence of an effect on a cause, and in what we are discussing it is not like that; and that is known by reflection. [T] Regarding the third: We say: If one of them is actualized and the contradictory of this is not actualized with the existence of that, then otherwise this composite would be among the necessary consequences of one of them; and that is not possible because it is impossible for one of them to absolutely cease when this composite absolutely ceases. And it cannot be said that it is possible for the composite to be actualized on that supposition and not be among its necessary consequences, for if it is not among the necessary consequences, its actualization is not necessary on that supposition.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage presents four objections against the validity of conditional syllogisms (specifically the second through sixth moods) and then provides three answers (the fourth objection appears unanswered, though the answers may implicitly cover it). The first objection concerns the need for the middle term (the antecedent of both conditionals) to be unified in time; the critic argues that since time unity is unknown, the syllogism cannot be productive. The second objection raises a dilemma: either the conclusion and one premise depend on each other (circularity) or they do not (ensuring separation), both of which allegedly invalidate the syllogism. The third objection posits that if the syllogism is productive, then either the conclusion or one premise is actualized; if not, it's obvious, but if so, the contradictory of the other must also be actualized, leading to a contradiction. The fourth objection gives a concrete counterexample using numbers: 'Whenever two is odd, it is prime; whenever it is odd, it is not even'—but the conclusion 'If it is prime, it is not even' is false because 2 is both prime and even. The author's answers: For the first, he asserts that time unity is a necessary condition in this context, as the universality of the premises is interpreted as the consequent always following the antecedent in all states, including time. For the second, he clarifies that dependence between premise and conclusion is not circular; it is akin to relative matters where mutual dependence exists without circularity, unlike cause-effect dependence. For the third, he argues that if one is actualized and the contradictory of the other is not, that composite is not a necessary consequence of either premise, so no contradiction arises.

دور
لازم
استلزام

Key Takeaway
The validity of a conditional syllogism depends on the proper understanding of necessity and universality; objections often rely on a misinterpretation of these concepts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Refutation of Objections to Conditional Syllogism Productivity
Author refutes four objections to conditional syllogism productivity using odd/even/prime example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze and evaluate the author's responses to four objections against the productivity of conditional syllogisms.

Core Idea
A conditional syllogism is productive when the consequent follows necessarily in all circumstances.

In this passage, the author defends the productivity of conditional syllogisms against four objections using a mathematical example about odd, even, and prime numbers. You will learn how to identify these objections, understand the author's counterarguments, and assess their logical strength. This is a key skill in logical debate (jadal) as practiced in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Restate the conditional syllogism
Identify the conditional premises: 'Whenever two is odd, it is prime' and 'Whenever something is odd, it is not even'. The intended conclusion is 'If it is prime, it is not even'. Determine whether this is a valid hypothetical syllogism.
The conclusion is actually false for the number 2, which is both prime and even.
2
List the four objections
From the passage, extract the four objections: (1) truth by conversion leads to contradiction, (2) circularity between premises, (3) the aggregate of premises is impossible, (4) the counterexample with 2 being prime and even.
The objections are marked with symbols [ت] and [ث] etc.
3
Analyze the author's responses
For each objection, summarize the author's answer: (1) unity in time is required for universality, (2) dependence is not circular unless cause-effect, (3) the aggregate need not be a necessary concomitant, (4) the answer from the first figure and the weak response about necessary concomitance.
Pay attention to the distinction between mutual dependence and circular reasoning.
4
Evaluate the refutation
Assess whether the author's responses successfully refute the objections. Consider the strength of the fourth response, which the author himself considers weak.
This step requires critical thinking about logical dialectic.

صدق إحدى المقدمتين فيكون بالعكس؛ وإنه هو المنافي لكونه منتجا. [ت] ومنها أن يقال: إنه إذا كان منتجا فلا يخلو من أن يتحقق أحدهما وهو صدق النتيجة أو صدق إحدى المقدمتين، أو لا يتحقق. فإن لم يتحقق فظاهر، وإن يتحقق فكذلك ضرورة تحقق نقيض هذا مع وجود ذلك وإلا لكان النقيض مستلزما لوجود ذلك، ومنافيا إياه؛ وذلك محال . [ث] ومنها أن يقال: إنه إذا كان منتجا كان منتجا في هذه الصورة وهي قولنا كلما كان الاثنان فردا فهو أولي وكلما كان فردا فهو غير زوج؛ ولا يكون منتجا فإنه لا يصدق أن يقال قد يكون إذا كان أوليا فهو غير زوج . وإنه أولي دائما وزوج كذلك؛ والمعنى من الأولي عدد لا يعده إلا واحد. وأما الجواب عنها فنقول [1] في الأول الاتحاد في الزمان من الضروريات فيما نحن فيه وإلا لا تكون الصغرى كلية على التفسير الذي مر ذكره؛ ولا الكبرى كذلك. وكلامنا في كون كل واحدة منهما كلية على ذلك التفسير وهو أن يكون التالي متحققا عند تحقق المقدم في جميع الأحوال والأوضاع. [ب] وفي الثاني نقول: إن من اللوازم أن يفتقر كل واحد منهما إلى صاحبه وإلا لا يكون القياس منتجا لكن افتقار أحدهما إلى الآخر مطلقا لا يكون دورا. ألا ترى أن الافتقار متحقق في سائر الأمور الإضافية؛ ولا دور فيها بل الدور فيما يفتقر كل واحد منهما إلى الآخر افتقار المسبب إلى السبب، وفيما نحن فيه ليس كذلك؛ وذلك يعرف بالتأمل. [ت] وفي الثالث نقول: إذا تحقق أحدهما ولا يتحقق نقيض هذا مع وجود ذلك وإلا لكان هذا المجموع من لوازم أحدهما؛ وذلك لا يمكن لامتناع ارتفاع أحدهما مطلقا عند ارتفاع هذا المجموع مطلقا. ولا يقال يمكن بأن يكون المجموع متحققا على ذلك التقدير، ولا يكون من لوازمه فإنه إذا لم يكن من اللوازم لا يكون تحققه ضروريا على ذلك التقدير : والقادح يدعي كونه ضروريا. [ث] وفي الرابع نقول: الجواب عنه هو الجواب الذي مر ذكره في الشكل الأول عن الشبهة الرابعة؛ وقد يقال في الجواب عنه إنه لا يكون وارداً لما أن الصغرى فيه لا تكون صادقة إلا وأن يكون كون العدد أوليا من لوازم كونه فردا. غير أنه ضعيف جدا فإن كون الاثنين أوليا على تقدير كونه فردا ضروري وإن لم يكن ذلك من لوازم كون العدد فردا.
The truth of one of the two premises, so it is by conversion; and it is the opposite of its being productive. [T] Among them is that it is said: If it is productive, then it is not free from either that one of them is realized, which is the truth of the conclusion or the truth of one of the two premises, or it is not realized. If it is not realized, it is apparent; and if it is realized, then likewise, necessarily the contradictory of this is realized with the existence of that, otherwise the contradictory would entail the existence of that and be opposite to it; and that is impossible. [Th] Among them is that it is said: If it is productive, it is productive in this form, namely our statement: Whenever two is odd, it is prime; and whenever something is odd, it is not even; but it is not productive, for it is not true that it is said: It may be that if it is prime, it is not even. And it is always prime and even likewise; and the meaning of 'prime' is a number that only one counts it. As for the answer to them, we say: [1] Regarding the first, unity in time is among the necessities in what we are discussing, otherwise the minor premise would not be universal according to the interpretation that has passed; nor would the major be so. And our discussion is about each of them being universal according to that interpretation, which is that the consequent is realized when the antecedent is realized in all states and conditions. [B] Regarding the second, we say: Among the necessary concomitants is that each of them depends on its companion, otherwise the syllogism would not be productive; but the dependence of one on the other absolutely is not a circle. Do you not see that dependence is realized in other relative matters; and there is no circularity in them; rather circularity is when each of them depends on the other as the dependence of the effect on the cause, and in what we are discussing it is not so; and that is known by reflection. [T] Regarding the third, we say: If one of them is realized and the contradictory of this is not realized with the existence of that, otherwise this aggregate would be among the necessary concomitants of one of them; and that is impossible because of the impossibility of the elevation of one of them absolutely when this aggregate is elevated absolutely. And it cannot be said that it is possible for the aggregate to be realized on that estimation, and it is not among its necessary concomitants, for if it is not among the necessary concomitants, its realization is not necessary on that estimation: and the objector claims it is necessary. [Th] Regarding the fourth, we say: The answer to it is the answer that has passed in the first figure concerning the fourth doubt; and it may be said in answer to it that it is not applicable because the minor premise in it is not true unless the fact that the number is prime is among the necessary concomitants of its being odd. But it is very weak, for the fact that two is prime on the estimation of its being odd is necessary even if that is not among the necessary concomitants of the number being odd.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange where the author refutes objections against the productivity of a conditional syllogism. The author begins by presenting two objections (marked [ت] and [ث]) regarding the syllogism about odd and even numbers. The first objection argues that if the syllogism is productive, then one of the premises must be true by conversion, but this leads to a contradiction. The second objection claims that the specific syllogism 'Whenever two is odd, it is prime; whenever something is odd, it is not even' is not productive because the conclusion 'If something is prime, it is not even' is false. The author then responds with answers. For the first objection, the author insists that unity in time is necessary for the minor premise to be universal in the sense that the consequent holds whenever the antecedent holds in all circumstances. For the second, the author clarifies that dependence between premises does not imply circularity unless it is like effect to cause. For the third objection (implied), the author discusses the necessity of the aggregate. For the fourth objection, the author references an answer from an earlier discussion about the first figure and notes a weak response about the necessary concomitance of 'prime' and 'odd'.

A conditional syllogism is productive only when the consequent follows necessarily from the antecedent in all circumstances, and mutual dependence does not entail circularity unless it is cause-effect.

The Five Moods of the Fourth Figure of Conditional Syllogism
Author enumerates and proves the five valid moods of the fourth figure of conditional syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the five moods of the fourth figure of conditional syllogism and apply the conversion or reductio method to validate them.

Core Idea
Fourth figure moods are proven by conversion or reductio, not direct deduction.

In this passage, the author lists and proves the five valid moods of the fourth figure of conditional syllogisms. This figure places the middle proposition as the antecedent in the major premise and the consequent in the minor premise. The proofs rely on two methods: converting the premises or using reductio ad absurdum. Understanding these moods allows you to recognize valid conditional arguments that might otherwise be overlooked.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood structure
Look at the two conditional premises. In the fourth figure, the middle proposition appears as the antecedent in the major premise and the consequent in the minor premise. Determine the quantifiers (universal 'كلما', possible 'قد يكون', absolute negation 'ليس البتة').
The quantifiers determine the mood number.
2
Apply the conversion method
For the first mood, swap the major and minor premises to form a first figure syllogism, then convert the conclusion. Check that the resulting conclusion matches the predicted mood outcome.
This works for moods where both premises are universal or possible.
3
Apply the reductio method
Assume the conclusion is false (i.e., assert its contradictory). Combine this with one of the original premises and derive a contradiction with a known truth. This proves the original conclusion.
The author illustrates this with the first mood.
4
Verify the term contradiction in mood 3
In mood 3, the minor term (consequent of major) contradicts the middle term, so it must contradict the major term (consequent of minor). This supports the absolute negation conclusion.
This reasoning is unique to mood 3.
5
Check the remaining moods
Moods 2, 4, and 5 combine possibility or universality with negation. Confirm they follow the same proof patterns as the first mood, adjusting for quantifiers.
The author states they follow 'due to what preceded'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في الشكل الرابع فالضروب فيه كما مر، والشرائط كذلك. أما الضرب الأول وهو قولنا: كلما كان (أ) ب فـ (ج) د، وكلما كان (ه) ز فـ (أ) ب، ينتج قد يكون إذا كان (ج) د فـ (ه) ز، إما بجعل الصغرى كبرى والكبرى صغرى ثم عكس النتيجة، وإما بالخلف مثلا كما يقال: لو كذب قولنا قد يكون إذا كان (ج) د فـ (ه) ز صدق نقيضه وهو قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان (ج) د فـ (ه) ز، فنضمه إلى الصغرى ونقول: كلما كان (أ) ب فـ (ج) د، وليس البتة إذا كان (ج) د فـ (ه) ز، فليس البتة إذا كان (أ) ب فـ (ه) ز، وقد صدق قولنا قد يكون إذا كان (أ) ب فـ (ه) ز، وهو عكس الكبرى في القياس الذي مر ذكره. والضرب الثاني وهو قولنا: كلما كان (أ) ب فـ (ج) د، وقد يكون إذا كان (ه) ز (أ) ب، ينتج قد يكون إذا كان (ج) د فـ (ه) ز، لما مر. والضرب الثالث وهو قولنا: ليس البتة إذا كان (أ) ب فـ (ج) د، وكلما كان (ه) ز فـ (أ) ب، ينتج ليس البتة إذا كان (ج) د فـ (ه) ز، لما مر ولأن الأصغر وهو (ج) د إذا نافى الأوسط وهو (أ) ب نافى ملزومه وهو الأكبر، وذلك (ه) ز. والضرب الرابع وهو قولنا: كلما كان (أ) ب فـ (ج) د، وليس البتة إذا كان (ه) ز فـ (أ) ب، ينتج ليس كلما كان (ج) د فـ (ه) ز، لما مر. والضرب الخامس وهو قولنا: قد يكون إذا كان (أ) ب فـ (ج) د، وليس البتة إذا كان (ه) ز فـ (أ) ب، ينتج ليس كلما كان (ج) د فـ (ه) ز، لما مر. وبيان الحصر في الضروب المذكورة قد مر في كل شكل من الأشكال الأربعة في القياسات الحملية.
As for the fourth figure, the moods are as previously mentioned, and the conditions likewise. As for the first mood: when we say 'Whenever A is B, then C is D' and 'Whenever E is Z, then A is B', it yields 'It is possible that whenever C is D, then E is Z', either by making the minor the major and the major the minor then converting the conclusion, or by reductio, for example: if our statement 'It is possible that whenever C is D, then E is Z' were false, its contradictory would be true, namely 'Absolutely not: whenever C is D, then E is Z'. Then we join it to the minor and say: 'Whenever A is B, then C is D' and 'Absolutely not: whenever C is D, then E is Z', so it yields 'Absolutely not: whenever A is B, then E is Z'. And our statement 'It is possible that whenever A is B, then E is Z' is true, and it is the converse of the major in the syllogism previously mentioned. The second mood: when we say 'Whenever A is B, then C is D' and 'It is possible that whenever E is Z, then A is B', it yields 'It is possible that whenever C is D, then E is Z', due to what preceded. The third mood: when we say 'Absolutely not: whenever A is B, then C is D' and 'Whenever E is Z, then A is B', it yields 'Absolutely not: whenever C is D, then E is Z', due to what preceded and because the minor term, which is 'C is D', when it contradicts the middle term 'A is B', it contradicts its necessary consequent, which is the major term, namely 'E is Z'. The fourth mood: when we say 'Whenever A is B, then C is D' and 'Absolutely not: whenever E is Z, then A is B', it yields 'Not always: whenever C is D, then E is Z', due to what preceded. The fifth mood: when we say 'It is possible that whenever A is B, then C is D' and 'Absolutely not: whenever E is Z, then A is B', it yields 'Not always: whenever C is D, then E is Z', due to what preceded. And the explanation of the enumeration of the mentioned moods has already passed in each of the four figures of categorical syllogisms.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage defines and proves the five valid moods of the fourth figure of conditional syllogisms. The author begins by stating that the moods and conditions are as previously given. For the first mood, he gives two methods of proof: conversion (by swapping minor and major and then converting the conclusion) and reductio ad absurdum (assuming the conclusion false leads to a contradiction with a true statement). He illustrates reductio with an example. The second mood is similar but with a possible premise for the minor, yielding a possible conclusion. The third mood uses absolute negation and a universal affirmative premise, concluding with absolute negation; the author adds a justification based on the contradiction of terms: if the minor contradicts the middle, it contradicts the large term. The fourth and fifth moods combine a universal or possible premise with an absolute negation, yielding a negated universal conclusion. He concludes that the enumeration of moods has already been covered for categorical syllogisms. All examples use abstract variables (أ, ب, etc.) which are standard in logical treatises.

شكل رابع
ضرب
خلف

Key Takeaway

Objections and Answers: Defending the Fourth Figure
Author presents four objections against the fourth figure's validity and gives answers to the first two.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze objections to conditional syllogisms in the fourth figure and evaluate the validity of the responses.

Core Idea
The fourth figure's productivity hinges on precise relationships between the middle term and extremes.

The fourth figure of conditional syllogism faced several objections from logicians. In this passage, the author presents four such objections and provides answers to the first two. By understanding these objections and responses, you will deepen your grasp of how conditional syllogisms are validated in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the first objection
The objection claims the first mood of the fourth figure is not productive unless the middle term is unified, and then argues that unification would allow the middle to be more specific than the major, which is impossible.
Note the logical structure: the objection uses a conditional (if unified then more specific) and then denies the possibility of more specific.
2
Analyze the answer to the first objection
The author responds by clarifying that the middle term cannot be more specific; it is either equal or more general. This refutes the objection's claim that unification leads to impossibility.
The answer hinges on the relationship between minor, middle, and major.
3
Read the second objection and its answer
The second objection argues that unity of the middle would force it to be a necessary condition of something that is a condition of the minor, leading to equality of middle and minor. The answer denies that this entailment follows.
Focus on the logic of necessary condition and consequent.
4
Consider the third and fourth objections
The third objection questions self-productivity, and the fourth gives a concrete example. The text does not provide full answers for these, so consider what a possible response might be based on the principles of the fourth figure.
This step encourages critical thinking beyond the given text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يورد في هذا الشكل من الشبه أيضًا؛ وذلك بوجوه: [1] منها أن يقال: في الضرب الأول إنه لا يكون منتجا إلا وأن يكون الأوسط متحدا؛ وذلك في حيز المنع فإنه إذا كان متحدا كان من الممكن أن يكون أخص من الأكبر لما أنه يمكن أن يكون الأصغر مساويًا للأكبر. ولا يمكن أن يكون أخص من الأكبر فإنه من لوازم الأكبر. [ب] ومنها أن يقال: إن الأوسط لا يكون متحدا في هذه الصورة إلا وأن يكون لازما لشيء هو ملزوم لذلك الشيء وهو الأصغر حال كونه ملزوما للأكبر؛ وذلك لا يمكن وإلا لكان من اللوازم أن يكون الأوسط مساويًا في التحقق للأصغر، وليس كذلك. [ت] ومنها أن يقال: إنه إذا كان منتجا فإما أن يكون منتجا لذاته؛ وذلك ممتنع لما مر من قبل في القياسات الحملية، وإما أن يكون بتوسط الغير؛ وإنه من جملة ما ينافي كونه منتجا لذاته. فيلزم أن لا يكون منتجا لذاته، وإن كان منتجاً في نفس الأمر. [ث] ومنها أن يقال إنه إذا كان منتجا كان منتجا في هذه الصورة وهي قولنا كلما كان الإنسان فرسا كان صهالا، وكلما كان صهالا كان فرسا؛ ولا يكون منتجا فإنه لا يصح أن يقال قد يكون إذا كان الإنسان صهالا كان فرسا. وأما الجواب فنقول [1] في الأول : لا يمكن أن يكون الأوسط أخص من الأكبر بل يكون مساويا للأكبر أو أعم منه فإن الأصغر لا يخلو من أن يكون مساويًا للأكبر أو لا يكون. فإن كان مساويًا يكون الأوسط مساويًا له أيضًا. وإن لم يكن مساويًا له يكون أعم منه لامتناع كونه أخص فيكون الأوسط أعم منه كذلك وإلا لكان أخص منه؛ وذلك لا يمكن. [ب] وفي الثاني نقول : لا يلزم من كون الأوسط متحدًا أن يكون لازما للشيء وهو ملزوم لذلك الشيء.
And objections are also raised against this figure, in several ways: [1] One is that it is said: In the first mood, it is not productive unless the middle term is unified; but this is subject to denial because if it is unified, it is possible that it be more specific than the major, since it is possible that the minor be equal to the major. But it cannot be more specific than the major, for it is among the necessary consequents of the major. [B] Another is that it is said: The middle term is not unified in this form unless it is a necessary consequent of something that is the necessary condition of that thing, namely the minor while being a necessary condition of the major; but that is impossible, otherwise it would follow that the middle term is equivalent in actuality to the minor, and it is not so. [T] Another is that it is said: If it is productive, then either it is productive by itself, which is impossible due to what preceded in categorical syllogisms, or it is productive by means of something else, and this is among the things that contradict its being productive by itself. So it follows that it is not productive by itself, even if it is productive in reality. [Th] Another is that it is said: If it is productive, then it is productive in this form, which is our saying 'Whenever a human is a horse, he is neighing,' and 'Whenever he is neighing, he is a horse'; but it is not productive, for it is not correct to say 'It is possible that whenever a human is neighing, he is a horse.' As for the answer, we say [1] to the first: It is not possible for the middle term to be more specific than the major; rather it is equal to the major or more general than it. For the minor is either equal to the major or not. If it is equal, then the middle is also equal to it. If it is not equal, then it is more general than it, because it cannot be more specific, so the middle is also more general than it, otherwise it would be more specific; and that is impossible. [B] To the second, we say: It does not follow from the unity of the middle term that it is a necessary consequent of something that is a necessary condition of that thing.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage presents four objections (shubah) raised against the validity of the fourth figure of conditional syllogism, along with answers to the first two. The first objection claims that for the first mood to be productive, the middle term must be unified (i.e., identical in meaning) with something, but this is denied because if unified, the middle could be more specific than the major, which is impossible since it is a consequent of the major. The answer clarifies that the middle cannot be more specific; it is either equal or more general. The second objection argues that unity of the middle would require it to be a necessary condition of something that itself is a necessary condition of the minor, leading to equality of middle and minor, which is false. The answer denies the entailment: unity does not imply that relationship. The third objection says productivity cannot be by itself (based on earlier categorical proofs), so it must be by another, which contradicts self-productivity; but this is left unanswered in the text. The fourth objection gives a concrete example: 'Whenever a human is a horse, he is neighing; whenever he is neighing, he is a horse' does not yield a valid conclusion about possibility. The text does not provide an explicit answer to the fourth. The objections and answers demonstrate the rigorous scrutiny applied to logical forms within the Islamic logical tradition.

The fourth figure of conditional syllogism is defended by showing that the middle term cannot be more specific than the major; it is always equal or more general, preserving productivity.

Refuting Objections to the Second Figure of Conditional Syllogisms
Author presents four objections to the second figure of conditional syllogisms and refutes each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute four common objections to the second figure of conditional syllogisms by applying the author's reasoning about the middle term's extension, the necessary consequence relation, and the distinction between productivity and appearance.

Core Idea
Objections to logical forms often stem from misunderstanding the middle term's role; the author exposes these fallacies.

The second figure of conditional syllogisms (qiyās istithnā'ī) is powerful but often challenged. In this passage, the author presents four objections and systematically refutes each. By studying these refutations, you will sharpen your ability to defend valid syllogistic forms and identify invalid criticisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection's logical structure
For each objection, locate the claim that the syllogism is not productive unless certain conditions hold. Determine what condition is being asserted (e.g., middle term must be identical, or must be both a necessary consequence and condition).
The objectors are trying to show that the second figure is flawed.
2
Analyze the author's refutation strategy
Notice how the author either denies the condition (e.g., middle term cannot be more specific) or shows it does not lead to the claimed impossibility. For the first objection, he argues the middle term is either equal or more general.
The author uses distinctions (equality, generality) and logical principles (e.g., transitivity of consequence).
3
Extract the key logical principle
For each answer, identify the principle used. For the first: the middle term must be equal or more general than the major, never more specific. For the second: a necessary consequence of a necessary consequence does not necessarily follow the original.
These principles protect the second figure from invalid objections.
4
Apply the principle to a new example
Imagine a conditional syllogism in Islamic law, e.g., 'If a sale involves uncertainty (gharar), it is invalid. If a contract is invalid, it does not transfer ownership. Therefore, a sale with gharar does not transfer ownership.' Raise the first objection (middle term not identical) and refute using the author's principle.
This shows the practical utility of the refutations.

Source Text

ف «هز» لما مر . وبيان الحصر في الضروب المذكروة قد مرَّ في كل شكل من الأشكال الأربعة في القياسات الحملية. وقد يورد في هذا الشكل من الشبه أيضًا؛ وذلك بوجوه: [1] منها أن يقال: في الضرب الأول إنه لا يكون منتجا إلا وأن يكون الأوسط متحدا؛ وذلك في حيز المنع فإنه إذا كان متحدا كان من الممكن أن يكون أخص من الأكبر لما أنه يمكن أن يكون الأصغر مساويًا للأكبر. ولا يمكن أن يكون أخص من الأكبر فإنه من لوازم الأكبر. [ب] ومنها أن يقال: إن الأوسط لا يكون متحدا في هذه الصورة إلا وأن يكون لازما لشيء هو ملزوم لذلك الشيء وهو الأصغر حال كونه ملزوما للأكبر؛ وذلك لا يمكن وإلا لكان من اللوازم أن يكون الأوسط مساويًا في التحقق للأصغر، وليس كذلك. [ت] ومنها أن يقال: إنه إذا كان منتجا فإما أن يكون منتجا لذاته؛ وذلك ممتنع لما مر من قبل في القياسات الحملية، وإما أن يكون بتوسط الغير؛ وإنه من جملة ما ينافي كونه منتجا لذاته. فيلزم أن لا يكون منتجا لذاته، وإن كان منتجاً في نفس الأمر. [ث] ومنها أن يقال إنه إذا كان منتجا كان منتجا في هذه الصورة وهي قولنا كلما كان الإنسان فرسا كان صهَّالا ، وكلَّما كان صهالا كان فرسا؛ ولا يكون منتجا فإنه لا يصح أن يقال قد يكون إذا كان الإنسان صهالا كان هالا. وأما الجواب فنقول [1] في الأول : لا يمكن أن يكون الأوسط أخص من الأكبر بل يكون مساويا للأكبر أو أعم منه فإن الأصغر لا يخلو من أن يكون مساويًا للأكبر أو لا يكون. فإن كان مساويًا يكون الأوسط مساويًا له أيضًا. وإن لم يكن مساويًا له يكون أعم منه لامتناع كونه أخص فيكون الأوسط أعم منه كذلك وإلا لكان أخص منه ؛ وذلك لا يمكن [ب] وفي الثاني نقول : لا يلزم من كون الأوسط متحدًا أن يكون لازما للشيء وهو ملزوم لذلك الشيء. وإن كان من الأشياء ما يكون لازما للشيء وملزوما لذلك الشيء كذلك لكن يلزم منه أن يكون ملزوما لشيء وهو الأصغر ، ولازما لشيء هو من لوازم ذلك الشيء وهو الأكبر؛ ولازم لازم الشيء لا يلزم أن يكون لازما لذلك الشيء يعرف من بعد. [ت] وفي الثالث [ نقول] إنه منتج لذاته لما مر من قبل لكن لا يظهر إنتاجه بالعوض للغير نحو الردّ إلى الأول والخلف وغير ذلك؛ والظهور غير الإنتاج لا محالة. [ث] وفي الرابع نقول: مثل هذا التركيب لا يكون من التركيبات القياسية فإن الحدود المختلفة فيها من اللوازم؛ وليس كذلك في هذا ولا فيما هو بمثله أيضًا، وهذا ظاهر.
Regarding 'HZ' as previously. The enumeration of the mentioned moods has already been covered in each of the four figures of categorical syllogisms. Also, in this figure, objections are raised through several aspects: [1] Among them is the claim that in the first mood it is not productive unless the middle term is identical [in extension]; this is subject to denial because if it is identical, it could be more specific than the major term, since the minor can be equal to the major. But it cannot be more specific than the major because it is a necessary consequence of the major. [B] Another is the claim that the middle term is not identical in this form unless it is a necessary consequence of something that is the necessary condition of that thing, namely the minor while being a necessary condition for the major; this is impossible, otherwise it would follow that the middle term is equal in realization to the minor, which is not the case. [T] Another is the claim that if it is productive, it must be either productive by itself—which is impossible as previously stated in categorical syllogisms—or through the mediation of another; this is among the things that contradict its being productive by itself. Thus it follows that it is not productive by itself, even if it is productive in reality. [Th] Another is the claim that if it is productive, it would be productive in this form: 'Whenever a human is a horse, it neighs, and whenever it neighs, it is a horse'; but it is not productive, for it is not correct to say 'If a human is neighing, it is dying.' As for the answer: [1] Regarding the first: the middle term cannot be more specific than the major; rather, it is equal to the major or more general. For the minor is either equal to the major or not. If it is equal, the middle term is also equal to it. If it is not equal, [the middle term] is more general than it, because it cannot be more specific; thus the middle term is more general likewise, otherwise it would be more specific, which is impossible. [B] Regarding the second: we say that the middle term being identical does not necessitate that it is a necessary consequence of something that is the necessary condition of that thing. Even if there are things that are both necessary consequences and necessary conditions of something else, it follows that it is a necessary condition of something (the minor) and a necessary consequence of something that is among the necessary consequences of that thing (the major); and the necessary consequence of a necessary consequence of a thing does not necessarily follow that thing, as will be known later. [T] Regarding the third: we say that it is productive by itself, as previously stated, but its productivity does not appear through substitution for another, such as reduction to the first figure or contradiction, etc. Appearance is not productivity, undoubtedly. [Th] Regarding the fourth: we say that such a composition is not among syllogistic compositions, because the different terms are among the necessary consequences; this is not the case here, nor in anything like it. This is clear.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage presents four objections against the second figure of conditional syllogisms (قیاس استثنائی) and provides their refutations. The author first recalls that the enumeration of productive moods in categorical syllogisms has already been covered. Then he lists four objections: (1) The first mood requires the middle term to be identical in extension, but if so, it could be more specific than the major, which is impossible. (2) The middle term's identity forces it to be both a necessary consequence and a necessary condition, which leads to absurdity. (3) The syllogism cannot be productive by itself, but only through mediation, contradicting its self-productivity. (4) The form 'Whenever a human is a horse, it neighs; whenever it neighs, it is a horse' is not productive because it yields an invalid conclusion like 'If a human neighs, it dies.' The answers: (1) The middle term is either equal to or more general than the major, never more specific. (2) The middle term's identity does not entail the claimed relation; a necessary consequence of a necessary consequence does not necessarily follow the original thing. (3) The syllogism is productive by itself, but its productivity may not be apparent through reduction or contradiction. (4) The given example is not a valid syllogistic composition because the terms are not properly ordered; the different terms are among the necessary consequences, not the structured relations of a syllogism. The author uses an unusual example from imaginary creatures (human-horse) to illustrate the point, which is a typical method in logical exercises.

The Objection Against All Four Figures: Absurd Consequences
A general objection against all four figures of syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the general objection against the productivity of all four syllogistic figures and identify its logical structure.

Core Idea
Assuming all figures are productive forces unrelated propositions into entailment, an absurdity.

The author presents a powerful objection against the claim that all four figures of categorical syllogisms are productive (yield a conclusion). He argues that if they were, then any two completely unrelated propositions, like 'Zaid is standing' and 'Amr is sitting', would logically imply each other. This challenge uses the first two figures to derive entailment and contrariety from a composite proposition. Understanding this objection deepens your grasp of syllogistic validity and its limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the assumption under attack
The objection targets the claim that each of the four figures is productive, i.e., that in each figure a valid conclusion follows from the premises. The author assumes this for reduction to absurdity.
This is a standard reductio ad absurdum argument.
2
Construct the unrelated propositions
Take two propositions that are logically independent, e.g., 'Zaid is standing' (call it P) and 'Amr is sitting' (call it Q). They are neither entailing nor excluding each other.
The example shows they are unrelated.
3
Form the composite proposition
Consider the conjunction P and Q (P∧Q). This composite proposition entails each conjunct individually: if P∧Q holds, then P holds, and also Q holds.
Entailment from conjunction to conjunct is immediate.
4
Apply the first figure reasoning
Using the first figure: If P holds then P∧Q holds (since P alone doesn't guarantee Q, this step is problematic; the objection assumes that if P holds, the composite may hold? Actually, the passage says 'If the first holds, the composite holds' – this is not generally true, but the objection uses it as part of the reduction). Then from P∧Q to Q, we get 'If P then Q'. Thus P entails Q, which is false because they are unrelated.
This shows the first figure leads to absurd entailment.
5
Apply the second figure reasoning
Now consider 'P without Q' (P∧¬Q). This proposition entails P. From the second figure, if P holds, then P∧¬Q holds, and if P∧¬Q holds, then P holds, but the second figure would require contrariety between P and Q? Actually, the passage uses the second figure to derive that P is contrary to Q, meaning they cannot both be true, which is also false.
Thus both figures produce false consequences under the assumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من جملة ما يقدح في كل شكل من الأشكال الأربعة أن يقال لو كان كل واحد منها منتجا لكان من اللوازم أن كل أمرين لم يتعلق أحدهما بالآخر كقيام زيد و جلوس عمرو مثلا : فإن أحدهما يكون مستلزما للآخر في الجملة ومنافيا له كذلك؛ وذلك المجموع منهما مستلزم للأول دائما فالأول مما يستلزم في الجملة وكما أنه مستلزم للأول فكذلك مستلزم للثاني فالثاني مما يستلزمه كذلك؛ وحينئذ يلزم من الشكل الأول أن يكون الأول مستلزما للثاني في الجملة فيقال قد يكون إذا كان الأول ثابتا كان المجموع ثابتا، وكلما كان المجموع ثابتا كان الثاني ثابتاً فقد يكون إذا كان الأول ثابتا كان الثاني ثابتاً. وعلى هذا يقال إن الأول بدون الثاني مستلزم للأول دائما فالأول مما يستلزمه في الجملة. وكذلك الثاني بدون الأول مستلزم للثاني دائما فالثاني مما يستلزمه في الجملة أيضًا؛ وحينئذ يلزم من الشكل الثاني أن يكون الأول منافيا للثاني في الجملة فيقال قد يكون إذا كان الأول ثابتا كان الأول بدون الثاني ثابتا وليس البتة إذا كان الثاني ثابتاً كان الأول بدون الثاني ثابتاً فليس كلما كان الأول ثابتا كان الثاني ثابتاً لما مرّ من طريقي العكس والخلف وغير ذلك. وعلى هذا
Then among the things that refute each of the four figures is the claim: if each one of them were productive, then it would be necessary that for any two things that are not related to each other, like Zaid's standing and Amr's sitting for example, one of them would entail the other in some respect and also be contrary to it similarly; and that composite of them always entails the first, so the first is entailed in some respect, and just as it entails the first, so too it entails the second, so the second is also entailed by it. Then from the first figure it would follow that the first entails the second in some respect, so it is said: 'If the first holds, the composite holds, and whenever the composite holds, the second holds, so it could be that if the first holds, the second holds.' And likewise it is said that the first without the second always entails the first, so the first is entailed in some respect. Similarly, the second without the first always entails the second, so the second is also entailed in some respect. Then from the second figure it would follow that the first is contrary to the second in some respect, so it is said: 'If the first holds, then the first without the second holds, but it is not at all the case that if the second holds, then the first without the second holds.' Thus, it is not that whenever the first holds, the second holds, as per the methods of conversion and contradiction and others. And upon this...
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage presents a general objection against all four figures of syllogisms. The objection argues that if all four figures were productive, then for any two unrelated propositions (e.g., 'Zaid is standing' and 'Amr is sitting'), absurd consequences would follow. Specifically, consider the composite proposition (both standing and sitting). This composite entails each part. Using the first figure, if the composite entails the first, and the composite entails the second, then it would follow that the first entails the second, which is false because they are unrelated. Using the second figure, consider the proposition 'first without second' which entails the first. The second figure would then require that the first is contrary to the second, but again, that is not the case. The objection thus shows that the assumption of universal productivity leads to a contradiction. The author mentions that this is through methods like conversion (عكس) and contradiction (خلف). The text breaks off at 'وعلى هذا' (and upon this), likely continuing with further analysis or resolution. The example of Zaid standing and Amr sitting is a standard way to illustrate two logically independent propositions.

Key Takeaway
The objection uses reduction to absurdity: if all figures were productive, any two unrelated propositions would entail each other, which is impossible, so the assumption is false.

Construct an analogous objection for a pair of unrelated propositions from fiqh, e.g., 'The prayer is valid' and 'The fast is broken'. Show that assuming all four figures productive would force one to entail the other, and then identify the syllogistic figure used in your derivation.
?
Using the same pair, apply the second figure reasoning to derive a contrariety, and explain why this shows the assumption is false.

Refuting the Universal Objection Against the Four Figures
Author refutes a false objection that would invalidate all four syllogistic figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and refute the universal objection that the four figures force unwanted relations between unrelated terms.

Core Idea
The universal objection fails because all terms in a syllogism are related via the middle term.

The four figures of the categorical syllogism are foundational in Islamic logic. A persistent objection claims that if they were valid, any two unrelated terms would be forced into a relation, leading to absurdities. In this passage, the author dismantles that objection by exposing its false assumption of total unrelatedness. Let's walk through his refutation step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the universal objection
The objection states: if each figure is productive, then for any two unrelated things (like Zayd standing and 'Amr sitting), one would become a concomitant or contradictory of the other in some respect. This is supposed to be absurd.
2
Trace the objection through each figure
The objector works through figures 1-4, showing how each would force an unwanted relation between the two unrelated terms. For example, in the first figure, it would make the first a concomitant of the second.
The objection uses the terms 'first' and 'second' to stand for the two unrelated things.
3
Identify the author's preliminary points
Before the objection, the author clarifies that the third figure is productive intrinsically, but its productivity is not obvious through substitution. The fourth figure is not properly syllogistic because its terms involve different concomitants.
This shows the author is aware of the nuances of each figure.
4
Locate the core refutation
The author argues that the objection only attacks 'partial concomitances' (ملازمات جزئية), not universal ones. He then insists that any two things always have some relation—they are each a concomitant of and a substratum for something.
Thus, the scenario of absolutely unrelated terms is impossible.
5
Evaluate the refutation's strength
The author concludes that the cause of syllogistic productivity is general, covering all relations. The objection fails because it assumes a false premise.
This is a robust refutation that preserves the validity of the four figures.

And if among things there is what is a concomitant of a thing and a substratum of that thing likewise, yet it follows from this that it is a substratum of a thing which is the minor term, and a concomitant of a thing which is among the concomitants of that thing, which is the major term; and the concomitant of a concomitant of a thing does not necessarily follow that it is a concomitant of that thing, as will be known later. [Th] And in the fourth we say: such a composition is not among syllogistic compositions, for the different terms in it are among the concomitants; and it is not so in this nor in what is like it, and this is clear.

Then among the objections that are raised against each of the four figures is that it could be said: if each of them were productive, then it would follow that for any two things that are not related to each other, like Zayd standing and 'Amr sitting for example, one of them would be a necessary concomitant of the other in some respect and a contradictory to it likewise; and that sum of them is a necessary concomitant of the first always, so the first is among those things that are accompanied in some respect, and just as it is a concomitant of the first, so likewise it is a concomitant of the second, so the second is among things that it accompanies likewise; and then it would follow from the first figure that the first is a necessary concomitant of the second in some respect, so it is said: it may be that when the first is established, the sum is established, and whenever the sum is established, the second is established, so it may be that when the first is established, the second is established. And upon this it is said that the first without the second is a necessary concomitant of the first always, so the first is among things that accompany it in some respect. And likewise the second without the first is a necessary concomitant of the second always, so the second is among things that accompany it in some respect also; and then it would follow from the second figure that the first is contradictory to the second in some respect, so it is said: it may be that when the first is established, the first without the second is established, and it is never the case that when the second is established, the first without the second is established, so it is not the case that whenever the first is established, the second is established, as per the methods of conversion, reduction to the impossible, and others. And upon this it is said that the sum is a necessary concomitant of the first always and of the second likewise; and then it would follow from the third figure that the first is a necessary concomitant of the second in some respect, so it is said: whenever the sum is established, the first is established, and whenever the sum is established, the second is established, so it may be that when the first is established, the second is established, as per the methods of conversion, reduction to the impossible, and others. And upon this it is said that the first without the second is a necessary concomitant of the first, and the second is something that contradicts the first without the second necessarily; and then it would follow from the fourth figure that the first is contradictory to the second in some respect, so it is said: whenever the first without the second is established, the first is established, and it is never the case that when the second is established, the first without the second is established, so it is not the case that whenever the first is established, the second is established, as per the methods of conversion, reduction to the impossible, and others.

And in summary, the objection concerning these partial concomitances is an objection against each of the four figures; and whoever does not engage in the objection against them but rather acknowledges the soundness of these particulars must explain that the particular affirmative among them, as in the first and third figures, how is its state in the case of two mutually contradictory things? And the particular negative among them, as in the second and fourth figures, how is its state in the case of two mutually concomitant things? And it is impossible for something that is contradictory to a thing to be a necessary concomitant of that thing, and for something that is a necessary concomitant of a thing to be contradictory to it likewise, for otherwise it would not be a necessary concomitant of it and a contradiction to it always. And if it is said: our speech concerns what has no relation between one and the other, that is false, because it is impossible for there to be two things except that one of them has a relation to the other. And how not? For each of them has been a concomitant of one thing and a substratum of one thing likewise always. And because what is a cause of productivity is not specific to what has no relation between one and the other; rather it must be general, covering all things in all forms, for there is no thing except that its state with another is like the state mentioned earlier. But if it is said: their state in mutually concomitant and mutually contradictory things is like their state in other things, but the contradictory

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of a universal objection that was raised against the four figures of the categorical syllogism. The author first clarifies some preliminary points about concomitance and the third and fourth figures—noting that the third figure has intrinsic validity but its productivity is not obvious through substitution, and that the fourth figure is not properly syllogistic because its terms involve different concomitants. Then he presents the objection: if every figure were productive, then for any two unrelated things (like Zayd standing and 'Amr sitting), one would necessarily be implied by the other in some respect, which leads to absurdities in each figure. He works through each figure separately, showing how the objection would force an unwanted relation (either concomitant or contradictory) between the two unrelated terms. After stating the objection clearly, the author responds. He explains that the objection only attacks partial concomitances (ملازمات جزئية), not universal ones. He then demands that anyone who concedes the soundness of particular propositions (like particular affirmative in figures 1 and 3, or particular negative in figures 2 and 4) must explain how they function with mutually contradictory or mutually concomitant pairs. He denies that something can be both a necessary concomitant and a contradiction of the same thing. He then rejects the premise that the objection concerns pairs with absolutely no relation, because any two things always have some relation—they are each concomitant of and substratum for something. Finally, he insists that the cause of syllogistic productivity is general and not restricted to unrelated pairs; every pair has a state analogous to what was mentioned. The overall argument is that the objection fails because it assumes a false scenario of total unrelatedness, which does not exist, and it only targets a narrow class of partial concomitances that are not the basis of syllogistic validity.

A) The objection only applies to partial concomitances, and the assumption of totally unrelated terms is false.

Key Takeaway
The universal objection collapses because it wrongly assumes that two terms in a syllogism can be utterly unrelated, whereas they are always linked through the middle term.

Implication and Contradiction in the Third and Fourth Figures
Analysis of implications in the third and fourth figures of conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the particular affirmative and particular negative propositions that arise from the third and fourth figures of conditional syllogisms and explain how they relate to implication and contradiction.

Core Idea
The third figure yields a particular affirmative; the fourth yields a particular negative.

The passage discusses how the composite of a conditional syllogism implies both the first and second parts, leading to implications in the third figure and contradictions in the fourth. Understanding these partial propositions is crucial for evaluating the validity of syllogisms across all four figures. This lesson will walk you through the derivation of these propositions and their logical significance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the composite and its implications
The passage states that the composite (المجموع) implies both the first (الاول) and the second (الثاني) necessarily. Understand that in a conditional syllogism, the composite is the conjunction of premises that together imply each part.
This is a fundamental assumption for the analysis.
2
Derive the third figure implication
From the third figure, it follows that the first implies the second. The author uses methods of conversion and reduction by contradiction to show this. This is a particular affirmative proposition (بعض الأول ثابت فالثاني ثابت).
The third figure has the middle term as subject in both premises.
3
Derive the fourth figure contradiction
From the fourth figure, it follows that the first contradicts the second. Specifically, the first without the second implies the first and second, so the first and second cannot both be false. This yields a particular negative proposition (ليس كلما كان الأول ثابتاً كان الثاني ثابتاً).
The fourth figure has the middle term as predicate in the major premise and subject in the minor.
4
Recognize the importance of these partial propositions
The author emphasizes that challenging these partial implications challenges all four figures. Accepting them is necessary for the validity of syllogisms in each figure.
These propositions are not further reducible without logical consequences.

يقال إن المجموع مستلزم للأول دائمًا وللثاني كذلك؛ وحينئذ يلزم من الشكل الثالث أن يكون الأول مستلزما للثاني في الجملة فيقال كلما كان المجموع ثابتا كان الأول ثابتًا، وكلما كان المجموع ثابتا كان الثاني
