يبين بأن الموجبة الجزئية منها كما في الشكل الأول والثالث كيف حالها في الأمرين المتنافيين والسالبة الجزئية منها كما في الشكل الثاني والرابع كيف حالها في الأمرين المتلازمين. ولا يمكن أن يكون المنافي It is said that the composite necessarily implies the first and also implies the second; and then from the third figure it follows that the first implies the second in general, so it is said: whenever the composite is fixed, the first is fixed, and whenever the composite is fixed, the second is fixed. So it may be that when the first is fixed, the second is fixed, according to the methods of conversion and reduction by contradiction and others. And on this basis it is said that the first without the second implies the first and the second, which contradicts the first without the second necessarily; and then from the fourth figure it follows that the first contradicts the second in general, so it is said: whenever the first without the second is fixed, the first is fixed; but it is not at all the case that when the second is fixed, the first without the second is fixed, so it is not the case that whenever the first is fixed, the second is fixed, according to the methods of conversion and reduction by contradiction and others. In summary, challenging these partial implications is challenging every figure of the four figures. Whoever does not engage in challenging them but acknowledges the truth of these partial propositions must clarify how the particular affirmative among them, as in the first and third figures, is with respect to the two contradictory things, and how the particular negative among them, as in the second and fourth figures, is with respect to the two necessarily implied things. It is impossible that the contradictory of a thing implies that thing, and likewise the implicator cannot be contradictory to it, otherwise it would not be always implicating it and contradicting it. If it is said that we are speaking about [cases] where one has no connection to the other, that is false because it is impossible that there be two things without one having some connection to the other. How could it be, since each one of them is a necessary consequence of one thing and a necessary condition for one thing, always. And because whatever is a cause for inference is not specific to cases where one has no connection to the other, but must be general, covering all things in all forms. There is nothing whose situation with another is other than the situation mentioned earlier. But if it is said their situation with respect to the two necessarily implied and the two contradictory is like their situation with respect to the other, but the contradictory of a thing does not imply it, due to the cause we mentioned, unless it is not contradictory. For the definition of a thing when it is with the thing is not the opposite of that thing; the opposite, as you know, is what cannot be with the thing in one place at one time. Likewise, the implicator of a thing cannot be contradictory to it, due to the cause we mentioned, unless it is not an implicator. For what is a cause of a thing, for example, if it is without that thing, is not a cause of that thing. You have known that the cause is what cannot exist without the effect. That is not false, as known by reflection. It is not far-fetched that the Shaykh said concerning the third figure something like what you mentioned as an objection to that figure and what he answered. But since he said the third figure produces [a conclusion], he said that the particular affirmative is true; but the truth of that particular affirmative does not entail that the contradictory of a thing implies the thing in general. Whoever affirms this implication is far removed from rational principles. The truth is that all these partial propositions are true, for their truth is not dependent on contingent conditions and actual states, while it is impossible that the contradictory of a thing implies the thing.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed syllogistic analysis of the logical relationships between implications and contradictions in the four figures of conditional syllogisms. The author begins by discussing the composite (المجموع) that implies both the first and second components, leading to implications in the third and fourth figures. He uses the methods of conversion (عكس) and reduction by contradiction (خلف) to show how partial propositions (particular affirmative in first and third figures, particular negative in second and fourth) are true. The central argument is that accepting the truth of these partial propositions does not lead to the absurd conclusion that the contradictory of a thing implies the thing itself. The author refutes a potential objection that the cases involve things with no connection, asserting that all things have some connection. He explains that a cause (علة) for inference is universal. He further clarifies using definitions of opposite (ضد) and cause to show that a contradictory cannot imply its opposite, and an implicator cannot be contradictory. He concludes by noting that a scholar (الشيخ) acknowledged the truth of the particular affirmative in the third figure but denied that this implies the contradictory implying the thing, which is against rational principles.

The Syllogism

Why the Contradictory Cannot Imply: Definitions and Principles
Refutation of the claim that contradictory can imply, using definitions of opposite and cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the definitions of al-mustalzim (implicator) and al-munafi (contradictory) to argue that a contradictory cannot imply its opposite, using the concepts of cause and opposition.

Core Idea
A contradictory can never imply what it contradicts; an implicator can never contradict what it implies.

Having established the partial propositions in the previous lesson, the author now refutes a potential misunderstanding: that the truth of these partial propositions might lead to the absurd conclusion that the contradictory of a thing implies that thing. This lesson focuses on the logical impossibility of such an implication, based on the definitions of opposite and cause. You will learn how to use these definitions to block invalid inferences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim being refuted
Some might argue that from the truth of particular affirmatives (e.g., in the third figure), it follows that the contradictory of a thing (نقيض الشيء) implies the thing itself. The author declares this impossible.
This is a classical problem: how can a partial truth lead to a universal absurdity?
2
Examine the refutation of 'no connection'
An objection claims that the two terms might have no relation. The author rejects this, asserting that any two things necessarily have some connection, since each is a consequence of a common cause.
This is a metaphysical principle: all existents are related through causality or implication.
3
Apply the definition of opposite (ضد)
An opposite (or contradictory) is defined as what cannot coexist with the thing in the same subject at the same time. Therefore, if A is opposite to B, A cannot imply B because implication would require that A necessitates B, which would allow coexistence.
The definition of 'ضد' is given in the passage: 'ما لا يمكن أن يكون مع الشيء في محل واحد في زمان واحد'.
4
Apply the definition of cause (علة)
A cause is defined as that without which the effect cannot exist. If A is a cause of B, then A cannot contradict B, because if A contradicts B, then A would require the absence of B, but as a cause, it must be accompanied by B.
The passage says: 'العلة عبارة عما لا يمكن أن يوجد بدون المعلول' (the cause cannot exist without the effect).
5
Conclude with the Shaykh's position
The author notes that a scholar (الشيخ) affirmed the truth of the particular affirmative in the third figure but denied that it implies the contradictory implying the thing. This is because such an implication is against rational principles, as shown by the definitions.
The passage ends by asserting that all partial implications are true, but they do not lead to absurdities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقال إن المجموع مستلزم للأول دائمًا وللثاني كذلك؛ وحينئذ يلزم من الشكل الثالث أن يكون الأول مستلزما للثاني في الجملة فيقال كلما كان المجموع ثابتا كان الأول ثابتًا، وكلما كان المجموع ثابتا كان الثاني ثابتا فقد يكون إذا كان الأول ثابتا كان الثاني ثابتاً لما مرَّ من طريقي العكس والخلف وغيرهما. وعلى هذا يقال إن الأول بدون الثاني مستلزم للأول والثاني مما ينافي الأول بدون الثاني بالضرورة؛ وحينئذ يلزم من الشكل الرابع أن يكون الأول منافيًا للثاني في الجملة فيقال كلَّما كان الأول بدون الثاني ثابتاً كان الأول ثابتا، وليس البتة إذا كان الثاني ثابتاً كان الأول بدون الثاني ثابتاً فليس كلَّما كان الأول ثابتا كان الثاني ثابتاً لما مر من طريقي العكس والخلف وغيرهما. وبالجملة فالقدح في هذا الملازمات الجزئية قدح في كل شكل من الأشكال الأربعة؛ ومن لم يتعرض للقدح فيها بل يعترف بصحة هذه الجزئيات فعليه أن يبين بأن الموجبة الجزئية منها كما في الشكل الأول والثالث كيف حالها في الأمرين المتنافيين والسالبة الجزئية منها كما في الشكل الثاني والرابع كيف حالها في الأمرين المتلازمين. ولا يمكن أن يكون المنافي للشيء مستلزما لذلك الشيء، والمستلزم للشيء منافيًا له كذلك وإلا لا يكون مستلزما له ومنافيًا إياه دائمًا. وإن قيل كلامنا فيما لا يكون لأحدهما تعلق بالآخر فذلك باطل لما أنه لا يمكن أن يكون أمران إلا وأن يكون لأحدهما تعلق بالآخر. وكيف لا وقد كان كل واحد منهما لازما لشيء واحد وملزوم لشيء واحد كذلك دائما. ولأن ما يكون علة للإنتاج فذلك لا يكون مختصا بما لا يتعلق لأحدهما بالآخر بل يجب أن يكون عاماً يعم جميع الأشياء في جميع الصور فإن ما من شيء إلا وأن يكون حاله مع غيره نحو الحال الذي مرَّ ذكره فيما مر . فأما إذا قيل حالهما في المتلازمين والمتنافيين كحالهما في الغير لكن المنافي للشيء لا يكون مستلزما له بما ذكرنا من العلة إلا وأن لا يكون منافيًا. فإن حمد الشيء إذا كان مع الشيء لا يكون ضدا لذلك الشيء والضد كما علمت عبارة عما لا يمكن أن يكون مع الشيء في محل واحد في زمان واحد فكذلك المستلزم للشيء فإنه لا يكون منافيا له بما ذكرنا من العلة إلا وأن لا يكون مستلزما. فإن ما يكون علة للشيء مثلا إذا كان بدون ذلك الشيء لا يكون علة لذلك الشيء؛ وقد علمت بأن العلة عبارة عما لا يمكن أن يوجد بدون المعلول فذلك لا يكون باطلا يعرف بالتأمل. ولا يبعد أن يقال الشيخ قال في الشكل الثالث مثل ما قد ذكرتم فيه إشكالا على ذلك الشكل وما أجاب عنه: غير أنه لما قال بإنتاج الشكل الثالث فقد قال بصدق تلك الموجبة لكن لا يلزم من صدق تلك الموجبة أن يكون نقيض الشيء مستلزما للشيء في الجملة. ومن قال بصحة هذا الاستلزام فذلك بمعزل عن القواعد العقلية؛ والحق أن هذه الجزئيات صادقة بجميعها فإن صدقها لا يفتقر إلى التقادير الكائنة والأوضاع الواقعة مع أنه لا يمكن أن يكون نقيض الشيء مستلزما للشيء.
It is said that the composite necessarily implies the first and also implies the second; and then from the third figure it follows that the first implies the second in general, so it is said: whenever the composite is fixed, the first is fixed, and whenever the composite is fixed, the second is fixed. So it may be that when the first is fixed, the second is fixed, according to the methods of conversion and reduction by contradiction and others. And on this basis it is said that the first without the second implies the first and the second, which contradicts the first without the second necessarily; and then from the fourth figure it follows that the first contradicts the second in general, so it is said: whenever the first without the second is fixed, the first is fixed; but it is not at all the case that when the second is fixed, the first without the second is fixed, so it is not the case that whenever the first is fixed, the second is fixed, according to the methods of conversion and reduction by contradiction and others. In summary, challenging these partial implications is challenging every figure of the four figures. Whoever does not engage in challenging them but acknowledges the truth of these partial propositions must clarify how the particular affirmative among them, as in the first and third figures, is with respect to the two contradictory things, and how the particular negative among them, as in the second and fourth figures, is with respect to the two necessarily implied things. It is impossible that the contradictory of a thing implies that thing, and likewise the implicator cannot be contradictory to it, otherwise it would not be always implicating it and contradicting it. If it is said that we are speaking about [cases] where one has no connection to the other, that is false because it is impossible that there be two things without one having some connection to the other. How could it be, since each one of them is a necessary consequence of one thing and a necessary condition for one thing, always. And because whatever is a cause for inference is not specific to cases where one has no connection to the other, but must be general, covering all things in all forms. There is nothing whose situation with another is other than the situation mentioned earlier. But if it is said their situation with respect to the two necessarily implied and the two contradictory is like their situation with respect to the other, but the contradictory of a thing does not imply it, due to the cause we mentioned, unless it is not contradictory. For the definition of a thing when it is with the thing is not the opposite of that thing; the opposite, as you know, is what cannot be with the thing in one place at one time. Likewise, the implicator of a thing cannot be contradictory to it, due to the cause we mentioned, unless it is not an implicator. For what is a cause of a thing, for example, if it is without that thing, is not a cause of that thing. You have known that the cause is what cannot exist without the effect. That is not false, as known by reflection. It is not far-fetched that the Shaykh said concerning the third figure something like what you mentioned as an objection to that figure and what he answered. But since he said the third figure produces [a conclusion], he said that the particular affirmative is true; but the truth of that particular affirmative does not entail that the contradictory of a thing implies the thing in general. Whoever affirms this implication is far removed from rational principles. The truth is that all these partial propositions are true, for their truth is not dependent on contingent conditions and actual states, while it is impossible that the contradictory of a thing implies the thing.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed syllogistic analysis of the logical relationships between implications and contradictions in the four figures of conditional syllogisms. The author begins by discussing the composite (المجموع) that implies both the first and second components, leading to implications in the third and fourth figures. He uses the methods of conversion (عكس) and reduction by contradiction (خلف) to show how partial propositions (particular affirmative in first and third figures, particular negative in second and fourth) are true. The central argument is that accepting the truth of these partial propositions does not lead to the absurd conclusion that the contradictory of a thing implies the thing itself. The author refutes a potential objection that the cases involve things with no connection, asserting that all things have some connection. He explains that a cause (علة) for inference is universal. He further clarifies using definitions of opposite (ضد) and cause to show that a contradictory cannot imply its opposite, and an implicator cannot be contradictory. He concludes by noting that a scholar (الشيخ) acknowledged the truth of the particular affirmative in the third figure but denied that this implies the contradictory implying the thing, which is against rational principles.

الضد
العلة

Combining Necessary and Accidental Conditionals
Classification of composite conditional premises by necessity and accident

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a conditional premise as necessary or accidental and identify the three possible combinations in a composite conditional syllogism.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms tolerate both necessary and accidental premises, but necessary ones are more natural.

This passage introduces a key distinction in conditional logic: the difference between necessary and accidental conditionals. Understanding this allows you to evaluate the strength of conditional arguments in Islamic sciences, such as when analogical reasoning in fiqh depends on the necessity of the connection between cause and effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditional premises
The author gives an example: 'Whenever A is B, then C is D' and 'Whenever C is D, then E is Z'. These are the two premises of a composite conditional syllogism.
2
Determine the possible necessity-accident combinations
Each premise can be either necessary (luzumiyya) or accidental (ittifaqiyya). Thus there are three combinations: both necessary, both accidental, or one of each.
The author explicitly lists these three.
3
Relate to the four figures and moods
The author states that the moods (ضروب) of the four figures, which were previously discussed with abstract letters, can be realized in all these types of conditional syllogisms.
This shows the generality of the syllogistic forms.
4
Note the priority of necessary conditionals
The author remarks that necessary conditionals are 'prior in understanding and closer to nature' (أسبق إلى الفهم وأقرب إلى الطبع). Therefore accidental conditionals are considered only as a possibility.
This indicates a preference for necessary connections in logical demonstration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [في التركيبات من اللزوميات والاتفاقيات ] واعلم بأنا إذا قلنا كلَّما كان «أب» ف ج دا ، وكلما كان «ج د» فـ «هز» فإنه يمكن اعتبار كل واحدة من هاتين المقدمتين شرطية لزومية، ويمكن اعتبار كل واحدة منهما شرطية اتفاقية، ويمكن اعتبار إحدى المقدمتين منهما لزومية والأخرى اتفاقية. ولو كان كذلك لكانت الضــروب التي مرَّ ذكرها في الأشكال الأربعة بالحروف المقطعة يمكن أن يكون من القياسات الشرطية اللزومية، ويمكن أن يكون من القياسات الشرطية الاتفاقية غير أنه على سبيل الاحتمال في الاتفاقيات بالنسبة إلى اللزوميات / ؛ إذ اللزوميات أسبق إلى الفهم وأقرب إلى الطبع؛ فيجب أن نتعرض لبيان الاتفاقيات في
Section: On the combinations of necessary and accidental [conditionals]. Know that when we say 'Whenever A is B, then C is D' and 'Whenever C is D, then E is Z', then each of these two premises can be considered as a necessary conditional, and each can be considered as an accidental conditional, and one of the two premises can be considered necessary and the other accidental. If so, then the moods previously mentioned in the four figures with cut-off letters can be [composed] of necessary conditional syllogisms, and can be [composed] of accidental conditional syllogisms, but on the basis of possibility in the accidental relative to the necessary; for the necessary are prior in understanding and closer to nature; so we must engage in explaining the accidental in
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage defines a new topic: the composition of conditional syllogisms from necessary (لزومية) and accidental (اتفاقية) premises. The author illustrates with an example: 'Whenever A is B, then C is D' and 'Whenever C is D, then E is Z'. Each premise can be either necessary (where the implication holds by necessity) or accidental (where the implication holds only contingently). Thus, there are three possibilities: both necessary, both accidental, or one of each. The author notes that the moods (ضروب) of the four figures previously discussed with abstract letters can be realized in all these types of conditional syllogisms, but the accidental type is only possible relative to the necessary because necessary conditionals are more intuitive and natural. The author then indicates an intention to explain the accidental conditionals further, though the text cuts off.

Entailment in the Third Figure: Limits of Particular Conclusions
Refutation of invalid entailment claim in third figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the truth of a particular affirmative in the third figure does not entail a universal implication between a thing and its contradictory.

Core Idea
Third figure conclusions are particular; they do not support universal entailments between opposites.

In this passage, the author addresses a subtle objection about the third figure of the categorical syllogism. The objector seems to claim that if the third figure yields a true particular affirmative, then it also implies a general entailment between the subject and the contradictory of the predicate. The author firmly denies this, grounding his response in the principles of opposition and causation. This lesson unpacks that denial and its logical basis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Principle
Find the opening statement: 'A thing is not necessitating of it by the cause we mentioned unless it is not incompatible.' This establishes that entailment requires compatibility. Note how the author ties entailment to absence of opposition.
2
Define 'Opposite' and 'Cause'
The author defines opposite (ḍidd) as what cannot coexist in the same subject at the same time, and cause (ʿilla) as what cannot exist without its effect. These definitions are used to clarify the relationship between entailment and incompatibility.
3
Locate the Objection about the Third Figure
The passage refers to what 'the Shaykh said regarding the third figure' and mentions an objection. The objector apparently argued that because the third figure produces a true particular affirmative, the contradictory of the predicate is entailed by the subject in a general way.
4
Analyze the Author's Refutation
The author responds: 'it does not follow from the truth of that affirmative that the contradictory of a thing is entailed by the thing in general.' He says such a claim is contrary to rational principles. The truth of the particular does not depend on hypothetical situations, and the impossibility of a thing entailing its contradictory remains.
The key phrase: لَا يَلْزَمُ مِن صِدقِ تِلْكَ المُوجَبَةِ أنْ يَكُونَ نَقِيضُ الشَّيءِ مُسْتَلْزِمًا للشَّيءِ فِي الجُمْلَةِ.
5
Apply to Third Figure Syllogisms
The third figure yields only particular conclusions (e.g., Some A are B). From such a conclusion, one cannot infer a universal entailment like 'If something is A, then its contradictory is necessitated by it.' This limits the power of the third figure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للشيء لا يكون مستلزما له بما ذكرنا من العلة إلا وأن لا يكون منافيًا. فإن حمد الشيء إذا كان مع الشيء لا يكون ضدا لذلك الشيء والضد كما علمت عبارة عما لا يمكن أن يكون مع الشيء في محل واحد في زمان واحد فكذلك المستلزم للشيء فإنه لا يكون منافيا له بما ذكرنا من العلة إلا وأن لا يكون مستلزما. فإن ما يكون علة للشيء مثلا إذا كان بدون ذلك الشيء لا يكون علة لذلك الشيء؛ وقد علمت بأن العلة عبارة عما لا يمكن أن يوجد بدون المعلول فذلك لا يكون باطلا يعرف بالتأمل. ولا يبعد أن يقال الشيخ قال في الشكل الثالث مثل ما قد ذكرتم فيه إشكالا على ذلك الشكل وما أجاب عنه: غير أنه لما قال بإنتاج الشكل الثالث فقد قال بصدق تلك الموجبة لكن لا يلزم من صدق تلك الموجبة أن يكون نقيض الشيء مستلزما للشيء في الجملة. ومن قال بصحة هذا الاستلزام فذلك بمعزل عن القواعد العقلية؛ والحق أن هذه الجزئيات صادقة بجميعها فإن صدقها لا يفتقر إلى التقادير الكائنة والأوضاع الواقعة مع أنه لا يمكن أن يكون نقيض الشيء مستلزما للشيء.
A thing is not necessitating of it by the cause we mentioned unless it is not incompatible. For the judgment of a thing, if it is with the thing, cannot be opposite to that thing. And the opposite, as you know, is an expression for what cannot be together with the thing in one locus at one time. So likewise the necessitator of a thing is not incompatible with it by the cause we mentioned unless it is not a necessitator. For what is a cause of a thing, for example, if it exists without that thing, it is not a cause of that thing; and you have known that the cause is an expression for what cannot exist without the effect. That is not false, as known by reflection. And it is not unlikely that the Shaykh said regarding the third figure what you have mentioned as an objection against that figure and what he answered: except that when he said that the third figure produces, he said that that affirmative is true, but it does not follow from the truth of that affirmative that the contradictory of a thing is entailed by the thing in general. And whoever holds the validity of this entailment is far from rational principles. The truth is that these particulars are all true, for their truth does not depend on existing estimations and actual situations, although it is impossible that the contradictory of a thing is entailed by the thing.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific objection regarding entailment in the third figure of the categorical syllogism. The author begins by establishing a general principle: a thing cannot necessitate another thing unless it is not incompatible with it. He explains that the opposite cannot coexist with a thing in the same subject at the same time. Similarly, what necessitates a thing cannot be incompatible with it. He then applies this to causation: a cause cannot exist without its effect. After these preliminaries, he turns to an objection about the third figure. The Shaykh (likely Avicenna or a commentator) is reported to have said that the third figure produces a valid conclusion. The objection might be that this implies a general entailment between a thing and its contradictory. The author clarifies that the truth of a particular affirmative (one of the premises) does not entail that the contradictory of a thing is generally necessitated by the thing. He dismisses the view that such entailment is valid as contrary to rational principles. The truth of these particulars (the premises) does not depend on any hypothetical situations, but it remains impossible for the contradictory of a thing to be entailed by the thing itself.

Key Terms
مستلزم
ضد
علة
موجبة
نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the truth of a particular affirmative in the third figure not imply?
A) That the major term is true of all instances of the minor.
B) That the contradictory of the thing is entailed by the thing in general.
C) That the premise is false.
D) That the syllogism is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The truth of a particular affirmative in the third figure does not justify a universal entailment between a thing and its contradictory; such an inference violates rational principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider this third figure syllogism: All prophets are human. Some prophets are Meccan. Therefore, some humans are Meccan. Does this conclusion allow you to claim that 'non-human is entailed by Meccan' in general? Explain using the author's reasoning.
?
In a fiqh context: Some intoxicants are forbidden. All intoxicants are drinks. Therefore, some drinks are forbidden. Can you infer from this that 'the contradictory of drink is necessitated by forbidden'? Why or why not?

The Basic Distinction Between Necessary and Accidental Conditionals
Author introduces necessary and accidental conditionals and their combinations in syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a conditional premise is necessary or accidental based on the connection between antecedent and consequent.

Core Idea
A conditional is necessary only if the consequent follows from the antecedent by a real connection; otherwise it is accidental.

In this passage, the author introduces the two types of conditional propositions used in conditional syllogisms: necessary (lāzimiyya) and accidental (ittifāqiyya). He explains that any conditional premise can be of either type, and that this affects the syllogism's epistemic value. We will focus on understanding this core distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of conditionals
Look for terms لزومية and اتفاقية. Understand that لزومية means there is a real connection forcing the consequent from the antecedent.
2
Understand the possibility of combination
The author says each premise can be necessary or accidental, so there are four combinations: both necessary, both accidental, or one of each.
3
Note the priority of necessary conditionals
The author says necessary conditionals are 'prior to understanding and closer to nature.' They reflect real causal relationships and are more intuitive.
4
Recognize the need to explain accidentals
Since necessary conditionals are natural, the author must detail accidentals, which are less reliable and require clarification.

بالصدق والبعض بالكذب ؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن تكون المقدمتان قد تجتمعان بالصدق في كل حال كما يقال كلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الحمار ناهقا، وكلَّما كان الحمار ناهقًا كان الغراب ناعقا. وقد تجتمعان بالكذب كذلك كما يقال كلَّما كان الإنسان ناهقًا كان الحمار ناطقا، وكلَّما كان الحمار ناطقا كان الغراب ناطقا . وقد لا تجتمعان على كل واحد منهما بل عليهما كما يقال كلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الحمار ناهقا، وكلما كان الحمار ناهقا كان الأسد ناعقًا؛ وعلى هذا في السوالب. ثمَّ إنا إذا قلنا كلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الحمار ناهقا فهو صادق والصدق فيه ظاهر . فأما إذا قلنا كلما كان كل إنسان ناطقا كان كل حمار ناهقًا فليس كذلك؛ إذ لا يبعد أن يظن أنه كاذب وإن كان ذلك الظنّ باطلا ؛ فإن قولنا كل حمار ناهق لا يفتقر في الصدق على وجود الحمار حتى تكون الكثرة مانعة. ومنهم من زعم أنه يلزم من قولنا كلما كان كل إنسان ناهقًا أن يكون كل حمار ناطقاً، وبالعكس لكن لا يلزم البتة لا على سبيل اللزوم؛ وهذا ظاهر، ولا على سبيل الاتفاق حتى إذا فرض أن هذا صادقًا يمكن أن يوجد ذلك صادقا معه؛ وذلك من جملة ما لا يمكن صدقه أصلا. ولما عرفت هذا فلك أن تعرف بأن ما يكون معتبراً من التركيبات الشرطية فذلك ما يتركب من الشرطيات الحقيقية وهي اللزومية؛ فإن ما يتركب من الغير وهو الاتفاقية، فذلك من جملة ما يكون خاليا عن الإفادة. ولهذا لا يعتبر في المباحث العلمية بل لا يستعمل فيها لما أن العلم بالنتيجة حاصل قبل تركيب المقدمات وترتيبها من الاتفاقيات الصرفة.
Chapter: On the combinations of necessary and accidental [conditionals]. Know that when we say 'Whenever AB is, then CD is', and 'Whenever CD is, then EF is', it is possible to consider each of these two premises as a necessary conditional, and it is possible to consider each of them as an accidental conditional, and it is possible to consider one of the two premises as necessary and the other as accidental. If that is so, then the moods that were mentioned in the four figures with isolated letters can be from necessary conditional syllogisms, and can be from accidental conditional syllogisms—except that [it is] on the basis of possibility in the accidentals relative to the necessary; for the necessary are prior to understanding and closer to nature. So we must undertake an explanation of the accidentals in this place in a manner that indicates their modes in detail. We say: the terms mentioned in accidentals, like the minor, middle, and major, can each occur as true, and can occur as false, or some as true and some as false. Consequently, the two premises may both be true in every case, as is said: 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying'; and 'Whenever the donkey is braying, the raven is cawing'. And they may both be false likewise, as is said: 'Whenever man is braying, the donkey is rational'; and 'Whenever the donkey is rational, the raven is rational'. And they may not both be [true] in each one but in both [together], as is said: 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying'; and 'Whenever the donkey is braying, the lion is cawing'. And similarly for negatives. Then, when we say 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying', it is true, and the truth of it is apparent. But if we say 'Whenever every man is rational, every donkey is braying', it is not so; for it is not unlikely that one might think it false, although that opinion is invalid. For our statement 'every donkey is braying' does not depend, in its truth, on the existence of a donkey such that multiplicity prevents. And some claim that it follows from our saying 'Whenever every man is braying' that every donkey is rational, and vice versa, but it does not follow at all, neither on the basis of necessity—this is apparent—nor on the basis of accident, such that if this is assumed true, that could be true with it; and that is among what cannot be true at all. And when you have understood this, you may know that what is considered among the conditional combinations is that which is composed of real conditionals, namely necessary conditionals. For what is composed of other [conditionals], namely accidental ones, is among what is devoid of benefit. Therefore it is not considered in scientific investigations, nor is it used therein, because knowledge of the result is obtained before composing the premises and ordering them from pure accidentals.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a chapter explaining the distinction between necessary (lāzimiyya) and accidental (ittifāqiyya) conditionals in the context of conditional syllogisms. The author begins by stating that when we have two conditional premises, each can be either necessary or accidental, leading to combinations. The figures of syllogisms (with their moods) can be constructed from either type, but necessary conditionals are more intuitive. The author then details accidental conditionals: the terms (minor, middle, major) can be true or false in various combinations. He gives examples: 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying' is a true accidental conditional (though the antecedent and consequent are not really connected). He shows cases where both premises are true, both false, or mixed. He then discusses a subtle point: when the antecedent and consequent are universally quantified ('every man is rational' and 'every donkey is braying'), the conditional may be considered false by some, but the author argues it is actually true because the truth does not depend on the existence of the things. He refutes a claim that p→q implies q→p (in a universal sense), showing it does not follow necessarily nor accidentally. The conclusion: only necessary conditionals (where there is a real connection) are beneficial in scientific investigations because accidental conditionals do not produce new knowledge—the result is known before composing the premises.

شرطية اتفاقية
حدود
اتفاقيات صرفة

Accidental conditionals lack a real connection between antecedent and consequent, making them less reliable for scientific reasoning.

The Possible Truth Values of Terms in Accidental Conditionals
Author details the possible truth combinations of premises in accidental conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the three possible truth-value combinations for premises in accidental conditional syllogisms.

Core Idea
In accidental conditionals, the terms can be true, false, or mixed, making the premises true or false without any necessary pattern.

The author now details how accidental conditionals behave. Since there is no necessary connection, the truth of the antecedent and consequent is contingent. He explains that the minor, middle, and major terms can each be true or false independently, leading to various truth-value scenarios for the two premises. We will examine the three cases he illustrates.

Guided Path
1
Recognize that terms can be true or false arbitrarily
The author states that the minor, middle, and major terms can occur as true, false, or mixed (some true, some false). This gives many possibilities for the truth of each premise.
2
First case: both premises are true
Example: 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying' (true) and 'Whenever the donkey is braying, the raven is cawing' (true). Both premises are true.
These are accidental truths because there is no real connection.
3
Second case: both premises are false
Example: 'Whenever man is braying, the donkey is rational' (false) and 'Whenever the donkey is rational, the raven is rational' (false). Both false.
The antecedents and consequents are arbitrarily false.
4
Third case: one premise true, the other false
Example: 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying' (true) and 'Whenever the donkey is braying, the lion is cawing' (false). One true, one false.
The author says they are not both true on each one but on both together, meaning mixed.

Why Accidental Conditionals Are Not Useful in Science
Author concludes that only necessary conditionals are beneficial for science; accidentals are useless.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why accidental conditionals are considered devoid of benefit for scientific reasoning.

Core Idea
Accidental conditionals produce no new knowledge because the conclusion is already known before the premises are composed.

The author concludes his discussion by arguing that only necessary conditionals are useful in scientific investigations. He illustrates with an example involving universal quantifiers and refutes a mistaken claim about conversion. The fundamental problem with accidentals is that they do not provide genuine inference; they merely restate information already known.

Guided Path
1
Understand the universally quantified conditional
The author contrasts 'Whenever man is rational, donkey is braying' with 'Whenever every man is rational, every donkey is braying'. The universal version might be thought false because it requires all donkeys to bray, but the author says it is still true because 'every donkey is braying' does not depend on existence.
2
Refute the mistaken implication
Some claim that from 'Whenever every man is braying, every donkey is rational' one can infer the converse. The author refutes this: it does not follow necessarily nor accidentally; it is impossible.
3
Grasp the main conclusion
Only conditionals composed of necessary (real) conditionals are considered beneficial. Accidentals are devoid of benefit because the result (conclusion) is already known before composing the premises.
4
Apply to scientific investigations
In scientific reasoning (like kalam or fiqh), we use only necessary conditionals to derive new knowledge. Accidentals are dismissed as they do not yield new information.

فليس كذلك؛ إذ لا يبعد أن يظن أنه كاذب وإن كان ذلك الظنّ باطلا ؛ فإن قولنا كل حمار ناهق لا يفتقر في الصدق على وجود الحمار حتى تكون الكثرة مانعة. ومنهم من زعم أنه يلزم من قولنا كلما كان كل إنسان ناهقًا هذا فلك أن تعرف بأن ما يكون معتبراً من التركيبات الشرطية فذلك ما يتركب من الشرطيات الحقيقية وهي اللزومية؛ فإن ما يتركب من الغير وهو الاتفاقية، فذلك من جملة ما يكون خاليا عن الإفادة. ولهذا لا يعتبر في المباحث العلمية بل لا يستعمل فيها لما أن العلم بالنتيجة حاصل قبل تركيب المقدمات وترتيبها من الاتفاقيات الصرفة.
Chapter: On the combinations of necessary and accidental [conditionals]. Know that when we say 'Whenever AB is, then CD is', and 'Whenever CD is, then EF is', it is possible to consider each of these two premises as a necessary conditional, and it is possible to consider each of them as an accidental conditional, and it is possible to consider one of the two premises as necessary and the other as accidental. If that is so, then the moods that were mentioned in the four figures with isolated letters can be from necessary conditional syllogisms, and can be from accidental conditional syllogisms—except that [it is] on the basis of possibility in the accidentals relative to the necessary; for the necessary are prior to understanding and closer to nature. So we must undertake an explanation of the accidentals in this place in a manner that indicates their modes in detail. We say: the terms mentioned in accidentals, like the minor, middle, and major, can each occur as true, and can occur as false, or some as true and some as false. Consequently, the two premises may both be true in every case, as is said: 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying'; and 'Whenever the donkey is braying, the raven is cawing'. And they may both be false likewise, as is said: 'Whenever man is braying, the donkey is rational'; and 'Whenever the donkey is rational, the raven is rational'. And they may not both be [true] in each one but in both [together], as is said: 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying'; and 'Whenever the donkey is braying, the lion is cawing'. And similarly for negatives. Then, when we say 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying', it is true, and the truth of it is apparent. But if we say 'Whenever every man is rational, every donkey is braying', it is not so; for it is not unlikely that one might think it false, although that opinion is invalid. For our statement 'every donkey is braying' does not depend, in its truth, on the existence of a donkey such that multiplicity prevents. And some claim that it follows from our saying 'Whenever every man is braying' that every donkey is rational, and vice versa, but it does not follow at all, neither on the basis of necessity—this is apparent—nor on the basis of accident, such that if this is assumed true, that could be true with it; and that is among what cannot be true at all. And when you have understood this, you may know that what is considered among the conditional combinations is that which is composed of real conditionals, namely necessary conditionals. For what is composed of other [conditionals], namely accidental ones, is among what is devoid of benefit. Therefore it is not considered in scientific investigations, nor is it used therein, because knowledge of the result is obtained before composing the premises and ordering them from pure accidentals.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a chapter explaining the distinction between necessary (lāzimiyya) and accidental (ittifāqiyya) conditionals in the context of conditional syllogisms. The author begins by stating that when we have two conditional premises, each can be either necessary or accidental, leading to combinations. The figures of syllogisms (with their moods) can be constructed from either type, but necessary conditionals are more intuitive. The author then details accidental conditionals: the terms (minor, middle, major) can be true or false in various combinations. He gives examples: 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying' is a true accidental conditional (though the antecedent and consequent are not really connected). He shows cases where both premises are true, both false, or mixed. He then discusses a subtle point: when the antecedent and consequent are universally quantified ('every man is rational' and 'every donkey is braying'), the conditional may be considered false by some, but the author argues it is actually true because the truth does not depend on the existence of the things. He refutes a claim that p→q implies q→p (in a universal sense), showing it does not follow necessarily nor accidentally. The conclusion: only necessary conditionals (where there is a real connection) are beneficial in scientific investigations because accidental conditionals do not produce new knowledge—the result is known before composing the premises.

Mixed Composites of Necessary and Agreement Conditionals
Author explains how composites of a necessary and agreement conditional yield a conclusion of the same type as the agreement premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the type of conclusion (general or specific agreement conditional) that follows from a mixed composite syllogism with a necessary minor and an agreement major.

Core Idea
Mixing necessary and agreement conditionals yields a conclusion that matches the agreement premise's type.

In this passage, the author explains a sophisticated rule: when you combine a necessary conditional (luzumiyya) as the minor premise with an agreement conditional (ittifaqiyya) as the major, the conclusion is an agreement conditional of the same type as the major—general if the major is general, specific if the major is specific. This shows how conditional syllogisms can mix premise types under specific conditions, expanding the range of valid arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise types
Check which premise is necessary (luzumiyya) and which is agreement (ittifaqiyya). Note the minor and major premises in the syllogism.
The necessary conditional expresses a real connection; the agreement conditional expresses a coincidental or asserted connection.
2
Determine the agreement premise’s type
Is the major a general agreement (ittifaqiyya ‘amma) or a specific agreement (ittifaqiyya khassa)? General agreement negates a universal conditional; specific agreement negates a particular conditional.
Look for terms like 'ليس البتة' (absolutely not) for general, or other qualifiers for specific.
3
Apply the rule
The conclusion will be an agreement conditional of the same type as the major. The reasoning: the major indicates that the major term is false or contrary to the middle term, which is a necessary concomitant of the minor; thus the truth of the agreement conditional follows.
The necessary minor ensures the middle term is tied to the minor term, so the major's evaluation of the middle transfers to the minor.
4
Work through the examples
Verify the rule with the two examples given: (1) minor necessary 'إذا كان الإنسان ناطقا كان حيوانا', major general agreement 'ليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان الخلاء موجودا' → conclusion general agreement 'ليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الخلاء موجودا'; (2) minor necessary 'كلما كان الإنسان فرسا كان صهالا', major specific agreement 'ليس البتة إذا كان صهالا كان ناطقا' → conclusion specific agreement 'ليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان فرسا كان ناطقا'.
Notice how the conclusion mirrors the major's quantifier and negation pattern.

فإن من التركيبات الواقعة على هذا التقدير ما لا يكون بخلافه وإنها قد تكون من الاتفاقيات الصرفة، وقد لا تكون بل تكون من المختلطات كما إذا كانت الصغرى لزومية والكبرى اتفاقية عامة فإنه ينتج اتفاقية عامة. وذلك لأن الكبرى في مثل هذه الصورة تدل على كون الأكبر كاذبا أو منافيًا للأوسط الذي هو لازم للأصغر؛ وأيهما لزم يلزم صدق الاتفاقية العامة كما في قولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقا كان حيوانا، وليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان الخلاء موجودًا فليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الخلاء موجودا: صدق العامة لا ينافي صدق الخاصة يمكن أن تكون الخاصة صادقة أيضًا. وأما إذا كانت الكبرى اتفاقية خاصة فالنتيجة اتفاقية خاصة فإن الأوسط لا يكون صادقًا إلا وأن يكون الأكبر كاذبا فإنه إذا لم يكن كاذبًا كانت السالبة الاتفاقية كاذبة، ولا يكون كاذبا أيضًا إلا وأن يلزم من كذبه كذب الأصغر لما أنه من لوازم الأصغر فيلزم صدق النتيجة إذن كما في قولنا كلما كان الإنسان فرسا كان صهَّالا، وليس البتة إذا كان صهَّالا كان ناطقا فليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان فرسا كان ناطقاً. وإن كان على العكس
For among the composites occurring upon this estimation, some are not otherwise, and they may be pure agreement conditionals, or they may not be but rather be mixed, such as when the minor premise is a necessary conditional and the major is a general agreement conditional, then it produces a general agreement conditional. That is because the major in such a form indicates that the major term is false or contrary to the middle term which is a necessary concomitant of the minor; and whichever is necessitated, the truth of the general agreement conditional is necessitated, as in our statement: 'Whenever man is rational, he is an animal; and absolutely not: if man is an animal, then void exists; so absolutely not: if man is rational, then void exists.' The truth of the general does not contradict the truth of the specific; it is possible that the specific is also true. And if the major is a specific agreement conditional, then the conclusion is a specific agreement conditional. For the middle term is not true unless the major term is false; because if it is not false, the negative agreement conditional would be false. And it is also not false unless the falsity of the minor is necessitated because it is a necessary concomitant of the minor; thus the truth of the conclusion is necessitated, as in our statement: 'Whenever man is a horse, he neighs; and absolutely not: if he neighs, he is rational; so absolutely not: if man is a horse, he is rational.' And if it is the opposite...
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage explains how to combine two conditional premises in a syllogism where one is a necessary conditional (luzumiyya) and the other is an agreement conditional (ittifaqiyya). The author states that if the minor is luzumiyya and the major is a general agreement conditional (ittifaqiyya 'amma), the conclusion is a general agreement conditional. The reason: the major indicates that the major term is false or contrary to the middle term, which is a necessary concomitant of the minor. This forces the conclusion to be a general agreement conditional. An example is given: major is 'Whenever man is an animal, void exists' (false universal negative agreement), and minor is 'Whenever man is rational, he is an animal' (necessary), yielding 'Whenever man is rational, void exists' (false universal negative agreement). Note that truth of the general does not conflict with a possible specific truth. Conversely, if the major is a specific agreement conditional (ittifaqiyya khassa), the conclusion is specific. Here, the middle term is true only if the major term is false; otherwise the negative agreement would be false. The falsity of the major forces falsity of the minor due to the necessary connection. Example: minor 'Whenever man is a horse, he neighs' (necessary), major 'Absolutely not: if he neighs, he is rational' (specific agreement negative), conclusion 'Absolutely not: if man is a horse, he is rational'. The last phrase 'وإن كان على العكس' suggests a discussion of the reverse case, but the text ends abruptly.

General and Specific Conditionals in Mixed Syllogisms
Author distinguishes general and specific conditionals and their production from mixed premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish general accidental from specific accidental conditionals and determine the type of conclusion in a mixed syllogism with an implicative minor and an accidental major.

Core Idea
General and specific conditionals differ in scope; mixing them with implicatives yields predictable conclusion types.

In logical investigations, the focus is usually on universal laws, but the author shows that considering particular conditional forms yields additional benefits. This lesson explores two types of accidental (ittifāqiyya) conditionals—general and specific—and how they behave when combined with an implicative (luzūmiyya) premise in a syllogism. Understanding these distinctions helps evaluate arguments that mix different conditional types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two accidental conditionals
Read the definitions: a general accidental (ittifāqiyya 'āmma) is true for all cases under the condition but does not preclude specific false instances; a specific accidental (ittifāqiyya khāṣṣa) is true only for a particular instance, requiring the consequent to be false whenever the antecedent is true.
The terms 'āmma and khāṣṣa indicate scope: general vs. particular.
2
Analyze the mixed syllogism rules
When the minor is implicative and the major is a general accidental, the conclusion is a general accidental. The major's falsity or incompatibility with the middle term, which is a necessary concomitant of the minor, ensures the truth of the general accidental. Conversely, when the major is a specific accidental, the conclusion is a specific accidental, because the middle term is true only when the major is false, and that falsehood entails the minor's falsehood.
The examples provided (human/animal/void for general; horse/whinnying/rational for specific) illustrate these rules.
3
Test the examples
Take the first example: 'Whenever a human is rational, he is an animal; but it is absolutely not the case that whenever a human is an animal, void exists; so it is absolutely not the case that whenever a human is rational, void exists.' Here the minor is implicative, the major is a general accidental, and the conclusion is a general accidental. The specific accidental could also be true. In the second example: 'Whenever a human is a horse, he is whinnying; but it is absolutely not the case that whenever he is whinnying, he is rational; so it is absolutely not the case that whenever a human is a horse, he is rational.' Here the major is specific accidental, so the conclusion is specific accidental.
Note that the specific accidental conclusion is stronger: it asserts that whenever the antecedent is true, the consequent is false, not just generally.
4
Apply to Islamic sciences
Consider how these conditionals appear in kalam arguments about the contingency of the world. For instance, 'If the world is created, it has a creator; but it is not the case that if it has a creator, it is eternal; therefore, if the world is created, it is not eternal.' Is the conditional 'if it has a creator, it is eternal' general or specific? This affects the validity of the conclusion.
The distinction helps in evaluating theological proofs that use conditional syllogisms.

بحسب ما يكون مخصوصا بالبعض منها دون البعض. ثم إنه من جملة ما لا يليق بالمباحث المنطقية وإن كان فيه من الفوائد؛ إذ المباحث المنطقية هي الكليات العامة التي هي القوانين لسائر الجزئيات لكنهم لما انتهضوا لهذا التكلّف وتعرضوا لكل واحدة من الخاصة والعامة في المطالب والمقدمات الموصلة إليها لما أن اعتبار العامة من حيث هي العامة غير اعتبار الخاصة؛ و اعتبار الخاصة من حيث هي الخاصة غير اعتبار العامة كذلك. والعامة قد تكون مفيدة لما لا تكون الخاصة مفيدة لذلك، وبالعكس فنتعرض لكلواحدة منهما إذن تحصيلا للزوائد من الفوائد. فإن من التركيبات الواقعة على هذا التقدير ما لا يكون بخلافه وإنها قد تكون من الاتفاقيات الصرفة، وقد لا تكون بل تكون من المختلطات كما إذا كانت الصغرى لزومية والكبرى اتفاقية عامة فإنه ينتج اتفاقية عامة. وذلك لأن الكبرى في مثل هذه الصورة تدل على كون الأكبر كاذبا أو منافيًا للأوسط الذي هو لازم للأصغر؛ وأيهما لزم يلزم صدق الاتفاقية العامة كما في قولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقا كان حيوانا، وليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان الخلاء موجودًا فليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الخلاء موجودا: صدق العامة لا ينافي صدق الخاصة يمكن أن تكون الخاصة صادقة أيضًا. وأما إذا كانت الكبرى اتفاقية خاصة فالنتيجة اتفاقية خاصة فإن الأوسط لا يكون صادقًا إلا وأن يكون الأكبر كاذبا فإنه إذا لم يكن كاذبًا كانت السالبة الاتفاقية كاذبة، ولا يكون كاذبا أيضًا إلا وأن يلزم من كذبه كذب الأصغر لما أنه من لوازم الأصغر فيلزم صدق النتيجة إذن كما في قولنا كلما كان الإنسان فرسا كان صهالا ، وليس البتة إذا كان صهالا كان ناطقا فليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان فرسا كان ناطقاً.
According to what is particular to some of them without others. Then it is among what is not fitting for logical investigations, though it contains benefits. For logical investigations are the general universals that are the laws for all particulars. But when they undertook this trouble and discussed each of the specific and general in the requests and premises leading to them, because consideration of the general qua general is different from consideration of the specific, and consideration of the specific qua specific is different from consideration of the general likewise. The general may be useful for what the specific is not useful for, and vice versa, so we discuss each of them in order to attain additional benefits. For among the combinations occurring on this estimation, some are not otherwise: they may be purely accidental (ittifāqiyya ṣirfa), or they may be mixed, as when the minor is implicative (luzūmiyya) and the major is a general accidental, then it produces a general accidental. That is because the major in such a form indicates that the major term is false or contradictory to the middle term which is a necessary concomitant of the minor; whichever follows, the truth of the general accidental follows, as in our saying: Whenever a human is rational, he is an animal; but it is absolutely not the case that whenever a human is an animal, void exists; so it is absolutely not the case that whenever a human is rational, void exists. The truth of the general does not contradict the truth of the specific; the specific may also be true. And if the major is a specific accidental, then the conclusion is a specific accidental, because the middle term is true only if the major term is false; for if it were not false, the negative accidental would be false, and it (the major) is false only if its falsehood necessitates the falsehood of the minor, since it is among the necessary concomitants of the minor; thus the truth of the conclusion follows, as in our saying: Whenever a human is a horse, he is whinnying; but it is absolutely not the case that whenever he is whinnying, he is rational; so it is absolutely not the case that whenever a human is a horse, he is rational.

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates two types of conditional propositions: the general accidental (ittifāqiyya 'āmma) and the specific accidental (ittifāqiyya khāṣṣa). The author first explains that although logical investigations normally focus on universal laws, it is sometimes beneficial to consider particular conditional forms because they yield additional insights. He then discusses mixed syllogisms where one premise is implicative (luzūmiyya) and the other is accidental. When the minor is implicative and the major is a general accidental, the conclusion is a general accidental. The reasoning: the major indicates that the major term is false or incompatible with the middle term, which is a necessary concomitant of the minor; whichever of these holds, the general accidental conclusion follows. An example is given using 'human' and 'animal' and 'void'. The author notes that the truth of the general accidental does not preclude the truth of a specific accidental. Conversely, when the major is a specific accidental, the conclusion is a specific accidental. This is because the middle term is true only when the major term is false, and that falsehood necessitates the falsehood of the minor (since the middle is a necessary concomitant of the minor). An example with 'horse' and 'whinnying' and 'rational' illustrates this.

Ibn Sina on Mixed Conditionals with Negative Major
Author presents and refutes a critique of Ibn Sina's view on a specific mixed conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Sina's argument for the productiveness of a mixed conditional with a negative major and evaluate the critique against it.

Core Idea
The truth of a conditional syllogism's conclusion depends on the truth of its premises, not on metaphysical necessity.

In this passage, the author enters a technical debate about a specific type of mixed conditional syllogism: where the minor is accidental and the major is a negative implicative. Ibn Sina holds that such a combination yields a valid conclusion, while others object. The author both defends Ibn Sina and refutes the critics, sharpening our understanding of conditional validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Terms of the Debate
The minor premise is an accidental conditional (ittifāqiyya), meaning the antecedent and consequent have no necessary connection. The major premise is a negative implicative (luzūmiyya saliba), asserting a necessary non-implication. The conclusion sought is also a negative implicative.
The example: 'Whenever a human is an animal, black is a color' (accidental) and 'It is not the case that whenever black is a color, a human is sentient' (negative implicative).
2
Reconstruct Ibn Sina's Argument
Ibn Sina argues that the universal negative major is true only if the major term entails nothing of the middle term at all. Given the truth of the premises, the conclusion (a negative implicative) must follow. The author confirms this, adding that the truth of the conclusion is tied to the truth of the premises, not to intrinsic reality.
The author sides with Ibn Sina: 'The truth is this: the truth of the conclusion is by virtue of the truth of the two premises.'
3
Analyze the Critique Against Ibn Sina
Critics argue that if the major term is a necessary concomitant of the minor, then it would also be a necessary concomitant of the middle term. This would imply a general principle that non-entailment of one thing from another entails non-entailment from everything, which is false (e.g., Zayd's eating and 'Amr's drinking are independent).
The critics claim this renders Ibn Sina's demonstration invalid.
4
Evaluate the Author's Refutation
The author refutes the critique by distinguishing between absolute non-entailment and context-specific non-entailment. Non-entailment of the major from the middle does not imply non-entailment from everything; it only means it does not follow from that specific term. Thus the critique fails.
The author's refutation is based on possibility and impossibility: if the major were entailed by the middle, an impossibility would follow on a possible assumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان على العكس كما إذا كانت الصغرى اتفاقية والكبرى السالبة لزومية فإنه لا ينتج عند قوم من المتقدمين لاحتمال أن تكون الحدود الثلاثة صادقة في نفس الأمر، وأن يكون الأكبر من لوازم الأصغر كما في قولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان حيوانا كان السواد لونا، وليس البتة إذا كان السواد لونا كان الإنسان حساسا؛ ولا يمكن صدق النتيجة في مثل هذه الصورة. وعند قوم ينتج سالبة لزومية؛ والشيخ من جملتهم؛ إذ الكبرى السالبة الكلية صادقة إلا وأن لا يلزم الأكبر شيئًا من الأوسط أصلا فإنه إذا لزم بوجه ما كانت الكبرى كاذبة؛ وذلك محال (۱)، والحق هذا فإن صدق النتيجة باعتبار صدق المقدمتين لاعتبار أنها صادقة في نفس الأمر. ولو كانت الكبرى صادقة في هذه الصورة كانت النتيجة صادقة كذلك وإلا لا تكون صادقة. ومنهم من طعن الشيخ في بيان إنتاج هذه القرينة بأنه إذا لزم الأكبر للأصغر لزم للأوسط أيضًا أنه لا يكون كذلك إلا وأن يلزم من عدم لزوم شيء لشيء عدم لزومه لكل شيء؛ وذلك محال فإن من الأشياء ما لا يلزم بعضه بعضًا كأكل زيد وشرب عمرو ونحو ذلك. غير أنه باطل فإنه لا يلزم منه عدم لزومه لكل شيء مطلقا بل يلزم منه عدم لزومه لكل ما يمكن أن يكون مع ذلك الشيء وهو الأوسط فيما نحن فيه فإنه إذا لزمه لزم المحال على تقدير ممكن؛ وذلك محال.
And if it is the opposite, as when the minor is accidental and the major is a negative implicative, then according to some of the earlier scholars it does not produce a conclusion, because it is possible that the three terms are all true in themselves, and that the major term is among the necessary concomitants of the minor, as in our saying: Whenever a human is an animal, black is a color; but it is absolutely not the case that whenever black is a color, a human is sentient; and the conclusion cannot be true in such a form. According to some, it produces a negative implicative conclusion; and the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) is among them, because the universal negative major is true only if the major term entails nothing of the middle term at all; for if it entails in any way, the major would be false, and that is impossible. The truth is this: the truth of the conclusion is by virtue of the truth of the two premises, not by virtue of their being true in themselves. And if the major were true in this form, the conclusion would also be true; otherwise it would not be true. Some criticized the Shaykh's demonstration of the productiveness of this conjunction, arguing that if the major term is a necessary concomitant of the minor, then it would also be a necessary concomitant of the middle term, which is not the case unless it follows from the fact that if something is not entailed by something, it is not entailed by everything; but that is impossible, because some things are not entailed by each other, like Zayd's eating and 'Amr's drinking, etc. But this is false because it does not entail that it is not entailed by everything absolutely; rather it entails that it is not entailed by everything that can coexist with that thing, which is the middle term in our case; for if it were entailed by it, an impossibility would follow on a possible assumption, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage critiques and defends Ibn Sina's position on a particular mixed conditional syllogism. The case is when the minor premise is an accidental conditional (ittifāqiyya) and the major premise is a negative implicative (saliba luzūmiyya). Some earlier logicians argued that no valid conclusion follows because all three terms could be true simultaneously, and the conclusion (a negative implicative) would be false. They give an example: 'Whenever a human is an animal, black is a color; but it is absolutely not the case that whenever black is a color, a human is sentient'; here the premises are true but the conclusion 'It is absolutely not the case that whenever a human is an animal, a human is sentient' does not follow. Others, including Ibn Sina, hold that a valid negative implicative conclusion does follow. The author explains Ibn Sina's reasoning: the universal negative major is true only if the major term (the predicate of the conclusion) entails nothing of the middle term. Since the premises are taken as true, the conclusion must follow. The author adds that the truth of the conclusion depends on the actual truth of the premises, not on their intrinsic truth. Then a critique of Ibn Sina is presented: the critic argues that if the major term is a necessary concomitant of the minor, it would also be a necessary concomitant of the middle term; this would imply a general principle linking non-entailment to absolute non-entailment, which is false (as shown by independent events). The author refutes this critique, stating that the non-entailment is not absolute but limited to the specific context; non-entailment of one thing from another does not imply non-entailment from everything. The refutation uses the notion of possibility and impossibility.

Why Pure Accidentals Are Not Syllogistic
Ibn Sina states that pure accidental conditionals are not syllogistic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Sina's argument against considering pure accidental conditionals as syllogistic and identify the epistemic flaw that makes the middle term redundant.

Core Idea
Pure accidentals fail because the middle term adds no knowledge.

Ibn Sina distinguishes between conditionals that carry necessary connection and those that are purely accidental. In this passage, he dismisses the latter as useless for syllogistic reasoning. Understanding this critique sharpens your ability to evaluate the strength of conditional premises in Islamic theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Define pure accidental conditional
Review the term: a conditional where the antecedent and consequent have no necessary connection, only accidental coincidence.
Example: 'If it is Friday, then the imam wears a turban.'
2
Locate Ibn Sina's claim
Find the statement in the passage: 'the Shaykh stated that it is preferable that what is composed of them is not syllogistic.'
He refers to al-Shifa.
3
Reconstruct the reasoning
Ibn Sina argues: to know the conclusion, you must already know the major term exists in itself; once known, its existence with the minor is also known. The middle term becomes irrelevant.
So the 'syllogism' does not produce new knowledge.
4
Connect to syllogistic theory
A valid syllogism must have an informative middle term that mediates the inference. Here, the middle term fails to mediate.
This violates the purpose of syllogism.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider a conditional in kalam: 'If the world is temporally originated, then it has a creator.' If the connection is accidental (e.g., because we observe some originated things without creators), the inference collapses.
Highlight the epistemic test: can the conclusion be known without the conditional?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القول في الاتفاقيات الصرفة فالشيخ صرّح في الشفاء أن الأولى عدم قياسية ما ينعقد منها ومع اللزوميات في الموجبات الكبرى: أما من الاتفاقيات فلأنه لا يفيد أصلا ضرورة يوقف العلم به على وجود الأكبر في نفسه؛ ومتى علم وجود الأكبر في نفسه علم وجوده مع وجود الأصغر. وإن لم يلتفت إلى الأوسط فلا فائدة في إدخاله شرطا للإنتاج؛ وبهذا يظهر أن الكبرى إذا
And concerning pure accidentals (ittifāqiyyāt ṣirfa), the Shaykh stated in al-Shifā' that it is preferable that what is composed of them is not syllogistic, and also [when combined] with implicatives in affirmative majors. As for [syllogisms composed] of accidentals, it is because they are not useful at all, since knowledge of them depends on knowing the existence of the major term in itself; and once the existence of the major term in itself is known, its existence with the minor is known. If one does not pay attention to the middle term, there is no benefit in including it as a condition for production. And by this it appears that the major if...
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's view on pure accidental conditionals — that is, conditionals where both premises are accidental (no necessary connection). Ibn Sina declares in al-Shifā' that it is preferable not to consider such combinations as syllogistic, and similarly when they are combined with implicatives in affirmative major premises. The reason is that pure accidentals provide no epistemic benefit: to know the conclusion, one must already know that the major term exists independently. Once you know that the major term exists in itself, you already know it exists along with the minor. The middle term becomes irrelevant; it adds nothing to the inference. Therefore, inserting it as a condition for production is pointless. The passage ends mid-sentence ('and by this it appears that the major if...'), but the argument is clear: Ibn Sina argues against treating pure accidental conditionals as valid syllogistic forms.

Key Takeaway
Pure accidental conditionals are not syllogistic because they are epistemically uninformative: the middle term does no real work.

The Dispute Over the Validity of a Conditional Syllogism with an Accidental Minor Premise
Author presents a dispute over a conditional syllogism with accidental minor and necessary major negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the arguments for and against the validity of a conditional syllogism where the minor is accidental and the major is a necessary universal negative, by analyzing the truth conditions of the premises.

Core Idea
The truth of a syllogism depends on the truth of its premises, not on the modal nature of the conditionals.

This lesson delves into a classical dispute in Islamic logic regarding a specific mood of conditional syllogism. Some early logicians argued that when the minor premise is accidental (ittifaqiyya) and the major is a necessary universal negative (luzumiyya), no conclusion follows. However, Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh) maintained that it does produce a necessary negative conclusion. We will examine the reasoning on both sides.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood
The minor premise is an accidental conditional (ittifaqiyya) and the major is a necessary universal negative (luzumiyya). Note that 'accidental' means the antecedent and consequent are not essentially connected; 'necessary' means the consequent follows essentially.
2
Understand the early objection
Some earlier logicians argued that no conclusion follows because the three terms might be true in themselves and the major could be a consequent of the minor, making the intended conclusion false. The example given: 'Whenever man is an animal, blackness is a color' (true) and 'It is not the case that whenever blackness is a color, man is sentient' (true), but the conclusion 'Whenever man is an animal, man is not sentient' would be false.
3
Examine Ibn Sina's defense
Ibn Sina argues that the universal negative major premise is true only if the major term does not entail any instance of the middle; if it did, the major would be false. Therefore, the syllogism does produce a necessary negative conclusion.
4
Follow the author's resolution
The author affirms Ibn Sina's view, clarifying that the truth of the conclusion depends on the truth of the premises, not on the intrinsic truth of the terms. If the major is true in this form, the conclusion is true; otherwise not.
5
Summarize the positions
Two positions: (1) Early logicians: invalid because possible truth of terms leads to false conclusion. (2) Ibn Sina and author: valid because the major's truth condition ensures the conclusion follows necessarily.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا كانت الصغرى اتفاقية والكبرى السالبة لزومية فإنه لا ينتج عند قوم من المتقدمين لاحتمال أن تكون الحدود الثلاثة صادقة في نفس الأمر، وأن يكون الأكبر من لوازم الأصغر كما في قولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان حيوانا كان السواد لونا، وليس البتة إذا كان السواد لونا كان الإنسان حساسا؛ ولا يمكن صدق النتيجة في مثل هذه الصورة. وعند قوم ينتج سالبة لزومية؛ والشيخ من جملتهم؛ إذ الكبرى السالبة الكلية صادقة إلا وأن لا يلزم الأكبر شيئًا من الأوسط أصلا فإنه إذا لزم بوجه ما كانت الكبرى كاذبة؛ وذلك محال (۱)، والحق هذا فإن صدق النتيجة باعتبار صدق المقدمتين لاعتبار أنها صادقة في نفس الأمر. ولو كانت الكبرى صادقة في هذه الصورة كانت النتيجة صادقة كذلك وإلا لا تكون صادقة. ومنهم من طعن الشيخ في بيان إنتاج هذه القرينة بأنه إذا لزم الأكبر للأصغر لزم للأوسط أيضًا أنه لا يكون كذلك إلا وأن يلزم من عدم لزوم شيء لشيء عدم لزومه لكل شيء؛ وذلك محال فإن من الأشياء ما لا يلزم بعضه بعضًا كأكل زيد وشرب عمرو ونحو ذلك. غير أنه باطل فإنه لا يلزم منه عدم لزومه لكل شيء مطلقا بل يلزم منه عدم لزومه لكل ما يمكن أن يكون مع ذلك الشيء وهو الأوسط فيما نحن فيه فإنه إذا لزمه لزم المحال على تقدير ممكن؛ وذلك محال.
If the minor premise is accidental and the major premise is a necessary negative, then it does not produce a conclusion according to some of the earlier [logicians], because it is possible that the three terms are true in themselves, and that the major is a consequent of the minor, as in our saying: 'Whenever man is an animal, blackness is a color,' and 'It is not at all the case that whenever blackness is a color, man is sentient'; and the truth of the conclusion is not possible in such a form. And according to some [logicians], it produces a necessary negative conclusion; and the Shaykh [Ibn Sina] is among them, for the universal negative major premise is true only if the major does not entail any [instance] of the middle at all; because if it entails it in some way, the major would be false; and that is impossible. And the truth is this, for the truth of the conclusion is by virtue of the truth of the two premises, not by virtue of them being true in themselves. And if the major were true in this form, the conclusion would be true likewise; otherwise it would not be true. And among them is one who criticized the Shaykh in his explanation of the production of this mood, saying that if the major is a consequent of the minor, it would also be a consequent of the middle; this is not so unless the non-entailment of one thing from another implies its non-entailment from everything; and that is impossible, for among things there are those that do not entail each other, like Zayd eating and 'Amr drinking and the like. However, it is false, for from that it does not imply its non-entailment from everything absolutely, but rather it implies its non-entailment from everything that can be with that thing, which is the middle in our case; because if it entails it, then an impossibility would follow on a possible assumption; and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage discusses a specific conditional syllogism where the minor premise is an accidental (ittifaqiyya) proposition and the major premise is a necessary (luzumiyya) universal negative. The author first states that according to some earlier logicians, such a syllogism does not produce a conclusion because it is possible that all three terms are true in themselves and the major is a consequent of the minor, making the conclusion impossible. He gives an example: 'Whenever man is an animal, blackness is a color' (true) and 'It is not the case that whenever blackness is a color, man is sentient' (true), but the conclusion 'Whenever man is an animal, man is not sentient' would be false. Then the author notes that according to others, including Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh), it does produce a necessary negative conclusion, because the universal negative major premise is true only if the major term has no essential connection to the middle term; if it did, the major would be false. The author affirms this view, explaining that the truth of the conclusion depends on the truth of the premises, not on their being true in themselves. He then addresses an objection: someone argued that if the major is a consequent of the minor, it would also be a consequent of the middle, but this is not necessarily true, as in unrelated events like Zayd eating and 'Amr drinking. The author refutes this objection by clarifying that the non-entailment is not absolute but relative to the specific middle term; if entailment were assumed, it would lead to an absurdity on a possible assumption. Thus, the syllogism is valid.

Refuting an Objection to Ibn Sina's Position on Conditional Syllogisms
Author refutes an objection against Ibn Sina's syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the objection that if the major term follows from the minor it must follow from the middle term, and explain the author's refutation using the concept of relative non-entailment.

Core Idea
Non-entailment is relative to the specific middle term, not absolute.

After affirming Ibn Sina's position, the author addresses a criticism: someone argued that if the major is a consequent of the minor, it would also be a consequent of the middle, which seems to lead to an absurdity. The author refutes this by clarifying that non-entailment is not absolute but relative to the specific relation in the syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
A critic argues that if the major term is a consequent of the minor, then it must also be a consequent of the middle term; otherwise, the non-entailment of one thing from another would imply its non-entailment from everything, which is false (as in unrelated events like Zayd eating and 'Amr drinking).
2
Understand the critic's example
The critic gives an example: Zayd eating does not entail 'Amr drinking, but that does not mean Zayd's eating is unrelated to everything. The critic intends to show that non-entailment is not absolute.
3
Analyze the author's refutation
The author counters that the non-entailment in question is not absolute; it only means that given the specific middle term, the major does not follow. If entailment were assumed, a contradiction would arise on a possible assumption.
4
Conclude the outcome
The objection fails because it misinterprets non-entailment as absolute. The author's refutation shows that the syllogism remains valid.
