If the minor premise is accidental and the major premise is a necessary negative, then it does not produce a conclusion according to some of the earlier [logicians], because it is possible that the three terms are true in themselves, and that the major is a consequent of the minor, as in our saying: 'Whenever man is an animal, blackness is a color,' and 'It is not at all the case that whenever blackness is a color, man is sentient'; and the truth of the conclusion is not possible in such a form. Commentary
This passage discusses a specific conditional syllogism where the minor premise is an accidental (ittifaqiyya) proposition and the major premise is a necessary (luzumiyya) universal negative. Why Pure Accidental Conditionals Yield No Benefit
Author explains why pure accidental propositions are useless in syllogistic inference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a pure accidental conditional proposition and explain why it yields no new knowledge when used as a premise in a syllogism.

Core Idea
A syllogism with a pure accidental conditional is valid but epistemically trivial: the conclusion is already known from the major alone.

Not all valid syllogisms are beneficial. Ibn Sina distinguishes between necessary and accidental conditionals. The former provide genuine inferences; the latter, while logically valid, add no new knowledge because the conclusion is already implied by one premise independently of the middle term. This lesson unpacks that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish accidental from necessary
A pure accidental proposition is one where the link between antecedent and consequent is coincidental, not essential. A necessary proposition has an essential link. In the passage, 'whenever man is animal, the horse is sentient' is accidental because horse sentience does not depend on man being animal.
2
Identify the premises and conclusion in the example
The example: minor premise (with middle term 'animal'): 'Whenever man is rational, he is animal'; major premise: 'Whenever man is animal, the horse is sentient'; conclusion: 'Whenever man is rational, the horse is sentient'.
3
See why the conclusion is already known
The existence of the major term 'horse is sentient' is known independently of the middle term 'animal'. Knowing that the horse is sentient directly gives the conclusion when combined with the minor premise (if man is rational, then he is animal, then horse is sentient). But the major premise already directly links horse sentience to any animal, so the deduction is trivial.
4
Understand 'la yufid' (no benefit)
Ibn Sina says such a composition 'does not benefit' because it produces no new knowledge. He clarifies that this is not a claim of invalidity: the syllogism is valid, just epistemically sterile. The same applies if the major is necessary but the minor accidental.
The author warns against misinterpreting 'no benefit' as invalidity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القول في الاتفاقيات الصرفة فالشيخ صرّح في الشفاء أن الأولى عدم قياسية ما ينعقد منها ومع اللزوميات في الموجبات الكبرى: أما من الاتفاقيات فلأنه لا يفيد أصلا ضرورة يوقف العلم به على وجود الأكبر في نفسه؛ ومتى علم وجود الأكبر في نفسه علم وجوده مع وجود الأصغر. وإن لم يلتفت إلى الأوسط فلا فائدة في إدخاله شرطا للإنتاج؛ وبهذا يظهر أن الكبرى إذا كانت اتفاقية دون الصغرى فإنه لا يفيد أيضا كما إذا قلت كلما كان الإنسان ناطقا كان حيوانًا، وكلَّما كان الإنسان حيوانا كان الفرس حساسا فإن النتيجة وهي قولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الفرس حساسًا حاصلة بدون هذا التركيب. وكذلك إذا كانت الكبرى لزومية. ولا يظن أن قوله « لا يفيد» يدل على أنه لا ينتج مثل هذا التركيب بل ينتج في الغير من الصور لأنه لا خلل في التركيب.
As for the discussion on pure accidental [propositions], the Shaykh stated in al-Shifa' that the better view is the non-syllogistic nature of what is composed of them and with necessary [propositions] in affirmative major premises: as for [those composed] of accidentals, because it is of no benefit at all, since knowledge of it depends on the existence of the major in itself; and whenever the existence of the major in itself is known, its existence with the minor is known. And if one does not pay attention to the middle, there is no benefit in introducing it as a condition for production. And by this it appears that if the major is accidental while the minor is not, it is also of no benefit, as when you say: 'Whenever man is rational, he is an animal,' and 'Whenever man is an animal, the horse is sentient'; then the conclusion, namely 'Whenever man is rational, the horse is sentient,' is obtained without this composition. And likewise if the major is necessary. And one should not think that his saying 'it is of no benefit' indicates that such a composition does not produce [a conclusion]; rather, it produces [a conclusion] in other forms, because there is no defect in the composition.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage addresses the use of pure accidental propositions (al-ittifaqiyyat al-sirafa) in conditional syllogisms. The author refers to Ibn Sina's statement in al-Shifa' that the preferred view is that compositions involving only pure accidentals, or accidentals with necessary propositions in affirmative major premises, are not syllogistic. The reason is that they provide no new knowledge: knowing that the major term exists in itself already implies its existence with the minor, regardless of the middle. For example, if the minor is 'Whenever man is rational, he is an animal' (accidental? actually necessary? but the example uses it as accidental because man being rational and animal is essential? but the author treats it as accidental here? Actually the example: minor: 'Whenever man is rational, he is an animal' — this is a necessary implication, but the author calls it accidental? No, the context: the major 'the horse is sentient' is accidental to the middle 'animal'. The author says the conclusion 'Whenever man is rational, the horse is sentient' is already known without the syllogism because the existence of the major (horse is sentient) is known independently. Thus, introducing the middle term does not add any epistemic benefit. Similarly, if the major is necessary but the minor accidental, it is also useless. However, the author clarifies that Ibn Sina's statement 'it is of no benefit' does not mean such a composition is invalid; it can still produce a conclusion in other forms, but the inference is trivial.

Enumeration of Conditional Syllogism Combinations
Author calculates the total number of compositional possibilities for accidental and necessary premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the total number of possible conditional syllogisms by applying the three-stage multiplication described in the passage.

Core Idea
The total number of conditional syllogisms is 256, derived from three multiplicative steps.

You have learned about conditional propositions and their figures. Now we turn to the enumeration of all possible forms of conditional syllogisms. This passage from the text shows how to calculate the total number of compositional possibilities by accounting for accidental and necessary premises with quantified propositions. Follow the arithmetic to see how the number 256 emerges.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four quantified propositions
The four quantified propositions are: universal affirmative (A), universal negative (E), particular affirmative (I), and particular negative (O). Each premise in a syllogism can be any of these.
2
Calculate base combinations from quantified propositions
For one mood (a pair of premises), there are 4 choices for the first premise and 4 for the second, giving 4 × 4 = 16 combinations.
This is the starting number before considering accidental or necessary status.
3
Multiply for pure accidental premises
When both premises are accidental (rather than necessary), the text says 'it is four times that amount', so multiply 16 by 4 to get 64.
Pure accidental premises can be either universal or particular, doubling the possibilities for each premise? Actually the multiplication is by 4, indicating additional degrees of freedom.
4
Multiply for mixed accidental and necessary premises
When the syllogism mixes accidental and necessary premises, the number is again four times the previous amount: 64 × 4 = 256.
This yields the final total given in the passage.
5
Interpret the total
The final number 256 represents all possible combinations of accidental and necessary premises in conditional syllogisms, considering the four quantified propositions and the well-known conditions and figures.
The passage asserts that this matches the sum of all necessary and accidental combinations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول كما أن اللزوميات على أربعة أشكال فكذلك الاتفاقيات على الشرائط المشهورة والضروب المذكورة. غير أنه متعدد في الاتفاقيات أضعاف التعدد في اللزوميات؛ إذ كل ضرب منها بالنسبة إلى أن يركب من المحصورات الأربع هو الأربعة في الأربعة، وبالنسبة إلى أن يركب من الاتفاقيات الصرفة خاصة وعامة هو الأربعة في ذلك المبلغ وبالنسبة إلى أن يركَّب (۱) منها ومن اللزوميات هو الأربعة في هذا المبلغ؛ وذلك مائتان وستة وخمسون.
If you have understood this, we say: just as necessary [propositions] are in four figures, so too accidental [propositions] are under the well-known conditions and the mentioned moods. However, [the number] is multiplied in accidentals many times over the multiplicity in necessary [propositions]; for each mood of them with respect to being composed from the four quantified [propositions] is four times four, and with respect to being composed from pure accidentals, both particular and universal, it is four times that amount, and with respect to being composed from them and from necessary [propositions], it is four times this amount; that is two hundred and fifty-six.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage provides an enumeration of all possible combinations of conditional propositions in syllogisms. The author begins by stating that, like necessary propositions, accidental propositions also follow the four figures under the well-known conditions and moods. However, the number of possible combinations for accidentals is far greater than for necessary propositions. The calculation is done in three steps: First, for each mood, considering composition from the four quantified propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative), the number is 4 * 4 = 16 (since each of the two premises can be any of the four). Second, when composing from pure accidentals (both particular and universal), it is again 4 times that amount? Actually the text says 'the four in that amount' which likely means 4 * 16 = 64? But then says 'with respect to being composed from them and from necessary [propositions] it is four times this amount' — that would be 4 * 64 = 256. The total is given as 256. The calculation accounts for the fact that accidental premises can be either pure accidental or mixed with necessary premises, and each premise can be quantified in four ways. Thus, the total number of possible conditional syllogisms (including all figures and moods) is 256.

First Figure First Mood with Accidental Minor: Why the Syllogism is Unbeneficial
Author analyzes the first mood of the first figure where the minor is accidental and the major varies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a first-figure first-mood conditional syllogism with an accidental minor is unbeneficial and explain why.

Core Idea
An accidental minor premise in the first mood strips the syllogism of epistemic value.

The first mood of the first figure is normally powerful, but when the minor premise is accidental (i.e., not a necessary relationship), even a necessary major cannot rescue the syllogism from triviality. In this lesson, we will walk through the author's analysis of two cases—accidental major and necessary major—to see why neither yields new knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure and mood
Recognize that the passage deals with the first figure (middle term subject of major predicate of minor) and the first mood (two universal affirmative premises, here conditional).
The first figure's first mood is Barbara for categoricals; here it is applied to conditionals.
2
Parse the minor premise type
Notice that the minor premise is 'accidental' (ittiāqiyya), meaning the antecedent and consequent are not necessarily connected. In the example, 'whenever man is rational, the horse is neighing' is a mere coincidence, not a causal link.
Accidental conditionals do not provide knowledge of the consequent from the antecedent.
3
Analyze the accidental major case
With accidental major ('whenever the horse neighs, the donkey brays'), the conclusion ('whenever man is rational, the donkey brays') can be known directly from the existence of the major term (donkey braying) without needing the middle. Thus, the syllogism is redundant.
The author says 'knowledge of it depends on existence of major in itself'.
4
Analyze the necessary major case
With a necessary major ('whenever the donkey brays, it is an animal'), the truth of the major premise itself gives knowledge of the existence of the major term once the middle is known. So the conclusion is already implicit in the major premise.
The text cuts off but the reasoning is that the major premise's necessity ensures the consequent follows from the middle.
5
Conclude why both are unbeneficial
In both subcases, the syllogism fails to produce new knowledge because the conclusion can be obtained without the middle term, either from direct observation (accidental major) or from the major premise alone (necessary major).
This is not a formal invalidity but an epistemological triviality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الشكل الأول فالضرب الأول منه إذا كانت صغراه اتفاقية فالكبرى إما أن تكون اتفاقية كما في قولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الفرس صهالا، وكلَّما كان الفرس صهَّالا كان الحمار ناهقا؛ فإنه لا يفيد لما مر من قبل أن العلم به يتوقف على وجود الأكبر في نفسه وهي علم وجود الأكبر في نفسه علم وجوده مع وجود الأصغر. وإما أن تكون لزومية وإنه من جملة ما قد مر من قبل أنه لا يفيد كما في قولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الحمار ناهقا، وكلَّما كان الحمار ناهقا كان حيوانا؛ فإنه لا يفيد أيضًا لما أن العلم بوجود الأكبر في نفسه حاصل من صدق الكبرى ضرورة حصول العلم بالأوسط: واستلزامه الأكبر
As for the first figure, the first mood of it: if its minor is accidental, then the major is either accidental, as in our saying: 'Whenever man is rational, the horse is neighing,' and 'Whenever the horse is neighing, the donkey is braying'; then it is of no benefit, as previously mentioned, because knowledge of it depends on the existence of the major in itself, and knowledge of the existence of the major in itself is knowledge of its existence with the minor. Or it is necessary, and it is among what has passed before that it is of no benefit, as in our saying: 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying,' and 'Whenever the donkey is braying, it is an animal'; then it is also of no benefit, because knowledge of the existence of the major in itself is obtained from the truth of the major by necessity of obtaining knowledge of the middle: and its entailment of the major
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage begins the analysis of the first figure, first mood, specifically when the minor premise is accidental. The author considers two possibilities for the major premise: accidental or necessary. If the major is also accidental, as in the example 'Whenever man is rational, the horse is neighing' (minor) and 'Whenever the horse is neighing, the donkey is braying' (major), then the syllogism is of no benefit because, as explained earlier, the conclusion (man rational implies donkey braying) can be known directly from the existence of the major term (donkey braying) without needing the middle term. If the major is necessary, as in the example 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying' (minor) and 'Whenever the donkey is braying, it is an animal' (major), then similarly it is of no benefit because the truth of the major premise itself gives knowledge of the existence of the major term (animal) once the middle (donkey braying) is known. The text cuts off mid-sentence, but the reasoning is clear: both cases fail to produce new knowledge, not because of formal invalidity, but because the conclusion is already implicit in the major premise.

First Figure of Conditional Syllogisms: Accidental and Necessary Premises
Author examines the four moods of the first figure of conditional syllogisms, analyzing when they yield no knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a first-figure conditional syllogism with accidental premises yields new knowledge by applying the author's criteria.

Core Idea
Accidental conditionals often fail to produce new knowledge because the conclusion is already implicit in the premises.

In this passage, the author delves into the first figure of conditional syllogisms, distinguishing between accidental (ittifaqiyya) and necessary (luzumiyya) conditional premises. He systematically examines each of the four moods, showing that many formally valid combinations do not yield new information. The key insight is that if the major premise is true and the middle term is known, the conclusion is already known, rendering the syllogism epistemically redundant.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish accidental from necessary conditionals
Identify whether a conditional proposition asserts a necessary connection (luzumiyya) or mere coincidence (ittifaqiyya). For example, 'Whenever man is rational, he is animal' is necessary; 'Whenever man is rational, the horse neighs' is accidental.
This distinction is central to the author's analysis of epistemic benefit.
2
Identify the figure and mood
In the first figure, the middle term is the antecedent of the minor and the consequent of the major. The four moods correspond to universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. Note the structure of each mood in the examples.
The author treats the moods systematically.
3
Check if the conclusion is already known
For a given syllogism, ask: does the truth of the major premise and knowledge of the middle term already give us the conclusion? The author argues that in many cases the conclusion is already known, so the syllogism does not produce new knowledge.
This is the core epistemological test.
4
Evaluate the disputed case
Some scholars claim that if the consequent is hidden, the syllogism can reveal it. Assess the author's rebuttal: 'that would not be hidden for one who knows the minor and major.' Determine whether the syllogism can ever be beneficial.
This shows the limits of formal logical inference.

يكون إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان السواد لونًا، وليس البتة إذا كان السواد لونا كان الإنسان حساسا؛ وهذا من جملة ما قد مرَّ الكلام في الثاني أنه لا ينتج عند قوم.
If it were accidental without the minor, then it also does not benefit, as when you say: 'Whenever man is rational, he is animal, and whenever man is animal, the horse is sensitive' – then the result, which is our statement 'Whenever man is rational, the horse is sensitive', is obtained without this composition. Likewise if the major is necessary. And one should not think that his statement 'it does not benefit' indicates that such a composition does not produce a conclusion; rather it produces in other forms because there is no defect in the composition. If you understand this, we say: Just as necessary conditionals are of four figures, so too accidental conditionals are according to the well-known conditions and the mentioned moods. However, it is more numerous in accidental conditionals by multiples of the multiplicity in necessary conditionals; for each mood thereof, with respect to being composed of the four quantified [premises], is four in four, and with respect to being composed of purely accidental conditionals, both particular and universal, is four in that amount, and with respect to being composed of them and necessary conditionals, is four in this amount; that is two hundred and fifty-six. As for the first figure: its first mood: if its minor is accidental, then the major is either accidental, as in our saying 'Whenever man is rational, the horse is neighing, and whenever the horse is neighing, the donkey is braying' – then it does not benefit for what has preceded, because knowledge of it depends on the existence of the major in itself, and knowledge of the existence of the major in itself is knowledge of its existence with the existence of the minor. Or it is necessary, and it is among what has already preceded that it does not benefit, as in our saying 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying, and whenever the donkey is braying, it is animal' – then it also does not benefit because knowledge of the existence of the major in itself is obtained from the truth of the major necessarily by obtaining knowledge of the middle term: and its entailment of the major with the minor. And some claim that it benefits because the existence of the entailed necessitates the existence of the concomitant with it; and it can be beneficial if the concomitant is hidden in existence, then by means of this syllogism it would appear. But this is weak, for that would not be hidden in existence for one who knows the minor and the major. Likewise if the minor is necessary, then it also does not benefit; and discussion of it has preceded. And in its second mood: if its minor is accidental, then its major is either accidental, and the result is then accidental, as in our saying 'Whenever man is rational, the horse is neighing, and not at all whenever the horse is neighing, the void exists' – so 'not at all whenever man is rational, the void exists'; for the major is false in such a figure necessarily because of the truth of the middle term, otherwise the major would be false. And since the major is false, the accidental negative is true. Or it is necessary and the result is then a necessary negative; and this is what was mentioned before that it does not produce according to some. And the opposite of that is among what has been discussed likewise. And in the third mood: if its minor is accidental, then the major is either accidental, as in our saying 'It may be that whenever man is rational, the horse is neighing, and whenever the horse is neighing, the donkey is braying' – and it is among what does not benefit also for what has passed in the first mood. Or it is necessary, and it does not benefit likewise; and discussion of it has preceded. And in the fourth mood: if its minor is accidental, then its major is either accidental, as in our saying 'It may be that whenever man is rational, the horse is neighing, and not at all whenever the horse is neighing, the void exists' – so 'it may not be that whenever man is rational, the void exists' for what has passed in the second [mood]. Or it is necessary, as in our saying 'It may be that whenever man is animal, blackness is a colour, and not at all whenever blackness is a colour, man is sentient' – and this is among what has been discussed in the second that it does not produce according to some.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of conditional syllogisms, specifically the first figure when the premises involve accidental (ittifaqiyya) and necessary (luzumiyya) conditionals. The author begins by clarifying a point about combinations where the minor premise is accidental but the major is necessary, stating that such a composition does not produce new knowledge because the conclusion is already known from the truth of the major and the known middle term. He then proceeds to enumerate the many possible combinations of accidental conditionals in the first figure, noting that the number of possible moods is greatly multiplied because each mood can be composed from the four quantified propositions, from purely accidental conditionals (particular or universal), or from a mix of accidental and necessary conditionals, totaling 256 possible moods. The main focus is on the first figure, which has four moods. For each mood, the author considers both cases: when the major is accidental and when it is necessary. The first mood (universal affirmative) with an accidental minor yields no benefit in either case, because knowledge of the major's existence is already presupposed or easily inferred. Some argue that it could benefit if the concomitant is hidden, but the author rejects this. The second mood (universal negative) produces a negative conclusion; if the major is accidental, a false major leads to a true accidental negative. If the major is necessary, the result is a necessary negative, though some dispute its productivity. The third mood (particular affirmative) similarly does not benefit, as in the first. The fourth mood (particular negative) follows the pattern of the second: if the major is accidental, the conclusion is a particular accidental negative; if necessary, a particular necessary negative (disputed). Throughout, the author refers back to earlier discussions, indicating that this is part of a larger commentary.

Conditional Syllogisms with Accidental Minor Premises
Author analyzes second, third, and fourth moods of first figure conditional syllogisms when minor premise is accidental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a first-figure conditional syllogism with an accidental minor premise produces a valid conclusion by analyzing the type of major premise (accidental or necessary) and the resulting conclusion type.

Core Idea
An accidental minor premise alters the productivity of conditional moods; not all combinations yield certain conclusions.

In the previous lesson, you learned about first-figure conditional syllogisms where both premises are necessary. Now, we consider cases where the minor premise is accidental (ittifaqiyya). This changes the logical force of the syllogism. The author systematically examines each mood, showing that the type of major premise determines whether a conclusion follows and what kind. We will focus on the second mood to understand the reasoning, then briefly survey the third and fourth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood and premise types
Locate the second mood example in the passage. Note that the minor premise is accidental (ittifaqiyya) and the major premise is either accidental or necessary. The text gives two subcases.
The second mood is the first analyzed after the first mood dealt with necessary minors.
2
Analyze the accidental major subcase
In the example, both premises are accidental: 'Whenever human is rational, the horse is neighing' (accidental universal) and 'It is not at all that whenever the horse is neighing, void exists' (accidental universal negative). The conclusion is an accidental negative: 'It is not at all that whenever human is rational, void exists'. Understand the reasoning: the major term (void) is false because the middle term (horse neighing) is true, so the negative holds.
The author states the major term is false necessarily given the truth of the middle term.
3
Examine the necessary major subcase
If the major premise is necessary (luzumiyya), the conclusion would be a necessary negative. However, the author notes that some scholars reject this mood as unproductive. This is a point of debate.
This subcase was previously discussed and is controversial.
4
Extend to third and fourth moods
In the third mood, with an accidental minor, whether the major is accidental or necessary, the syllogism does not yield a beneficial conclusion (as in the first mood). In the fourth mood, with accidental major the conclusion is an accidental negative (similar to second mood), and with necessary major it is also disputed.
The passage references earlier discussions for these moods.

And in the second mood of it, if its minor premise is accidental, then its major premise either is accidental, and the conclusion is then accidental, as in our saying: Whenever human is rational, the horse is neighing; and it is not at all that whenever the horse is neighing, void exists; so it is not at all that whenever human is rational, void exists. The Syllogism

Second Figure Conditional Syllogisms: Accidental and Mixed Premises with Reductio Proof
Second figure conditional syllogisms with reductio proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the validity of conditional syllogisms in the second figure and apply reductio ad absurdum to prove them.

Core Idea
In the second figure, mixed conditional premises require reductio proof to avoid obvious conclusions.

This lesson explores the second figure of conditional syllogisms, a topic where the logician examines different compositions of premises. The author first considers the case where both premises are purely accidental (no necessary connection), finding that the conclusion is already known without the syllogism. Then, he turns to mixed premises—one accidental, one necessary—which initially seem unproductive according to some early scholars. However, by using a reductio ad absurdum (khalf), he demonstrates that such syllogisms are indeed valid. We will walk through these distinctions and the logical reasoning involved, step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second figure structure
In the second figure of conditional syllogisms, the middle term is the predicate in both premises. For example, 'If A then M' and 'If B then not M' (or 'not (if B then M)'). This figure contrasts with the first where the middle term is subject in one premise and predicate in the other.
2
Recognize the case of pure accidental premises
When both premises are accidental (ittifaqiyya), meaning there is no necessary connection between their terms, the conclusion is already obvious. The author gives the example: 'Whenever human is rational, the donkey is braying; it is not at all that whenever the horse is rational, the donkey is braying; therefore it is not at all that whenever human is rational, the horse is rational.' The conclusion is known without the syllogism, so the composition is futile.
Accidental premises do not express a real implication.
3
Examine the mixed case: one accidental, one necessary
Mixed compositions involve one accidental affirmative premise and one necessary negative premise. Some early scholars argued these also fail to produce a conclusion. The author presents their counterexample: 'Whenever human is animal, blackness is a color; it is not at all that whenever human is sentient, blackness is a color; therefore it is not at all that whenever human is animal, he is sentient.' They claim this conclusion is false, so the syllogism is invalid.
The conclusion appears false, but the author will defend the syllogism's formal validity.
4
Understand the reductio proof
The author rebuts by noting that the truth of the conclusion depends on the truth of the premises, not on independent facts. He then provides a reductio: assume the conclusion is false (i.e., its contradictory true), combine with the major premise, and derive a contradiction. This proves the conclusion must be true, validating the syllogism. The reductio is necessary because reduction to the first figure is impossible here.
Reductio ad absurdum (khalf) is a powerful tool when direct proof is unavailable.
5
Compare with the final variation
The passage concludes by stating that if the affirmative premise is necessary rather than accidental, the syllogism produces an accidental conclusion. This shows the subtle interplay between the nature of premises and the figure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في الشكل الثاني فنقول إذا كان التركيب من الاتفاقيات الصرفة فإنه لا يفيد لما أن العلم حاصل بأن السالبة لا تكون صادقة إلا وأن يكون أحدهما؛ وهو إما الأصغر أو الأكبر كاذبًا في نفسه. وإذا كان أحدهما كاذبًا في نفسه كان من الممتنع أن يكون صادقًا مع الآخر؛ وقد كانت السالبة من اللوازم في هذا الشكل فلا يفيد القياس إذن كما في الضرب الأول وهو قولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الحمار ناهقًا، وليس البتة إذا كان الفرس ناطقا كان الحمار ناهقًا فإن العلم بالنتيجة وهي قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الفرس ناطقا حاصل بدون هذا التركيب. وأما إذا كان التركيب من المختلطات فالموجبة لا تخلو من أن تكون اتفاقية وإنه لا يفيد أيضًا عند قوم من المتقدمين فإنه لا يلزم من مساعدة الأوسط لأحد الطرفين وعدم مساعدته للطرف الآخر بسلب اللزوم البتة حتى يصدق القياس من الموجبة الاتفاقية والسالبة اللزومية في مثل قولنا كلما كان الإنسان حيوانا كان السواد لونًا، وليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان حساسا كان السواد لونًا. ولا يمكن أن يصدق قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان حساسًا. غير أنه في حيز المنع لما مرَّ من قبل أن صدق النتيجة باعتبار صدق المقدمتين لا باعتبار الصدق في نفس الأمر. ثمّ إنه وإن تعذر بيانه بالرد إلى الأول لما أن الكلام في الأول نحو الكلام في هذا فلا يتعذر بیانه بالخلف وغيره فيقال إذا كذب قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان حيوانًا كان حساسًا صدق نقيضه وهو قولنا قد يكون إذا كان الإنسان حيوانًا كان حساسًا فنضمه إلى الكبرى ونقول قد يكون إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان حساسًا، وليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان حساسًا كان السواد لونا فليس كلَّما كان الإنسان حيوانًا كان السواد لونًا؛ وقد كان كلما كان الإنسان حيوانا كان السواد لونًا، هذا خلف أو لا تكون الموجبة اتفاقية بل تكون لزومية وإنه ينتج اتفاقية
As for the second figure, we say: if the composition is from pure accidentals, then it does not benefit because it is known that the negative is not true unless one of them [the terms] is false in itself. And if one of them is false in itself, it is impossible that it be true with the other; and the negative was among the necessary consequences in this figure, so the syllogism does not benefit, therefore, as in the first mood: our saying 'Whenever human is rational, the donkey is braying; and it is not at all that whenever the horse is rational, the donkey is braying' — for the knowledge of the conclusion, which is our saying 'It is not at all that whenever human is rational, the horse is rational,' is attained without this composition. And if the composition is from mixed [premises], the affirmative [premise] cannot but be accidental, and it does not benefit also according to some of the early scholars, because it is not necessary from the assistance of the middle term to one of the extremes and its non-assistance to the other extreme by way of negating the implication at all, such that the syllogism from the accidental affirmative and the necessary negative would be true in an example like our saying 'Whenever human is animal, blackness is a color; and it is not at all that whenever human is sentient, blackness is a color.' And it is not possible for our saying 'It is not at all that whenever human is animal, [he] is sentient' to be true. However, it is in the domain of prohibition due to what passed before, that the truth of the conclusion is by virtue of the truth of the two premises, not by virtue of truth in itself. Then, even if its demonstration cannot be by reduction to the first [figure] — because the discussion about the first is similar to the discussion about this — it is not impossible to demonstrate it by reductio ad absurdum (al-khalf) and other [methods]. So it is said: If our saying 'It is not at all that whenever human is animal, he is sentient' is false, its contradictory is true, which is our saying 'Possibly whenever human is animal, he is sentient.' Then we conjoin it to the major premise and say: 'Possibly whenever human is animal, he is sentient; and it is not at all that whenever human is sentient, blackness is a color; so it is not every time that whenever human is animal, blackness is a color.' And it was the case that every time that whenever human is animal, blackness is a color; this is a contradiction. Or the affirmative [premise] is not accidental but necessary, and it produces an accidental [conclusion].
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage discusses conditional syllogisms in the second figure. The author begins with the case where both premises are accidental (ittifaqiyya). The argument is that such a composition does not produce a conclusion because the negative conclusion is already known without the syllogism. The example given is: 'Whenever human is rational, the donkey is braying; it is not at all that whenever the horse is rational, the donkey is braying; therefore it is not at all that whenever human is rational, the horse is rational.' The conclusion is obvious without the premises. Next, the author considers mixed compositions, where one premise is accidental and the other necessary. According to some early scholars, this does not yield a conclusion either, because the truth of the conclusion cannot be derived from the middle term's relation. However, the author presents a rebuttal (man') by noting that the truth of the conclusion depends on the truth of the premises, not on independent truth. Then the author provides a proof by reductio ad absurdum (khalf). Assuming the conclusion is false leads to a contradiction, thereby demonstrating its truth. The passage ends by stating that if the affirmative premise is not accidental but necessary, then the conclusion is accidental.

Key Terms
اتفاقية صرفة (ittifaqiyya sirfa): A purely accidental proposition, with no necessary connection between its terms.
مختلطات (mukhtalitat): Mixed premises in a syllogism, where one premise is accidental and the other necessary.
خلف (khalf): Reductio ad absurdum, a proof by showing that the denial of the conclusion leads to a contradiction.
شكل ثاني (shakl thani): The second figure of the syllogism, where the middle term is the predicate in both premises.
لزوم (luzum): Logical implication or entailment between antecedent and consequent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The middle term does not connect the extremes
B) The negative conclusion is already known without the syllogism
C) The major premise is always false
D) The syllogism is valid but produces a necessary conclusion

Key Takeaway
Mixed conditional syllogisms in the second figure require a reductio proof to establish their validity, as direct reduction to the first figure is impossible.

First Mood of Second Figure: Coincidental and Mixed Premises
Analysis of first mood, second figure, conditional syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish pure coincidental and mixed premises in the second figure of conditional syllogisms and apply proof by contradiction to validate a conclusion.

Core Idea
Pure coincidentals give no new knowledge; mixed premises require proof by contradiction.

In this advanced lesson, we explore the first mood of the second figure of conditional syllogisms. The author distinguishes between pure coincidental premises, which yield no benefit, and mixed premises, which can produce a valid conclusion through reductio (khalf). You will learn to identify these types and use logical reasoning to verify syllogistic validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure and mood
Recognize that this is the first mood of the second figure of conditional syllogisms. The pattern involves a conditional negative major premise and a conditional affirmative minor premise, with the middle term connecting the extremes.
The second figure in conditional syllogisms has the middle term as predicate in both premises.
2
Distinguish premise types
Understand the difference between pure coincidental premises (ittifaqiyat sirfa) where the conditional is true only by coincidence without necessary connection, and mixed (mukhtalitat) where one premise is coincidental and the other is necessary.
Pure coincidentals do not produce new knowledge because the conclusion is already known.
3
Analyze the mixed case
Note that some earlier scholars denied mixed premises yield a conclusion. The author refutes this by using proof by contradiction (khalf). Follow his example: assume the conclusion false, combine with premises, and derive a contradiction.
Khalf is a powerful method for establishing syllogistic validity.
4
Apply the khalf method
Take the author's example: minor 'Whenever man is an animal, blackness is a color' (coincidental), major 'It is not at all the case that if man is sensory, blackness is a color' (necessary). Assume the conclusion 'It is not at all the case that if man is an animal, he is sensory' is false, then its contradictory is true. Combine with the major to get a contradiction with the minor.
The contradiction shows the original conclusion must be true.
5
Note exceptions
Understand when a specific negative conclusion follows and when a general coincidental conclusion does not. The author discusses cases where the negative is the major premise and notes the possibility of falsity of the middle term.
This shows the subtlety of conditional syllogistic logic.

وأما في الشكل الثاني فنقول إذا كان التركيب من الاتفاقيات الصرفة فإنه لا يفيد لما أن العلم حاصل بأن السالبة لا تكون صادقة إلا وأن يكون أحدهما؛ وهو إما الأصغر أو الأكبر كاذبًا في نفسه. وإذا كان أحدهما قولنا كلما كان الإنسان حيوانا كان السواد لونا، وليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان حساسا كان السواد لونا. ولا يمكن أن يصدق قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان حساسا. غير أنه في حيز المنع لما مر من قبل أن صدق النتيجة باعتبار صدق المقدمتين لا باعتبار الصدق في نفس الأمر. ثم إنه وإن تعذر بيانه بالرد إلى الأول لما أن الكلام في الأول نحو الكلام في هذا فلا يتعذر بيانه بالخلف وغيره فيقال إذا كذب قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان حساسا صدق نقيضه وهو قولنا قد يكون إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان حساسا فنضمه إلى الكبرى ونقول قد يكون إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان حساسا، وليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان حساسا كان السواد لونا فليس كلما كان الإنسان حيوانا كان السواد لونا؛ وقد كان كلما كان الإنسان حيوانا كان السواد لونا، هذا خلف أو لا تكون الموجبة اتفاقية بل تكون لزومية وإنه ينتج اتفاقية خاصة ضرورة كذب أحد الطرفين وإلا لكانت السالبة الخاصة وهي التي يتوقف صدق الموجبة فيها على صدق الطرفين كاذبة كما في قولنا كلما كان الإنسان فرسا كان حيوانا، وليس البتة إذا كان الخلاء موجودا كان الإنسان حيوانا فليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان فرسا كان الخلاء موجودا لما مر. وعند ذلك القوم إذا كانت السالبة هي الكبرى خاصة كانت أو عامة فإنه لا ينتج اتفاقية عامة البتة لاحتمال كذب الأوسط اللازم للأصغر وصدق الأكبر اللازم للأصغر كما في قولنا كلما كان الإنسان فرسا كان صهالا، وليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان صهالا فإنه لا يصدق قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان فرسا كان حيوانا، غير أنه في حيز المنع؛ إذ الكلام فيه نحو الكلام فيما إذا كانت الموجبة اتفاقية.
As for the second figure, we say: if the composition is of pure coincidentals, it does not benefit because it is known that the negative is not true unless one of the two—either the minor or the major—is false in itself. And if one of them is false in itself, it is impossible for it to be true with the other; and the negative is one of the necessary consequences in this figure, so the syllogism does not benefit, as in the first mood, namely our saying: 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying', and 'It is not at all the case that if the horse is rational, the donkey is braying', for knowledge of the conclusion—namely our saying: 'It is not at all the case that if man is rational, the horse is rational'—is obtained without this composition. And if the composition is of mixed [premises], the affirmative is either coincidental and it also does not benefit according to some of the earlier scholars, because it does not follow from the middle term's agreement with one extreme and its non-agreement with the other extreme by negating the necessary connection at all that the syllogism becomes true from the coincidental affirmative and the necessary negative, as in our saying: 'Whenever man is an animal, blackness is a color', and 'It is not at all the case that if man is sensory, blackness is a color'. And it is not possible for our saying: 'It is not at all the case that if man is an animal, he is sensory' to be true. However, it is subject to denial for the reason mentioned earlier, namely that the truth of the conclusion is based on the truth of the two premises, not on truth in itself. Then, if its demonstration by reduction to the first [figure] is difficult because the discussion in the first is similar to the discussion in this, its demonstration by contradiction and other methods is not difficult. So it is said: if our saying 'It is not at all the case that if man is an animal, he is sensory' is false, then its contradictory is true, namely our saying 'Sometimes if man is an animal, he is sensory'. Then we combine it with the major premise and say: 'Sometimes if man is an animal, he is sensory', and 'It is not at all the case that if man is sensory, blackness is a color'. Therefore, it is not the case that whenever man is an animal, blackness is a color; yet it was the case that whenever man is an animal, blackness is a color. This is a contradiction. Or the affirmative is not coincidental but is necessary, and it produces a specific coincidental necessarily because one of the two extremes is false; otherwise the specific negative—whose truth depends on the truth of both extremes—would be false, as in our saying: 'Whenever man is a horse, he is an animal', and 'It is not at all the case that if void exists, man is an animal'. Therefore, it is not at all the case that if man is a horse, void exists, for the reason mentioned. And according to those people, if the negative is the major premise, whether specific or general, it does not produce a general coincidental at all due to the possibility of the falsity of the middle term that is necessary for the minor and the truth of the major that is necessary for the minor, as in our saying: 'Whenever man is a horse, he neighs', and 'It is not at all the case that if man is an animal, he neighs'. For our saying 'It is not at all the case that if man is a horse, he is an animal' is not true. However, it is subject to denial, because the discussion about it is similar to the discussion about when the affirmative is coincidental.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is an advanced analysis of the first mood of the second figure of conditional syllogisms. The author begins by stating that if both premises are purely coincidental (ittifaqiyat sırfa), the syllogism produces no knowledge because the conclusion is already known without the syllogism. He illustrates with an example: 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying' and 'It is not at all the case that if the horse is rational, the donkey is braying' leads to a conclusion already obvious. Then he turns to mixed premises (mukhtalitat), where one premise is affirmative and coincidental, the other negative and necessary. Some earlier scholars held this also is unproductive, claiming that the middle term's agreement with one extreme and non-agreement with the other does not entail the negative conclusion. The author refutes this with reductio (al-khalf). He assumes the conclusion false, derives a contradiction, thus proving the conclusion's necessity. He further distinguishes cases where the negative is specific or general, and shows that even then a general coincidental conclusion may not follow. The key point is that the truth of the conclusion depends on the truth of the premises, not on absolute truth. This analysis is crucial for understanding the conditions under which conditional syllogisms in the second figure yield certain knowledge.

Second Mood of the Second Figure: Productivity Conditions
Author examines the second mood of the second figure, showing when it is productive and when it is not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a second-figure conditional syllogism with mixed premises is productive by checking whether the affirmative premise is coincidental or necessary.

Core Idea
In the second mood's mixed case, productivity hinges on the nature of the affirmative premise.

In conditional syllogisms of the second figure, not all premise combinations yield a valid conclusion. The author distinguishes between coincidental and necessary affirmative premises. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying this syllogistic form in Islamic reasoning, especially when distinguishing between categories like obligatory and forbidden acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure and mood
The passage discusses the second mood of the second figure. In conditional syllogisms, the second mood has a negative major premise and an affirmative minor premise.
The major premise is a negative conditional; the minor is an affirmative conditional.
2
Check the composition of the premises
If both premises are coincidental (i.e., true but without necessary connection), the syllogism is unproductive. If mixed (one coincidental, one necessary), proceed to the next step.
Coincidental premises are true by accident, not by logical necessity.
3
Examine the affirmative premise
Is the affirmative premise coincidental or necessary (luzumiyya)? If it is coincidental, some may think no conclusion follows, but the author argues it is still productive. If it is necessary, the syllogism is clearly productive.
Productivity here means the conclusion necessarily follows.
4
Apply the rule
When the affirmative premise is coincidental, the negation of necessity in the negative premise itself entails the conclusion. When the affirmative is necessary, the syllogism follows standard valid forms.
The author provides examples for both cases.
5
Test with Islamic examples
Practice by constructing your own example: Major: 'It is not the case that if an act is obligatory, it is sinful.' Minor: 'If an act is forbidden, it is sinful.' Conclusion: 'It is not the case that if an act is obligatory, it is forbidden.' Check if the minor is necessary.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الضرب الثاني منه إذا كان التركيب من الاتفاقيات فإنه لا يفيد لما مر . وإن كان من المختلطات فالموجبة إما أن تكون اتفاقية وإنه لا ينتج لاحتمال الصدق في الحدود الثلاثة كما في قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان الفرس صهالاً، وكلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الفرس صهالا؛ ولا مجال لصدق النتيجة البتة. غير أنه في حيز المنع فإنه ينتج لما مر من قبل أن سلب اللزوم عن أحد الطرفين مما يستلزم صدق النتيجة. أو لا تكون بل تكون لزومية وإنه ينتج كما في قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان صهالا، وكلَّما كان الإنسان فرسا كان صهالا فليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان فرسا لما مر من الطرق. (۱) النسخة حد. وما أثبتناه هو الصواب كما هو الظاهر من السياق.
And in the second mood of it: if the composition is of coincidentals, it does not benefit for what has passed. And if it is of mixed [premises], the affirmative is either coincidental and it does not produce due to the possibility of truth in the three limits, as in our saying: 'It is not at all the case that if man is an animal, the horse neighs', and 'Whenever man is rational, the horse neighs'; and there is no room for the truth of the conclusion at all. However, it is subject to denial, for it produces for what has passed, because negation of necessity from one of the two extremes entails the truth of the conclusion. Or it is not [coincidental] but is necessary, and it produces, as in our saying: 'It is not at all the case that if man is an animal, he neighs', and 'Whenever man is a horse, he neighs', so it is not at all the case that if man is an animal, he is a horse, for the methods that have passed. (1) The manuscript has 'حد', but what we have established is correct as is apparent from the context.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage analyzes the second mood of the second figure. The author first states that if both premises are coincidental, the syllogism is useless—this follows from the earlier discussion. Then he considers mixed premises. Two subcases arise: (a) the affirmative premise is coincidental. In this case, some might think no conclusion follows because all three terms could be true simultaneously (e.g., 'man is an animal' and 'horse neighs' are both true, but the conclusion 'not at all if man is an animal then horse neighs' is not forced). However, the author counters that the conclusion does follow: the negation of necessity from one extreme (i.e., the negative premise) actually necessitates the conclusion. (b) the affirmative premise is necessary (luzumiyya). Then the syllogism is productive, as in the example given: from 'It is not at all the case that if man is an animal, he neighs' and 'Whenever man is a horse, he neighs', the conclusion 'It is not at all the case that if man is an animal, he is a horse' is valid. The author refers to methods previously discussed for validation. A textual note indicates a variant reading in the manuscript for 'one of the two extremes'.

Mixed Conditional Syllogisms: Accidental vs. Necessary Premises
Author examines validity conditions for mixed conditional syllogisms in the second and third figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify conditional premises as accidental (ittifaqiyya) or necessary (luzumiyya) and determine the validity of mixed conditional syllogisms in the second figure.

Core Idea
In mixed conditional syllogisms, only necessary premises guarantee a valid conclusion.

This passage delves into the intricacies of mixed conditional syllogisms, a key tool in Islamic logic. The author focuses on the second figure, where the validity hinges on whether the premises are accidental or necessary. Understanding this distinction allows you to evaluate the soundness of arguments in disciplines like Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure
Determine that the syllogism is in the second figure, meaning the middle term appears as the consequent in both premises.
This is analogous to the categorical second figure where the middle term is predicate in both premises.
2
Classify each premise
Label each premise as accidental (ittifaqiyya) or necessary (luzumiyya) based on whether the connection between antecedent and consequent is necessary or coincidental.
The passage gives examples like 'Whenever man is a horse, he neighs' (necessary) vs. 'It may be that if man is an animal, he neighs' (accidental).
3
Apply the validity rule
If both premises are accidental, no conclusion follows. If mixed, check whether the affirmative premise is necessary; if so, a valid conclusion follows; if accidental, the conclusion may be blocked unless a special condition holds.
The author says that when the affirmative premise is necessary, it yields a conclusion 'as per the passed methods'.
4
Test with an example
Use the example from the text: major premise 'Absolutely not if man is an animal then he neighs' (negative, necessary) and minor premise 'Whenever man is a horse, he neighs' (affirmative, necessary). The conclusion 'So absolutely not if man is an animal then he is a horse' follows.
This is a valid mixed conditional syllogism in the second figure.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاصة ضرورة كذب أحد الطرفين وإلا لكانت السالبة الخاصة وهي التي يتوقف صدق الموجبة فيها على صدق الطرفين كاذبة كما في قولنا كلما كان الإنسان فرسا كان حيوانًا، وليس البتة إذا كان الخلاء موجودًا كان الإنسان حيوانا فليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان فرسا كان الخلاء موجودًا لما مر. وعند ذلك القوم إذا كانت السالبة هي الكبرى خاصة كانت أو عامة فإنه لا ينتج اتفاقية عامة البتة لاحتمال كذب الأوسط اللازم للأصغر وصدق الأكبر اللازم للأصغر كما في قولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان فرسا كان صهالا، وليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان حيوانًا كان صهَّالا فإنه لا يصدق قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان فرسًا كان حيوانًا، غير أنه في حيز المنع؛ إذ الكلام فيه نحو الكلام فيما إذا كانت الموجبة اتفاقية. وفي الضرب الثاني منه إذا كان التركيب من الاتفاقيات فإنه لا يفيد لما مر . وإن كان من المختلطات فالموجبة إما أن تكون اتفاقية وإنه لا ينتج لاحتمال الصدق في الحدود الثلاثة كما في قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان الفرس صهالاً، وكلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الفرس صهالا؛ ولا مجال لصدق النتيجة البتة. غير أنه في حيز المنع فإنه ينتج لما مر من قبل أن سلب اللزوم عن أحد (۱) الطرفين مما يستلزم صدق النتيجة. أو لا تكون بل تكون لزومية وإنه ينتج كما في قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان صهالا، وكلَّما كان الإنسان فرسا كان صهالا فليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان فرسا لما مرَّ من الطرق. وفي الضرب الثالث كذلك فإنه إذا كان من الاتفاقيات لا يفيد لما مرَّ في الضرب الأول. وإن كان من المختلطات فالموجبة إما أن تكون اتفاقية كما في قولنا قد يكون إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان السواد لونًا، (۱) النسخة حد. وما أثبتناه هو الصواب كما هو الظاهر من السياق. وليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان حساسًا كان السواد لونا: وفيه من الكلام أنه يفيد أم لا يفيد كما مر. وإما أن تكون لزومية وإنه ينتج اتفاقية خاصة كما في قولنا قد يكون إذا كان الإنسان فرسا كان حيوانًا، وليس البتة إذا كان الخلاء موجودًا كان الإنسان حيوانًا فقد لا يكون إذا كان الإنسان فرسا كان الخلاء موجودًا لما مرَّ في الأول. وفي الضرب الرابع إذا كان من الاتفاقيات لا يفيد أيضًا لما مر في الضرب الثاني. وإن كان من المختلطات فالموجبة إما أن تكون اتفاقية كما في قولنا قد لا يكون إذا كان الإنسان حيواناً كان الفرس صهالا ، وكلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الفرس صهالا ، وفيه من الكلام كما مر من قبل. وإما أن تكون لزومية كما في قولنا قد لا يكون إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان صهالاً، وكلما كان الإنسان فرسا كان صهالا فقد لا يكون إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان فرسا لما مر . [٣] وأما في الشكل الثالث فنقول إذا كان التركيب من الاتفاقيات الصرفة فإنه لا يفيد موجبة كانت الكبرى أو سالبة فإن صدق الموجبة يفتقر إلى صدق الأكبر ؛ وصدق السالبة يفتقر إلى كذبه وإنه إذا كان صادقًا كان صادقًا مع غيره. وكذلك إذا كان كاذبًا فلا حاجة إلى الأوسط. وأما إذا كان التركيب من المختلطات فالكبرى في الضرب الأول مثلا موجبة لا محالة. ولو كانت موجبة فإنه ينتج اتفاقية على وفق الاتفاقية المستعملة لاستلزام صدق الملزوم مع الشيء صدق اللازم معه، أو لأن صدق الكبرى يقتضي صدق الأكبر في نفسه أو عدم كونه منافيًا للأوسط. ويلزم من لزوم أيهما كان صدق الاتفاقية عامة كانت أو خاصة : هذا إذا كانت الكبرى اتفاقية دون الصغرى كما في قولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان حيوانا كان الإنسان ناطقا وكلَّما كان الإنسان حيوانا كان (۱) النسخة- كان، صح هامش.
Specifically, the necessity of the falsehood of one of the two sides, otherwise the particular negative, whose truth depends on the truth of both sides, would be false, as in our statement: Whenever man is a horse, he is an animal, and absolutely not if void exists then man is an animal. So it is absolutely not if man is a horse then void exists, as passed. And according to those people, if the negative is the major, whether particular or universal, then it does not produce a universal accidental (conclusion) at all, due to the possibility of the middle term being false as a necessary consequent of the minor, and the major being true as a necessary consequent of the minor, as in our statement: Whenever man is a horse, he neighs, and absolutely not if man is an animal then he neighs; for our statement 'absolutely not if man is a horse then he is an animal' is not true, except that it is in the domain of prevention; for the discourse about it is like the discourse about when the universal affirmative is accidental. In its second mood, if the composition is from accidental premises, it does not yield, as passed. And if it is from mixed premises, then the affirmative is either accidental, and it does not yield due to the possibility of truth in the three limits, as in our statement: Absolutely not if man is an animal then the horse is neighing, and whenever man is rational then the horse is neighing; and there is no room for the truth of the conclusion at all. Except that it is in the domain of prevention; for it yields, as passed, because negation of entailment from one side necessitates the truth of the conclusion. Or it is not accidental but rather necessary, and it yields, as in our statement: Absolutely not if man is an animal then he neighs, and whenever man is a horse then he neighs; so absolutely not if man is an animal then he is a horse, as per the passed methods. In the third mood similarly: if it is from accidental premises, it does not yield, as passed in the first mood. And if it is from mixed premises, then the affirmative is either accidental, as in our statement: It may be that if man is an animal then blackness is a color, (1) manuscript: 'hadd'. And what we have established is correct as apparent from context. and absolutely not if man is sentient then blackness is a color: and regarding it there is discourse as to whether it yields or not, as passed. Or it is necessary, and it yields a particular accidental conclusion, as in our statement: It may be that if man is a horse then he is an animal, and absolutely not if void exists then man is an animal; so it may be that if man is a horse then void does not exist, as passed in the first. In the fourth mood, if it is from accidental premises, it does not yield also, as passed in the second mood. And if it is from mixed premises, then the affirmative is either accidental, as in our statement: It may be that if man is an animal then the horse is neighing, and whenever man is rational then the horse is neighing, and regarding it there is discourse as passed before. Or it is necessary, as in our statement: It may be that if man is an animal then he neighs, and whenever man is a horse then he neighs, so it may be that if man is an animal then he is not a horse, as passed. [3] As for the third figure, we say: if the composition is purely accidental, then it does not yield, whether the major is affirmative or negative; for the truth of the affirmative depends on the truth of the major, and the truth of the negative depends on its falsehood, and if it is true, it is true with something else, and likewise if it is false, there is no need for the middle. And if the composition is mixed, then the major in the first mood, for example, is necessarily affirmative. And if it is affirmative, it produces an accidental conclusion in accordance with the accidental premise used, because the truth of the necessary concomitant with a thing necessitates the truth of the necessary consequent with it, or because the truth of the major necessitates the truth of the major in itself or its not being incompatible with the middle. And from whichever entailment occurs, the truth of the accidental conclusion follows, whether universal or particular. This is when the major is accidental without the minor, as in our statement: Whenever man is an animal, man is rational, and whenever man is an animal, ... (1) manuscript: 'kan', corrected in margin.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is an advanced discussion of mixed conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i) in the second and third figures. The author, likely in a commentary tradition, examines under what conditions a valid conclusion can be drawn when one premise is accidental (ittifaqiyya) and the other is necessary (luzumiyya). The text begins mid-sentence with a specific point about the particular negative premise in the second figure. The key distinction is between premises that are 'ittifaqiyya' (accidental, i.e., the connection is not necessary but coincidental) and 'luzumiyya' (necessary entailment). For the second figure, the author goes through four moods (adrab), showing that if both premises are accidental, no conclusion follows. When mixed, if the affirmative premise is accidental, a conclusion may be blocked unless a certain condition (negation of entailment from one side) holds, which the author places 'fi hayyiz al-man' (in the domain of prevention). When the affirmative premise is necessary, a valid conclusion does follow. The same pattern is repeated for the third and fourth moods. Then the text transitions to the third figure, where the same principle applies: purely accidental premises yield nothing; mixed premises can yield an accidental conclusion if the major is necessary, and the author provides an example of the first mood. The passage cuts off mid-sentence while giving an example. Throughout, the author uses technical terms and provides illustrative examples with conditional statements about human, horse, animal, void, neighing, etc. Students should focus on understanding the distinction between accidental and necessary premises, and how that determines validity in each mood. The commentary here follows the author's order, explaining each mood's reasoning.

Fourth Mood of Second Figure: Accidental vs Implicative Premises
Author analyzes the fourth mood of the second figure of conditional syllogism with accidental and mixed premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish accidental from implicative conditional premises in the fourth mood of the second figure and determine whether a conclusion follows.

Core Idea
In the second figure, accidental premises yield no conclusion; implicative ones yield only accidental conclusions.

In this passage, the author examines the fourth mood of the second figure of conditional syllogisms. He distinguishes between accidental (ittifāqiyya) and implicative (luzūmiyya) premises, showing how the nature of the conditional link determines whether a conclusion is valid. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic legal reasoning where conditionals often appear.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major and minor premises
Locate the two conditional premises. In the examples, the major is often introduced with 'قد لا يكون' (negative) and the minor with 'كلما' (universal affirmative).
2
Classify each premise as accidental or implicative
Determine if the antecedent and consequent have a necessary connection (luzūmiyya) or merely accidental (ittifāqiyya). For example, 'If man is horse then he is animal' is implicative; 'If void exists then man is animal' is accidental.
3
Apply the fourth mood rule
If both premises are accidental, no conclusion follows. If mixed, examine the affirmative premise: if it is accidental, the conclusion is accidental; if implicative, the conclusion is also accidental but specific (ittifāqiyya khāṣṣa).
The text states: 'In the fourth mood, if it is from accidentals, it does not benefit... If mixed, the affirmative is either accidental or implicative.'
4
Check the example
In the implicative case: 'It may not be that if man is animal then he neighs' (negative major, accidental), 'Whenever man is horse then he neighs' (affirmative minor, implicative). Conclusion: 'It may not be that if man is animal then he is horse' – an accidental conclusion.
The conclusion is accidental because the minor premise's implicative nature does not overcome the accidental major.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان حساسًا كان السواد لونا: وفيه من الكلام أنه يفيد أم لا يفيد كما مر. وإما أن تكون لزومية وإنه ينتج اتفاقية خاصة كما في قولنا قد يكون إذا كان الإنسان فرسا كان حيوانًا، وليس البتة إذا كان الخلاء موجودًا كان الإنسان حيوانًا فقد لا يكون إذا كان الإنسان فرسا كان الخلاء موجودًا لما مر في الأول. وفي الضرب الرابع إذا كان من الاتفاقيات لا يفيد أيضًا لما مر في الضرب الثاني. وإن كان من المختلطات فالموجبة إما أن تكون اتفاقية كما في قولنا قد لا يكون إذا كان الإنسان حيواناً كان الفرس صهالا ، وكلما كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الفرس صهالا ، وفيه من الكلام كما مر من قبل. وإما أن تكون لزومية كما في قولنا قد لا يكون إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان صهالاً، وكلما كان الإنسان فرسا كان صهالا فقد لا يكون إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان فرسا لما مر.
And not at all if man is sentient then blackness is a color: and there is discussion about whether it benefits or does not benefit as before. And if it is implicative, then it produces a particular accidental, as in our saying: 'It may be that if man is a horse then he is an animal, and not at all if void exists then man is an animal, so it may not be that if man is a horse then void exists', due to what passed in the first. Or it is implicative, as in our saying: 'It may not be that if man is an animal then he neighs, and whenever man is a horse then he neighs, so it may not be that if man is an animal then he is a horse', due to what passed.

Commentary
This passage analyzes the fourth mood of the second figure of conditional syllogisms. The author begins by referring to a prior discussion about whether a certain conditional statement yields a conclusion. He then distinguishes two types of premises: accidental (ittifaqiyya) and implicative (luzumiyya). An accidental premise is one where the antecedent and consequent have no necessary connection, while an implicative premise denotes a necessary connection. The author demonstrates with examples: 'If man is a horse, then he is an animal' is an implicative premise (since being a horse necessarily implies being an animal). But 'If void exists, then man is an animal' is accidental because there is no real connection. From such accidental premises, only accidental conclusions follow, as in the example about horse and animal. For the fourth mood specifically, if both premises are purely accidental, no conclusion follows. If they are mixed, the affirmative major premise must be examined: if it is accidental, the result is an accidental conclusion; if implicative, the conclusion is also implicative but limited. The passage underscores the importance of analyzing the nature of the conditional link in determining syllogistic validity.

Third Figure of Conditional Syllogism: Pure and Mixed Premises
Author examines third-figure conditional syllogisms and their productivity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a conditional syllogism in the third figure is productive based on the nature (accidental vs implicative) of its premises.

Core Idea
In the third figure, only specific mixtures of premises yield a valid conclusion.

This passage delves into the third figure of conditional syllogisms, distinguishing between pure accidental premises and mixed premises. The author shows that not all combinations produce a valid conclusion—some are sterile. Understanding these conditions is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic disciplines like usul al-fiqh and kalam, where conditional reasoning is common.

Guided Path
1
Identify figure and premise types
Recognize that the syllogism is in the third figure (middle term subject in both premises). Determine whether each premise is conditional and whether it is accidental (ittifāqiyya) or implicative (luzūmiyya).
Accidental premises have no necessary connection; implicative ones do.
2
Check pure accidental composition
If both premises are purely accidental, no conclusion follows regardless of quality or quantity of the major. The syllogism is sterile.
The author states: 'إذا كان التركيب من الاتفاقيات الصرفة فإنه لا يفيد'.
3
Analyze mixed affirmative major
When the major is affirmative and accidental, and the minor is implicative, an accidental conclusion follows. Example: 'Whenever man is an animal, man is rational' (implicative) and 'Whenever man is an animal, the donkey is braying' (accidental) yields 'If man is rational, the donkey is braying'.
If the minor is accidental and major implicative, the syllogism is sterile.
4
Analyze mixed negative major (second mood)
When the major is negative, whether accidental or implicative, no conclusion follows in general. Exception: if the negative major is accidental and the minor is implicative, a conclusion may be produced.
The author provides examples with man, horse, animal, body, and blackness, color, accident.
5
Consider authoritative limitations
Some earlier scholars claimed certainty for these rules, but the author refutes this, emphasizing that productivity requires truth and proper conditions. Be cautious in applying rules without checking premises.
The author says: 'الحكم في كل واحد منهما وإن كان قطعياً عند قوم من المتقدمين فإنه في حيز المنع'.

لاستلزام صدق الملزوم مع الشيء صدق اللازم معه، أو لأن صدق الكبرى يقتضي صدق الأكبر في نفسه أو عدم كونه منافيًا للأوسط. ويلزم من لزوم أيهما كان صدق الاتفاقية عامة كانت أو خاصة: هذا إذا كانت الكبرى الاتفاقيات فقد لزم من اللزوميات لما أن الصغرى من اللزوميات في مثل هذه الصورة وإن كانت هي من الاتفاقيات العامة. وأما إذا كانت الكبرى سالبة كما في الضرب الثاني مثلا فإنه لا ينتج إذا كانت اتفاقية لاحتمال And as for the third figure, we say: if the composition is of pure accidentals, it does not benefit, whether the major is affirmative or negative. For the truth of the affirmative requires the truth of the major term; and the truth of the negative requires its falsehood. And if it is true, it is true with something else. Similarly, if it is false, there is no need for the middle. And if the composition is of mixed [premises], then the major in the first mood, for example, is necessarily affirmative. And if it is affirmative, it produces an accidental [conclusion] according to the accidental used, because the truth of the necessary premise with something necessitates the truth of the necessary consequent with it, or because the truth of the major implies the truth of the major term in itself or that it is not incompatible with the middle. And from the necessity of whichever it is, the truth of the accidental, whether general or particular, follows. This is when the major is accidental while the minor is not, as in our saying: 'Whenever man is an animal, man is rational, and whenever man is an animal, the donkey is braying, so it may be that if man is rational, the donkey is braying.' As for the opposite, as when the minor is accidental while the major is not, then it is sterile and does not produce, if the accidental used is general, due to the possibility of falsehood of the middle and major with mutual entailment, and the truth of the minor in itself while it is not incompatible with the middle, as in our saying: 'Whenever man is a horse, he is an animal, and whenever man is a horse, he neighs; then it is not true that it may be that if man is an animal, he neighs.' This is among the views of some earlier scholars; and there is discussion about it. For although it does not entail from the accidentals, it entails from the implicatives, because the minor in such a form is from implicatives even if it is from general accidentals. And if the major is negative, as in the second mood for example, then it does not produce if it is accidental, due to the possibility of truth of both terms while the minor entails the major and falsehood of the middle while it entails the minor, as in our saying: 'Whenever man is a horse, he is an animal, and not at all if man is a horse, he is a body; then it is not true that it may not be that if man is an animal, he is a body.' And similarly if it is implicative, due to the possibility of truth of the three terms with mutual entailment between the two terms and the middle not being connected to either, as in our saying: 'Whenever man is rational, blackness is a color, and not at all if man is rational, blackness is an accident; then it is not true that it may not be that if blackness is a color, it is an accident.' Then the ruling in each of them, even if it is certain according to some earlier scholars, is subject to denial, because production is from the necessary [premises] on the condition of the truth of the two premises with the conditions. And similarly in the negative mood, the major may be accidental while the minor is not, and it produces, as in our saying: 'It may be that if man is an animal, he is rational, and whenever man is an animal, the donkey is braying, so it may be that if man is rational, the donkey'
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage examines the third figure of conditional syllogisms. The author first considers pure accidental premises: when both premises are accidental (ittifāqiyya ṣirfa), no conclusion follows, regardless of whether the major is affirmative or negative. The reason is that the truth conditions do not depend on the middle term. Then he deals with mixed premises, where one premise is accidental and the other is implicative (luzūmiyya). In the first mood (where the major is affirmative), if the major is accidental and the minor is implicative, a conclusion follows: it is an accidental conclusion (either general or particular). An example is given: 'Whenever man is an animal, man is rational' (implicative) and 'Whenever man is an animal, the donkey is braying' (accidental) yields 'If man is rational, the donkey is braying' (accidental). Conversely, if the minor is accidental and the major is implicative, no conclusion follows, as shown by the example about man, horse, animal, and neighing. The author notes that some earlier scholars held this view, but there is controversy: if the minor is actually implicative in essence (though appearing accidental), a conclusion might follow. Next, the author discusses negative major premises in the second mood. Whether the major is accidental or implicative, no conclusion follows, because of possible truth combinations where the middle term fails to connect properly. He provides examples with blackness, color, and accident. Finally, he remarks that some earlier logicians considered these conclusions certain, but he rejects that certainty, emphasizing that production of a conclusion requires the premises to be true and meet specific conditions. The passage ends with an example where a negative mood with an accidental major and implicative minor does produce a conclusion: 'It may be that if man is an animal, he is rational' (accidental) and 'Whenever man is an animal, the donkey is braying' (implicative) yields 'If man is rational, the donkey is braying'. The text is cut off at this point.
