Coincidental vs Implicative Premises in Conditional Syllogisms
Author distinguishes premise types and their effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish coincidental (ittifāqiyya) from implicative (luzūmiyya) premises in conditional syllogisms and explain how each type affects productiveness.

Core Idea
The type of premise determines whether a conditional syllogism yields a conclusion.

In conditional syllogisms, not all premises are equal: some express necessary implication (luzūmiyya), while others are merely coincidental (ittifāqiyya). This distinction is crucial for evaluating whether a syllogism is productive (muntij) or sterile (ʿaqīm). The passage examines how these premise types interact in the third figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premise types
Locate the terms اتفاقية and لزومية in the passage. Notice that the author repeatedly uses them to classify premises.
Coincidental premises have accidental connections; implicative premises have necessary ones.
2
Understand their logical force
An implicative premise (e.g., 'if man is an animal, he is sentient') guarantees a necessary relation. A coincidental premise (e.g., 'if man is a horse, he is an animal') may be true but not necessary.
The passage uses the example 'كلما كان الإنسان فرسا كان حيوانا' as coincidental.
3
See the interaction in the first mood
The author says that when the minor is coincidental and the major is implicative (opposite of the first mood), the syllogism is sterile. However, he later adds that if the minor is actually implicative in form, it may produce.
This shows the nuanced role of premise type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحمار ناهقًا فقد يكون إذا كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الحمار ناهقا. فأما إذا كان على العكس كما إذا كانت الصغرى اتفاقية دون الكبرى فإنه عقيم لا ينتج إذا كانت الاتفاقية المستعملة عامة لاحتمال كذب الأوسط والأكبر مع التلازم بينهما؛ وصدق الأصغر في نفسه مع عدم كونه منافيًا للأوسط كما في قولنا كلما كان الإنسان فرسا كان حيوانًا، وكلَّما كان الإنسان فرسا كان صهالا فإنه لا يصدق قد يكون إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان صهَّالا : هذا من جملة ما ذهب إليه قوم من المتقدمين؛ وفيه من الكلام فإنه وإن لم يلزم منه من الاتفاقيات فقد لزم من اللزوميات لما أن الصغرى من اللزوميات في مثل هذه الصورة وإن كانت هي من الاتفاقيات العامة. وأما إذا كانت الكبرى سالبة كما في الضرب الثاني مثلا فإنه لا ينتج إذا كانت اتفاقية لاحتمال صدق الطرفين مع استلزام الأصغر للأكبر وكذب الأوسط مع استلزامه للأصغر كما في قولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان فرسا كان حيوانًا، وليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان فرسا كان جسما فإنه لا يصدق قد لا يكون إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان جسما. وكذلك إذا كانت لزومية لاحتمال صدق الحدود الثلاثة مع التلازم بين الطرفين وعدم تعلق الأوسط بأحدهما كما في قولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقا كان السواد لونا، وليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان ناطقا كان السواد عرَضًا فإنه لا يصدق قد لا يكون إذا كان السواد لونا كان عرضًا. ثم الحكم في كل واحد منهما وإن كان قطعياً عند قوم من المتقدمين فإنه في حيز المنع لما أن الإنتاج من اللوازم على تقدير صدق المقدمتين مع الشرائط. وكذلك في الضرب السالبة فإن الكبرى قد تكون اتفاقية دون الصغرى وإنه ينتج كما في قولنا قد يكون إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان ناطقا، وكلما كان الإنسان حيوانا كان الحمار ناهقًا فقد يكون إذا كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الحمار ناهقًا لما مرَّ في الضرب الأول. وقد تكون لزومية وإنه لا ينتج عند قوم كما في قولنا قد يكون إذا كان الإنسان فرسا كان حيوانًا، وكلَّما كان الإنسان فرسا كان صهالا فإنه لا يصدق قد يكون إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان صهالا وفيه من الكلام كما مرَّ في الأول. وفي الضرب الرابع كذلك إذا كانت الكبرى اتفاقية فإنه ينتج كما في قولنا كلما كان الإنسان حيوانا كان حساسا، وقد يكون إذا كان الإنسان حيوانًا كان الفرس صهَّالا فقد يكون إذا كان الإنسان حساسًا كان الفرس صهالا . فأما إذا كانت لزومية فإنه لا ينتج عند ذلك القوم كما في قولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان أسدًا كان حيوانًا، وقد يكون إذا كان الإنسان أسدًا كان زائرا فإنه لا يصدق قد يكون إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان زائرا وفيه من الكلام كما مرَّ. وفي الضرب الخامس إذا كانت الكبرى اتفاقية فإنه لا ينتج كما في قولنا قد يكون إذا كان الإنسان فرسًا كان حيوانًا، وليس البتة إذا كان فرسًا كان حساسًا. وكذلك إذا كانت لزومية فإنه لا ينتج عن ذلك القوم؛ والكلام فيهما قد مرَّ في الضرب الثاني. وعلى هذا في الضرب السادس فإنه لا ينتج عند ذلك القوم كذلك اتفاقية كانت الكبرى كما في قولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان فرسا كان حيوانًا، وليس كلَّما كان الإنسان فرسا كان حساسًا، وكذلك إذا كانت لزومية؛ والكلام فيهما أيضًا نحو الكلام في الضرب الثاني.
The donkey is braying, so it may happen that if man is rational, the donkey is braying. But if it is the opposite, as when the minor is coincidental without the major, then it is sterile and produces nothing, if the coincidental used is general, due to the possibility of the middle and major being false while there is implication between them; and the minor being true in itself without being contrary to the middle, as in our saying: Whenever man is a horse, he is an animal, and Whenever man is a horse, he neighs — then it is not true that it may happen that if man is an animal, he neighs. This is among what a group of earlier scholars held; and there is discussion in it, for although a conclusion does not follow from it with coincidentals, it does follow with implicatives because the minor is among implicatives in such a form, even if it is from general coincidentals. And if the major is negative, as in the second mood for example, then it does not produce if it is coincidental, due to the possibility of the two terms being true with the implication of the minor for the major, and the middle being false with its implication for the minor, as in our saying: Whenever man is a horse, he is an animal, and it is never the case that if man is a horse, he is a body — then it is not true that it may not happen that if man is an animal, he is a body. And similarly if it is implicative, due to the possibility of the three terms being true with the implication between the two terms and the middle not being attached to either, as in our saying: Whenever man is rational, blackness is a color, and it is never the case that if man is rational, blackness is an accident — then it is not true that it may not happen that if blackness is a color, it is an accident. Then the ruling on each of them, even if it is certain according to a group of earlier scholars, is subject to denial, since production is from implications on the assumption of the truth of both premises with conditions. And similarly in the negative mood, the major may be coincidental without the minor, and it produces, as in our saying: It may happen that if man is an animal, he is rational, and Whenever man is an animal, the donkey is braying — then it may happen that if man is rational, the donkey is braying, as was mentioned in the first mood. And it may be implicative and does not produce according to some, as in our saying: It may happen that if man is a horse, he is an animal, and Whenever man is a horse, he neighs — then it is not true that it may happen that if man is an animal, he neighs, and the discussion is as in the first. And in the fourth mood similarly, if the major is coincidental, it produces, as in our saying: Whenever man is an animal, he is sentient, and It may happen that if man is an animal, the horse neighs — then it may happen that if man is sentient, the horse neighs. But if it is implicative, it does not produce according to that group, as in our saying: Whenever man is a lion, he is an animal, and It may happen that if man is a lion, he is visiting — then it is not true that it may happen that if man is an animal, he is visiting, and the discussion is as before. And in the fifth mood, if the major is coincidental, it does not produce, as in our saying: It may happen that if man is a horse, he is an animal, and it is never the case that if he is a horse, he is sentient. And similarly if it is implicative, it does not produce according to that group; the discussion on both has passed in the second mood. And likewise in the sixth mood, it does not produce according to that group, whether the major is coincidental as in our saying: Whenever man is a horse, he is an animal, and Not whenever man is a horse, he is sentient, and similarly if it is implicative; and the discussion on both is also similar to the discussion in the second mood.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a detailed examination of the productiveness (إنتاج) of conditional syllogisms in the third figure, specifically focusing on whether the premises are coincidental (اتفاقية) or implicative (لزومية). The author follows a group of earlier scholars and critiques their views. The passage opens with an example: 'The donkey is braying' as a consequent. The author first discusses the case where the minor is coincidental and the major is implicative (the opposite of the first mood), concluding that it is sterile (عقيم) because the middle and major could be false while still implying each other, and the minor could be true without contradicting the middle. An example is given: 'Whenever man is a horse, he is an animal; whenever man is a horse, he neighs' does not imply 'if man is an animal, he neighs'. The author notes that some earlier scholars held this, but objects that while no conclusion follows from coincidentals, it does follow from implicatives because the minor is implicative. The discussion then moves to the second mood where the major is negative and coincidental: it does not produce, as shown by an example. If the major is implicative, it also does not produce because the three terms could be true with no relation between middle and one term. The author then presents a general principle: the ruling of sterility is subject to denial (في حيز المنع) because production depends on conditions. In the negative mood (الضرب السالبة), if the major is coincidental and the minor is not, it does produce, as in the example from the first mood. But if the major is implicative, it does not produce according to some. The fourth mood is then examined: if the major is coincidental, it produces; if implicative, it does not. The fifth and sixth moods are similar to the second and never produce, whether the major is coincidental or implicative. Throughout, the author maintains that the earlier scholars' judgments are not absolute.

Key Terms
اتفاقية
لزومية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of premise expresses a necessary implication?
A) اتفاقية (coincidental)
B) لزومية (implicative)
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
A premise's type—coincidental or implicative—is a critical factor in determining whether a conditional syllogism can produce a valid conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Classify the following premises as coincidental or implicative and justify: (1) 'Whenever the sun rises, it is day.' (2) 'Whenever Zayd is in the house, Amr is in the market.'
?

The Syllogism
advanced
15 min
The Six Moods of the Third Figure: Which Are Productive?
Author enumerates and evaluates six moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the six moods of the third-figure conditional syllogism produce a conclusion and explain why based on premise types.

Core Idea
Productiveness in the third figure depends on the interplay of coincidental and implicative premises across six moods.

The third figure of conditional syllogisms has six moods (أضرب), each defined by the combination of premise types and their order. The author systematically evaluates each mood, showing that some are productive and others sterile. This lesson walks through the six moods and the reasoning behind their productiveness.

Guided Path
1
Learn the structure of the third figure
In the third figure, the middle term (أوسط) is the antecedent of both premises. The conclusion is a conditional with the antecedent from one premise's consequent and the consequent from the other's consequent.
The passage uses examples like 'if man is a horse, he is an animal' as the minor premise.
2
Identify the six moods
The author discusses six moods (ضرب) from first to sixth. Each mood differs in whether the minor and major are coincidental or implicative, and whether they are affirmative or negative.
The moods are: 1st (minor coincidental, major implicative), 2nd (major negative), 3rd (negative), 4th (major coincidental), 5th (major negative coincidental), 6th (major negative).
3
Determine productiveness for each mood
For each mood, the author states whether it produces (ينتج) or is sterile (عقيم). For example, the first mood is sterile if the minor is coincidental, but if the minor is implicative it may produce. The fourth mood produces if the major is coincidental, but not if implicative.
Refer to the table below: mood 1: not produce if minor coincidental; mood 2: not produce; mood 3 (negative): produces if major coincidental; mood 4: produces if major coincidental; mood 5: not produce; mood 6: not produce.
4
Understand the reasoning behind sterility
Sterility often arises from the possibility of the terms being true without the required relations. For instance, in the first mood, the middle and major could be false while still implying each other, and the minor true without contradicting the middle.
The author gives examples to illustrate each case.

Critiquing Absolute Judgments: The Author's Refinement
Author critiques earlier scholars' absolute productiveness rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's critique of earlier scholars' absolute claims about productiveness and evaluate his reasoning.

Core Idea
The author denies the absolute certainty of earlier scholars' rulings on productiveness, arguing that production depends on conditions.

The author repeatedly notes that a group of earlier scholars made definite judgments about which moods are productive. However, he places these rulings 'in the realm of denial' (في حيز المنع), arguing that production from implied premises requires specific conditions. This lesson focuses on his critical approach and its logical basis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the earlier scholars' position
The passage mentions 'قوم من المتقدمين' (a group of earlier scholars) who held that certain moods are absolutely sterile (عقيم) or productive.
For example, they said the first mood is sterile when the minor is coincidental.
2
Find the author's denial statement
Look for the phrase 'في حيز المنع' (subject to denial). The author states that even if the earlier scholars thought the ruling was certain, it is actually deniable.
This occurs after the discussion of the second mood.
3
Understand the basis of the denial
The author argues that production (إنتاج) from necessary implications (لزوميات) is conditional upon the truth of both premises and certain conditions. The earlier scholars' absolute judgments ignored these conditions.
The passage says: 'الإنتاج من اللوازم على تقدير صدق المقدمتين مع الشرائط' (production from implications is on the assumption of the truth of both premises with conditions).
4
Apply the critique to specific moods
The author shows that in many moods, if you consider the premises as implicative rather than coincidental, the syllogism becomes productive. For example, in the first mood, he says that although no conclusion follows from coincidentals, it does follow from implicatives because the minor in that form is implicative.
This demonstrates that the earlier scholars' classification was too rigid.

The donkey is braying, so it may happen that if man is rational, the donkey is braying. But if it is the opposite, as when the minor is coincidental without the major, then it is sterile and produces nothing, if the coincidental used is general, due to the possibility of the middle and major being false while there is implication between them; and the minor being true in itself without being contrary to the middle, as in our saying: Whenever man is a horse, he is an animal, and Whenever man is a horse, he neighs — then it is not true that it may happen that if man is an animal, he neighs. This is among what a group of earlier scholars held; and there is discussion in it, for although a conclusion does not follow from it with coincidentals, it does follow with implicatives because the minor is among implicatives in such a form, even if it is from general coincidentals. And if the major is negative, as in the second mood for example, then it does not produce if it is coincidental, due to the possibility of the two terms being true with the implication of the minor for the major, and the middle being false with its implication for the minor, as in our saying: Whenever man is a horse, he is an animal, and it is never the case that if man is a horse, he is a body — then it is not true that it may not happen that if man is an animal, he is a body. And similarly if it is implicative, due to the possibility of the three terms being true with the implication between the two terms and the middle not being attached to either, as in our saying: Whenever man is rational, blackness is a color, and it is never the case that if man is rational, blackness is an accident — then it is not true that it may not happen that if blackness is a color, it is an accident. Then the ruling on each of them, even if it is certain according to a group of earlier scholars, is subject to denial, since production is from implications on the assumption of the truth of both premises with conditions. And similarly in the negative mood, the major may be coincidental without the minor, and it produces, as in our saying: It may happen that if man is an animal, he is rational, and Whenever man is an animal, the donkey is braying — then it may happen that if man is rational, the donkey is braying, as was mentioned in the first mood. And it may be implicative and does not produce according to some, as in our saying: It may happen that if man is a horse, he is an animal, and Whenever man is a horse, he neighs — then it is not true that it may happen that if man is an animal, he neighs, and the discussion is as in the first. And in the fourth mood similarly, if the major is coincidental, it produces, as in our saying: Whenever man is an animal, he is sentient, and It may happen that if man is an animal, the horse neighs — then it may happen that if man is sentient, the horse neighs. But if it is implicative, it does not produce according to that group, as in our saying: Whenever man is a lion, he is an animal, and It may happen that if man is a lion, he is visiting — then it is not true that it may happen that if man is an animal, he is visiting, and the discussion is as before. And in the fifth mood, if the major is coincidental, it does not produce, as in our saying: It may happen that if man is a horse, he is an animal, and it is never the case that if he is a horse, he is sentient. And similarly if it is implicative, it does not produce according to that group; the discussion on both has passed in the second mood. And likewise in the sixth mood, it does not produce according to that group, whether the major is coincidental as in our saying: Whenever man is a horse, he is an animal, and Not whenever man is a horse, he is sentient, and similarly if it is implicative; and the discussion on both is also similar to the discussion in the second mood.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a detailed examination of the productiveness (إنتاج) of conditional syllogisms in the third figure, specifically focusing on whether the premises are coincidental (اتفاقية) or implicative (لزومية). The author follows a group of earlier scholars and critiques their views. The passage opens with an example: 'The donkey is braying' as a consequent. The author first discusses the case where the minor is coincidental and the major is implicative (the opposite of the first mood), concluding that it is sterile (عقيم) because the middle and major could be false while still implying each other, and the minor could be true without contradicting the middle. An example is given: 'Whenever man is a horse, he is an animal; whenever man is a horse, he neighs' does not imply 'if man is an animal, he neighs'. The author notes that some earlier scholars held this, but objects that while no conclusion follows from coincidentals, it does follow from implicatives because the minor is implicative. The discussion then moves to the second mood where the major is negative and coincidental: it does not produce, as shown by an example. If the major is implicative, it also does not produce because the three terms could be true with no relation between middle and one term. The author then presents a general principle: the ruling of sterility is subject to denial (في حيز المنع) because production depends on conditions. In the negative mood (الضرب السالبة), if the major is coincidental and the minor is not, it does produce, as in the example from the first mood. But if the major is implicative, it does not produce according to some. The fourth mood is then examined: if the major is coincidental, it produces; if implicative, it does not. The fifth and sixth moods are similar to the second and never produce, whether the major is coincidental or implicative. Throughout, the author maintains that the earlier scholars' judgments are not absolute.

Fourth Figure Sterility with Pure Coincidentals
Author discusses the fourth figure's productivity with pure coincidental and mixed premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when the fourth figure of a conditional syllogism yields no conclusion based on premise type, specifically when both premises are pure coincidentals.

Core Idea
In the fourth figure, pure coincidental premises produce no necessary conclusion.

The fourth figure of conditional syllogisms has specific rules. The author establishes a key rule: if both premises are purely coincidental, no conclusion follows, regardless of the minor's quality. This lesson breaks down the reasoning behind this rule and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure and premise types
Recognize that the syllogism is in the fourth figure (shakl rābiʿ). Determine whether each premise is pure coincidental (ittifāqiyya ṣirfa) or implicative (luzūmiyya).
Pure coincidental premises have no necessary link between antecedent and consequent.
2
Apply the rule for pure coincidentals
If both premises are pure coincidental, the syllogism is useless (lā yufīd aṣlan). No necessary conclusion can be drawn.
The author states this explicitly: 'إذا كان التركيب من الاتفاقيات الصرفة فلا يفيد أصلا'
3
Examine the reasoning for affirmative minor
If the minor is affirmative, its truth requires the truth of its consequent (the minor term). This consequent's truth with the major depends on whether the major is true or false, yielding no necessary connection.
This reasoning shows why no conclusion follows.
4
Examine the reasoning for negative minor
If the minor is negative, its truth requires the falsity of its consequent (the minor term). Since the major premise is true, the middle term is true, leading to the minor term being false with the major—again no necessary conclusion.
Both cases lead to the same result: no conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٤] وأما في الشكل الرابع فنقول إذا كان التركيب من الاتفاقيات الصرفة فلا يفيد أصلا موجبة كانت الصغرى أو سالبة. فإن صدق الموجبة يقتضي صدق تاليها وهو الأصغر فيلزم صدقه مع الأكبر إن كان الأكبر صادقا، وعدم صدقه معه إن كان كاذبًا. وصدق السالبة يقتضي كذب الأصغر الذي هو تاليها لصدق الأوسط قطعًا ضرورة صدق الكبرى. ولما كذب الأصغر في نفسه كذب مع الأكبر. وأما إذا كان التركيب من المختلطات كما إذا كانت الصغرى لزومية

And the truth of the negative requires the falsity of the minor term, which is its consequent, because of the truth of the middle necessarily, due to the truth of the major. And since the minor term is false in itself, it is false with the major. And if the composition is of mixed types, as when the minor is implicative...

Commentary
This passage begins a discussion of the fourth figure (الشكل الرابع) of conditional syllogisms. The author states a general rule: if both premises are pure coincidentals (اتفاقيات صرفة), no conclusion whatsoever can be drawn, regardless of whether the minor is affirmative or negative. The reasoning is explained: if the minor is affirmative, its truth entails the truth of its consequent (the minor term), which then implies that the minor term is true with the major if the major is true, or false with it if false—this does not yield a new necessary connection. If the minor is negative, its truth requires the falsity of the minor term (since the consequent of a negative conditional is false), and because the major premise is true, the middle term is true, leading to the falsity of the minor term with the major. In either case, no necessary conclusion follows. The author then begins to address mixed premises (مختلطات), where one premise is implicative and the other coincidental, but the text breaks off mid-sentence at 'as when the minor is implicative', leaving the discussion incomplete.

Fourth Figure of Conditional Syllogisms: Conditions for Productivity
Author examines the fourth figure of conditional syllogisms and its productivity conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a fourth-figure conditional syllogism yields a conclusion by analyzing the modal status (necessary or accidental) of its premises.

Core Idea
Pure accidentals yield nothing; mixed premises yield only under specific conditions.

The fourth figure of conditional syllogisms is often misunderstood as always productive. In this passage, the author carefully dissects the conditions under which a conclusion follows, distinguishing between pure accidental premises and mixed compositions. We will walk through the rules and apply them to Islamic examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure and premises
Recognize the fourth figure: the middle term is the consequent of the major premise and the antecedent of the minor premise (or vice versa, depending on the mood). List the two conditional premises clearly.
The middle term is the common proposition linking the premises.
2
Classify each premise as necessary or accidental
Determine whether each conditional is necessary (luzumiyya) – the antecedent necessarily implies the consequent – or accidental (ittifaqiyya) – a contingent connection. Subdivide accidentals into general (universal) and special (particular).
Use the author's terminology: 'pure accidentals' means both are accidental.
3
Apply the rule for pure accidentals
If both premises are pure accidentals, the syllogism yields no conclusion at all. The author explains the reasoning: an affirmative minor forces the truth of its consequent, but that consequent may not combine with the major; a negative minor forces the falsity of its consequent, which then cannot be true with the major.
Example: 'Every time it is day, I am awake' (accidental) and 'Every time I eat, it is day' (accidental) – no necessary conclusion.
4
Analyze mixed premises for the first and second moods
If one premise is necessary and the other accidental, a conclusion may follow only if the major is a special accidental (not general). The author gives examples: necessary minor + special accidental major yields a special accidental conclusion; necessary minor + general accidental major does not. Conversely, if the minor is accidental and the major is necessary, no conclusion follows.
Trace the reasoning using the author's examples: human/animal/sentient for the positive case; void existence for the negative.
5
Examine the third mood
In the third mood, if the minor is a special accidental, a conclusion (special accidental) follows regardless of the major's truth value. The author shows that the middle term's truth or falsity both lead to the conclusion. If the minor is a general accidental, the text is incomplete but suggests a conclusion may still hold.
Focus on the logical structure: the middle is negated from the minor, leading to a disjunctive reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في الشكل الرابع فنقول إذا كان التركيب من الاتفاقيات الصرفة فلا يفيد أصلا موجبة كانت الصغرى أو سالبة. فإن صدق الموجبة يقتضي صدق تاليها وهو الأصغر فيلزم صدقه مع الأكبر إن كان الأكبر صادقا، وعدم صدقه معه إن كان كاذبًا. وصدق السالبة يقتضي كذب الأصغر الذي هو تاليها لصدق الأوسط قطعًا ضرورة صدق الكبرى. ولما كذب الأصغر في نفسه كذب مع الأكبر. وأما إذا كان التركيب من المختلطات كما إذا كانت الصغرى لزومية دون الكبرى أو بالعكس فإنه ينتج اتفاقية خاصة إن كانت الكبرى كذلك كما في الضرب الأول. فإن الأوسط ملزوم للأصغر وإنه مع الأكبر فيلزم أن يكون الأصغر مع الأكبر كما في قولنا كلما كان الإنسان حيوانا كان حساسًا، وكلَّما كان السواد لونًا كان الإنسان حيوانًا فقد يكون إذا كان الإنسان حساسًا كان السواد لونًا. وإن كانت الكبرى اتفاقية عامة فإنه لا ينتج لاحتمال كذب الأكبر في نفسه وصدق الأوسط في الأصغر مع التلازم بينهما وعدم التلازم بين الأصغر والأكبر كما في قولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان حيوانًا كان حساسًا، وكلَّما كان الخلاء موجودًا كان الإنسان حيوانًا مع أنه لا يصدق قد يكون إذا كان الإنسان حساسا كان الخلاء موجودًا. وأما إذا كانت الصغرى اتفاقية خاصة كانت أو عامة والكبرى لزومية فإنه لا ينتج أصلا لاحتمال صدق الأوسط والأصغر، وكذب الأكبر مع كونه مستلزما للأوسط دون الأصغر كما في قولنا كلما كان الإنسان حيوانا كان ضاحكًا، وكلَّما كان الإنسان فرسا كان حيوانا مع أنه لا يصدق قد يكون إذا كان الإنسان ضاحكًا كان فرسًا. وعلى هذا في الضرب الثاني منه فإنه مع الضرب الأول على السواء والأحكام المذكورة فيقال قد يكون إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان حساسًا، وكلما كان السواد لونا كان الإنسان حيوانا فقد يكون إذا كان الإنسان حساسًا كان السواد لونًا كما مرَّ في الأول. وفي الضرب الثالث إن كانت الصغرى اتفاقية خاصة ينتج اتفاقية خاصة؛ إذ الأوسط المسلوب عنه الأصغر إما أن يكون صادقًا؛ وحينئذ يلزم كذب الأصغر وإلا لكذبت السالبة وكذب الأصغر مما يستلزم صدق النتيجة. وإما أن يكون كاذبا؛ وحينئذ يلزم كذب الأكبر لانتفاء ما يلزمه، وكذب الأكبر مما يستلزم صدق النتيجة أيضًا. وإن كانت الاتفاقية عامة فصدقها يقتضي كون الأصغر
As for the fourth figure, we say: if the composition is from pure accidentals, it yields nothing at all, whether the minor is affirmative or negative. For the truth of the affirmative necessitates the truth of its consequent, which is the minor, so its truth with the major follows if the major is true, and its non-truth with it if it is false. And the truth of the negative necessitates the falsity of the minor, which is its consequent, due to the truth of the middle necessarily by the necessity of the truth of the major. And since the minor is false in itself, it is false with the major. As for if the composition is mixed, as when the minor is necessary without the major or vice versa, then it produces a special accidental if the major is also like that, as in the first mode. For the middle is implied by the minor, and it is with the major, so it follows that the minor is with the major, as in our saying: Every time a human is an animal, he is sentient; and every time blackness is a color, a human is an animal; so it may be that if a human is sentient, blackness is a color. And if the major is a general accidental, it does not produce, due to the possibility of the major being false in itself and the middle being true in the minor with the concomitance between them and the lack of concomitance between the minor and the major, as in our saying: Every time a human is an animal, he is sentient; and every time a void exists, a human is an animal; and it is not true that it may be that if a human is sentient, a void exists. As for if the minor is a special or general accidental and the major is necessary, it does not produce at all, due to the possibility of the middle and the minor being true, and the major being false despite being implied by the middle without the minor, as in our saying: Every time a human is an animal, he is laughing; and every time a human is a horse, he is an animal; and it is not true that it may be that if a human is laughing, he is a horse. And likewise in its second mode, it is the same as the first mode with the rulings mentioned, and it is said: it may be that if a human is an animal, he is sentient; and every time blackness is a color, a human is an animal; so it may be that if a human is sentient, blackness is a color, as mentioned in the first. And in the third mode: if the minor is a special accidental, it yields a special accidental; for the middle from which the minor is negated is either true; then the falsity of the minor follows, otherwise the negative would be false, and the falsity of the minor necessitates the truth of the conclusion; or it is false; then the falsity of the major follows due to the non-existence of what implies it, and the falsity of the major also necessitates the truth of the conclusion. And if the accidental is general, its truth necessitates that the minor...
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a detailed examination of the fourth figure of conditional syllogisms. The author begins by stating that when both premises are pure accidentals (i.e., neither is necessary), no conclusion follows at all, whether the minor premise is affirmative or negative. He explains why: if the minor is affirmative, its truth forces the truth of its consequent (the minor term), which then may or may not be true with the major term depending on the major's truth value. If the minor is negative, its truth forces the falsity of its consequent (the minor term), but then the minor term is false in itself, so it cannot be true with the major. Next, the author considers mixed compositions where one premise is necessary and the other accidental. If the minor is necessary and the major is a special accidental (i.e., a contingent but actual connection), then a conclusion does follow in the form of a special accidental. He illustrates with an example: 'Every time a human is an animal, he is sentient' (necessary minor) and 'Every time blackness is a color, a human is an animal' (accidental major) yields 'It may be that if a human is sentient, blackness is a color.' However, if the major is a general accidental (a universal but non-necessary connection), no conclusion follows because the major could be false in itself while the middle is true of the minor, yet there is no necessary link between the minor and major. Example: 'Every time a human is an animal, he is sentient' and 'Every time a void exists, a human is an animal' does not yield 'If a human is sentient, a void exists.' Conversely, if the minor is accidental (special or general) and the major is necessary, no conclusion follows at all because the middle and minor could be true while the major is false, even though the major is implied by the middle, it is not implied by the minor. Example: 'Every time a human is an animal, he is laughing' (necessary) and 'Every time a human is a horse, he is an animal' (accidental) does not yield 'If a human is laughing, he is a horse.' The author then states that the second mode of the fourth figure is analogous to the first. Finally, he analyzes the third mode: if the minor is a special accidental, a conclusion follows because the middle term (from which the minor is negated) is either true or false; in either case, the conclusion (a special accidental) holds. If the minor is a general accidental, the text cuts off, but it suggests that the truth of the minor necessitates the minor term's existence, implying a conclusion. The commentary follows the author's order, explaining each subcase with its reasoning and examples, all within the framework of Islamic logic. Note: the example 'blackness is a color' and 'void exists' are abstract metaphysical concepts, but they serve as typical logical examples in the tradition.

Key Terms

الاتفاقية الصرفة
اللزومية
الاتفاقية الخاصة
الاتفاقية العامة
الضرب الأول
الضرب الثاني
الضرب الثالث

A) A necessary conclusion
B) A special accidental conclusion

D) A universal affirmative conclusion

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure of conditional syllogisms is productive only when at least one premise is necessary and the combination meets specific conditions; pure accidentals never yield a conclusion.

Structure of Conditional Syllogisms in the First Figure
Author analyzes productiveness of conditional syllogisms when a premise is missing or reversed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the minor, middle, and major terms (as propositions) in a conditional syllogism and distinguish between necessary and accidental connections.

Core Idea
In conditional syllogisms, the middle term is a shared proposition, and connection type determines productiveness.

This passage explores how conditional syllogisms produce conclusions in the first and second figures when one premise is missing or reversed. The author uses the terms 'asghar' (minor), 'awsat' (middle), and 'akbar' (major) to denote the propositions involved. Understanding these components is essential before analyzing validity conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three terms
In the author's analysis, 'asghar' is the antecedent of the minor premise, 'awsat' is the shared proposition (the consequent of one and antecedent of the other), and 'akbar' is the consequent of the major premise. Locate these in the Arabic example: 'كلما كان الإنسان حيوانا كان حساسا' and 'كلما كان السواد لونا كان الإنسان حيوانا'.
2
Classify the connection type of each premise
The author distinguishes between 'luzūmiyya' (necessary connection) and 'ittifāqiyya' (accidental connection). The example 'كلما كان الإنسان حيوانا كان حساسا' is a necessary connection because being an animal necessarily implies being sentient. Determine if each premise is necessary or accidental.
3
Map the inference pattern
The author states that when the major premise is specific accidental, the syllogism produces a specific accidental conclusion. Map the pattern: from 'If A then B' and 'If C then A', the conclusion is 'If B then C', but only under specific conditions.
This is not a universally valid form; the author is identifying when it works.

Source Text

دون الكبرى أو بالعكس فإنه ينتج اتفاقية خاصة إن كانت الكبرى كذلك كما في الضرب الأول. فإن الأوسط ملزوم للأصغر وإنه مع الأكبر فيلزم أن يكون الأصغر مع الأكبر كما في قولنا كلما كان الإنسان حيوانا كان حساسًا، وكلما كان السواد لونًا كان الإنسان حيوانا فقد يكون إذا كان الإنسان حساسًا كان السواد لونًا. وإن كانت الكبرى اتفاقية عامة فإنه لا ينتج لاحتمال كذب الأكبر في نفسه وصدق الأوسط في الأصغر مع التلازم بينهما وعدم التلازم بين الأصغر والأكبر كما في قولنا كلما كان الإنسان حيوانا كان حساسًا، وكلما كان الخلاء موجودًا كان الإنسان حيوانا مع أنه لا يصدق قد يكون إذا كان الإنسان حساسا كان الخلاء موجودًا. وأما إذا كانت الصغرى اتفاقية خاصة كانت أو عامة والكبرى لزومية فإنه لا ينتج أصلا لاحتمال صدق الأوسط والأصغر، وكذب الأكبر مع كونه مستلزما للأوسط دون الأصغر كما في قولنا كلما كان الإنسان حيوانا كان ضاحكًا، وكلما كان الإنسان فرسا كان حيوانا مع أنه لا يصدق قد يكون إذا كان الإنسان ضاحكًا كان فرسًا. وعلى هذا في الضرب الثاني منه فإنه مع الضرب الأول على السواء والأحكام المذكورة فيقال قد يكون إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان حساسًا، وكلما كان السواد لونا كان الإنسان حيوانا فقد يكون إذا كان الإنسان حساسًا كان السواد لونًا كما مر في الأول.
Without the major premise or the opposite, then it produces a specific accidental [conclusion] if the major premise is also like that, as in the first figure. For the middle term is implied by the minor term, and it [the middle] together with the major term, so it follows that the minor term is together with the major term, as in our saying: Whenever man is an animal, he is sentient, and whenever blackness is a color, man is an animal; so it may be that when man is sentient, blackness is a color. And if the major premise is a general accidental, then it does not produce due to the possibility of the major term being false in itself and the middle term being true in the minor term while there is a necessary connection between them and no connection between the minor and major, as in our saying: Whenever man is an animal, he is sentient, and whenever void exists, man is an animal, even though it is not true that whenever man is sentient, void exists. As for when the minor premise is a specific or general accidental and the major premise is necessary, then it does not produce at all due to the possibility of the middle and minor terms being true, and the major term false while being implied by the middle but not by the minor, as in our saying: Whenever man is an animal, he is laughing, and whenever man is a horse, he is an animal, even though it is not true that whenever a man is laughing, he is a horse. And likewise in the second figure, it is the same as the first figure with the mentioned rulings; so it is said: It may be that when man is an animal, he is sentient, and whenever blackness is a color, man is an animal; so it may be that when man is sentient, blackness is a color, as has passed in the first.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage examines the productiveness (intāj) of conditional syllogisms (qiyās istithnā'ī) in the first and second figures when one premise is missing or reversed. The author first considers the case where the major premise is a specific accidental connection (ittifāqiyya khāṣṣa): if the major premise is also specific accidental, the syllogism produces a specific accidental conclusion. An example is given: 'Whenever man is an animal, he is sentient' (major premise) and 'Whenever blackness is a color, man is an animal' (minor premise); from this one might conclude 'Whenever man is sentient, blackness is a color' (but this is not always true, illustrating the weakness). Next, if the major premise is a general accidental connection (ittifāqiyya 'āmma), the syllogism does not produce a valid conclusion because the major term could be false even though the middle and minor hold. The example with 'void' shows this: 'Whenever man is an animal, he is sentient' and 'Whenever void exists, man is an animal' does not imply 'Whenever man is sentient, void exists'. Then the author considers the case where the minor premise is accidental (specific or general) and the major is necessary (luzūmiyya); here the syllogism fails to produce because the major term, though implied by the middle, may be false for the minor. The example uses 'Whenever man is an animal, he is laughing' and 'Whenever man is a horse, he is animal' but it does not follow that 'Whenever man is laughing, he is a horse'. Finally, he states that the second figure follows the same rulings as the first, so the same examples apply.

Validity Conditions for Conditional Syllogisms
Author analyzes productiveness of conditional syllogisms when a premise is missing or reversed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a conditional syllogism produces a conclusion based on the connection types (accidental vs. necessary) of the premises.

Core Idea
Productiveness depends on the combination of accidental and necessary connections in the premises.

The author presents three scenarios for the first figure: when the major premise is specific accidental, general accidental, or necessary, and correspondingly when the minor is accidental. He shows which combinations yield a conclusion. This lesson focuses on the conditions for validity (productiveness) in these syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the connection type of major premise
In the passage, the author first checks if the major premise (الكبرى) is specific accidental (اتفاقية خاصة), general accidental (اتفاقية عامة), or necessary (لزومية). Note the example for each.
2
Identify the connection type of minor premise
The minor premise (الصغرى) may be accidental or necessary. The author considers cases where the minor is accidental (specific or general) and the major is necessary, and vice versa.
3
Apply the productiveness rule
According to the author: (1) If major is specific accidental, minor can be any, produces specific accidental conclusion. (2) If major is general accidental, does not produce. (3) If minor is accidental and major is necessary, does not produce. Practice by checking the given examples.
4
Test the second figure
The author says the same rulings apply to the second figure. Use the same conditions to analyze a second-figure syllogism.

The Syllogism
advanced
8 min
Third Figure with Specific Accidental Minor
Author proves productiveness for specific accidental minor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a third-figure conditional syllogism with a specific accidental minor premise is productive and justify your reasoning using the author's reductio argument.

Core Idea
A specific accidental minor in the third figure always yields a specific accidental conclusion.

In this passage, the author examines the conditions under which the third figure of conditional syllogism produces a valid conclusion. He begins with the case where the minor premise is a specific accidental conditional. Understanding his reductio reasoning is key to evaluating similar syllogisms in Islamic sciences, such as those used in kalam arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogism structure
The third figure in conditional syllogism has a major premise that is a universal negative conditional and a minor premise that is a conditional. The middle term is the term denied from the minor in the major premise. Here, the minor is a specific accidental conditional (ittifāqiyya khāṣṣa).
The major premise has the form: 'Not at all (minor is middle)'.
2
Analyze the case when the middle term is true
If the middle term (the proposition 'minor is middle') is true, then the minor premise must be false (otherwise the major would be false). The falsity of the minor premise (which is part of the conclusion) makes the conditional conclusion true.
This is a reductio: assuming the conclusion false leads to contradiction.
3
Analyze the case when the middle term is false
If the middle term is false, then the major premise (which implies the middle) must be false. The falsity of the major premise also makes the conclusion true.
Both cases force the conclusion to be true, proving productiveness.
4
Conclude the outcome
Since both possibilities lead to the truth of the conclusion, the syllogism is productive and yields a specific accidental conclusion.
The conclusion is of the same type as the minor premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الضرب الثالث إن كانت الصغرى اتفاقية خاصة ينتج اتفاقية خاصة؛ إذ الأوسط المسلوب عنه الأصغر إما أن يكون صادقًا؛ وحينئذ يلزم كذب الأصغر وإلا لكذبت السالبة وكذب الأصغر مما يستلزم صدق النتيجة. وإما أن يكون كاذبا؛ وحينئذ يلزم كذب الأكبر لانتفاء ما يلزمه، وكذب الأكبر مما يستلزم صدق النتيجة أيضًا. وإن كانت الاتفاقية عامة فصدقها يقتضي كون الأصغر كاذبًا في نفسه أو كونه منافيًا للأوسط وأيهما لزم يلزم صدق الاتفاقية الخاصة؛ ولا يلزم صدق العامة لاحتمال كذب الأصغر وصدق الأوسط والأكبر مع كون الأكبر مستلزما للأوسط، وعدم كونه منافيًا للأصغر كما في قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان الخلاء موجودًا، وكلما كان الإنسان حساسًا كان الإنسان حيوانا فإنه لا يصدق ليس البتة إذا كان الخلاء موجودًا كان الإنسان حساسا؛ إذ الخلاء لا يكون موجودًا إلا أن يكون الإنسان حساسا لما أنه حساس في نفسه؛ ويلزم منه أن يكون حساسًا مع غيره. وإن كان بالعكس كما إذا كانت الصغرى لزومية والكبرى اتفاقية فإنه لا ينتج أصلا لاحتمال صدق الحدود مع التلازم بين الأوسط والأصغر وبين الأصغر والأكبر كما في قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان السواد لونا كانت الثلاثة عددًا، وكلما كانت الثلاثة فردًا كان السواد لونًا مع أنه لا يصدق قد لا يكون إذا كانت الثلاثة عددًا كانت فردا؛ فهذه الأحكام وإن كانت قطعية عند الأكثر من المتقدمين فقد كانت في حيز المنع لما مر من قبل.
And in the third figure: If the minor premise is a specific accidental, it produces a specific accidental [conclusion]. For the middle [term] which is denied from the minor is either true; then the falsity of the minor follows, otherwise the negative would be false, and the falsity of the minor necessitates the truth of the conclusion. Or it is false; then the falsity of the major follows due to the non-existence of what implies it, and the falsity of the major also necessitates the truth of the conclusion. And if the [minor premise] is a general accidental, then its truth requires that the minor is false in itself or that it is opposed to the middle; whichever follows, the truth of the specific accidental [conclusion] follows; but the truth of the general [accidental conclusion] does not follow due to the possibility that the minor is false while the middle and major are true with the major implying the middle but not being opposite to the minor, as in our saying: Not at all when man is an animal is void existent, and whenever man is sentient, man is an animal; then it is not true that not at all when void exists is man sentient, for void does not exist unless man is sentient, since he is sentient in himself; and it follows from that that he is sentient along with other things. And if the opposite [i.e., the minor is necessary and the major accidental], then it does not produce at all due to the possibility of the terms being true with the connection between the middle and minor and between the minor and major, as in our saying: Not at all when blackness is a color is three a number, and whenever three is odd, blackness is a color, even though it is not true that it may be that when three is a number, it is odd. So these rulings, even though they are decisive for most of the early [scholars], have been in the realm of suspension due to what has passed earlier.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage focuses on the third figure of conditional syllogism. The author first considers the case where the minor premise is a specific accidental (ittifāqiyya khāṣṣa). He argues that it yields a specific accidental conclusion through a reductio reasoning: if the middle term (the proposition that is negated of the minor) is true, then the minor must be false (to keep the negative premise true), and the falsity of the minor (which is part of the conclusion) makes the conditional conclusion true; if the middle is false, then the major must be false (since the major implies the middle), and the falsity of the major also makes the conclusion true. Next, if the minor premise is a general accidental, its truth forces that the minor is either false or contradictory to the middle; in either case the specific accidental conclusion follows, but the general accidental conclusion does not necessarily follow. He gives an example: 'Not at all when man is an animal is void existent' (i.e., void never exists when man is an animal) and 'Whenever man is sentient, he is an animal' — from these one cannot conclude 'Not at all when void exists is man sentient' because void's existence actually requires man to be sentient (since man is essentially sentient), so the conclusion would be false. Then the author considers the reverse scenario: when the minor is necessary (luzūmiyya) and the major accidental. Here, the syllogism fails entirely because the terms could all be true with the required connections but the conclusion does not follow. The example involves 'Not at all when blackness is a color is three a number' and 'Whenever three is odd, blackness is a color' — but it does not follow that 'Not at all when three is a number is it odd'. The author notes that these rulings, though considered certain by most earlier scholars, are subject to contention due to earlier points (possibly about the nature of conditional connections).

Key Terms
اتفاقية خاصة
اتفاقية عامة
لزومية
ناتج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the third figure, if the minor premise is a specific accidental, what does the author conclude?
A) The syllogism is not productive.
B) It produces a specific accidental conclusion.
C) It produces a general accidental conclusion.
D) It produces a necessary conclusion.

Key Takeaway
A specific accidental minor in the third figure always yields a specific accidental conclusion through a reductio proof that covers both truth values of the middle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Third Figure with General Accidental and Mixed Premises
Author examines general accidental and mixed cases, giving counterexamples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish which premise combinations in the third figure yield conclusions (specific accidental) and which yield none, using the author's examples.

Core Idea
A general accidental minor yields only a specific accidental conclusion; mixing necessary and accidental premises often leads to no conclusion.

Having dealt with the specific accidental minor, the author now turns to the general accidental minor and the reverse case where the minor is necessary and the major accidental. He shows that the type of conclusion is limited and that some combinations are entirely unproductive. These nuances are critical for correctly applying conditional syllogisms in Islamic legal theory and theology.

Guided Path
1
Examine the general accidental minor case
If the minor premise is a general accidental (ittifāqiyya 'āmma), then its truth forces either the falsity of the minor term or its opposition to the middle. Both lead to the truth of a specific accidental conclusion, but not a general one.
The conclusion is weaker than the minor premise.
2
Understand why a general conclusion does not follow
It is possible that the minor term is false while the middle and major are true, with the major implying the middle but not contradicting the minor. In that case, the general accidental conclusion would be false.
The author provides a concrete example: 'Not at all when man is an animal is void existent' and 'Whenever man is sentient, man is an animal' do not imply 'Not at all when void exists is man sentient'.
3
Analyze the mixed case (necessary minor, accidental major)
When the minor is necessary (luzūmiyya) and the major accidental, no conclusion follows at all. The terms can be true with the required connections but the conclusion fails.
Example: 'Not at all when blackness is a color is three a number' and 'Whenever three is odd, blackness is a color' do not imply 'Not at all when three is a number is it odd'.
4
Evaluate the author's closing remark
The author notes that these rulings, though considered certain by most early scholars, are disputed due to earlier discussions (possibly about the nature of conditional connections). This reminds us that logical rules are not always uncontested.
This invites students to engage critically with the tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الضرب الثالث إن كانت الصغرى اتفاقية خاصة ينتج اتفاقية خاصة؛ إذ الأوسط المسلوب عنه الأصغر إما أن يكون صادقًا؛ وحينئذ يلزم كذب الأصغر وإلا لكذبت السالبة وكذب الأصغر مما يستلزم صدق النتيجة. وإما أن يكون كاذبا؛ وحينئذ يلزم كذب الأكبر لانتفاء ما يلزمه، وكذب الأكبر مما يستلزم صدق النتيجة أيضًا. وإن كانت الاتفاقية عامة فصدقها يقتضي كون الأصغر كاذبًا في نفسه أو كونه منافيًا للأوسط وأيهما لزم يلزم صدق الاتفاقية الخاصة؛ ولا يلزم صدق العامة لاحتمال كذب الأصغر وصدق الأوسط والأكبر مع كون الأكبر مستلزما للأوسط، وعدم كونه منافيًا للأصغر كما في قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان الخلاء موجودًا، وكلما كان الإنسان حساسًا كان الإنسان حيوانا فإنه لا يصدق ليس البتة إذا كان الخلاء موجودًا كان الإنسان حساسا؛ إذ الخلاء لا يكون موجودًا إلا أن يكون الإنسان حساسا لما أنه حساس في نفسه؛ ويلزم منه أن يكون حساسًا مع غيره. وإن كان بالعكس كما إذا كانت الصغرى لزومية والكبرى اتفاقية فإنه لا ينتج أصلا لاحتمال صدق الحدود مع التلازم بين الأوسط والأصغر وبين الأصغر والأكبر كما في قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان السواد لونا كانت الثلاثة عددًا، وكلما كانت الثلاثة فردًا كان السواد لونًا مع أنه لا يصدق قد لا يكون إذا كانت الثلاثة عددًا كانت فردا؛ فهذه الأحكام وإن كانت قطعية عند الأكثر من المتقدمين فقد كانت في حيز المنع لما مر من قبل.
And in the third figure: If the minor premise is a specific accidental, it produces a specific accidental [conclusion]. For the middle [term] which is denied from the minor is either true; then the falsity of the minor follows, otherwise the negative would be false, and the falsity of the minor necessitates the truth of the conclusion. Or it is false; then the falsity of the major follows due to the non-existence of what implies it, and the falsity of the major also necessitates the truth of the conclusion. And if the [minor premise] is a general accidental, then its truth requires that the minor is false in itself or that it is opposed to the middle; whichever follows, the truth of the specific accidental [conclusion] follows; but the truth of the general [accidental conclusion] does not follow due to the possibility that the minor is false while the middle and major are true with the major implying the middle but not being opposite to the minor, as in our saying: Not at all when man is an animal is void existent, and whenever man is sentient, man is an animal; then it is not true that not at all when void exists is man sentient, for void does not exist unless man is sentient, since he is sentient in himself; and it follows from that that he is sentient along with other things. And if the opposite [i.e., the minor is necessary and the major accidental], then it does not produce at all due to the possibility of the terms being true with the connection between the middle and minor and between the minor and major, as in our saying: Not at all when blackness is a color is three a number, and whenever three is odd, blackness is a color, even though it is not true that it may be that when three is a number, it is odd. So these rulings, even though they are decisive for most of the early [scholars], have been in the realm of suspension due to what has passed earlier.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage focuses on the third figure of conditional syllogism. The author first considers the case where the minor premise is a specific accidental (ittifāqiyya khāṣṣa). He argues that it yields a specific accidental conclusion through a reductio reasoning: if the middle term (the proposition that is negated of the minor) is true, then the minor must be false (to keep the negative premise true), and the falsity of the minor (which is part of the conclusion) makes the conditional conclusion true; if the middle is false, then the major must be false (since the major implies the middle), and the falsity of the major also makes the conclusion true. Next, if the minor premise is a general accidental, its truth forces that the minor is either false or contradictory to the middle; in either case the specific accidental conclusion follows, but the general accidental conclusion does not necessarily follow. He gives an example: 'Not at all when man is an animal is void existent' (i.e., void never exists when man is an animal) and 'Whenever man is sentient, he is an animal' — from these one cannot conclude 'Not at all when void exists is man sentient' because void's existence actually requires man to be sentient (since man is essentially sentient), so the conclusion would be false. Then the author considers the reverse scenario: when the minor is necessary (luzūmiyya) and the major accidental. Here, the syllogism fails entirely because the terms could all be true with the required connections but the conclusion does not follow. The example involves 'Not at all when blackness is a color is three a number' and 'Whenever three is odd, blackness is a color' — but it does not follow that 'Not at all when three is a number is it odd'. The author notes that these rulings, though considered certain by most earlier scholars, are subject to contention due to earlier points (possibly about the nature of conditional connections).

اتفاقية عامة
لزومية

Non-Productive Figures in Conditional Syllogisms
Author shows that the fourth and fifth figures fail to produce conclusions under various premise combinations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the fourth and fifth figures of conditional syllogisms are non-productive (la yantij) and test a given conditional syllogism in these figures for validity.

Core Idea
Not all figure placements yield a conclusion; some are structurally non-productive even with true premises.

The author examines the fourth and fifth figures of conditional syllogisms, showing that they never produce valid conclusions regardless of whether the minor premise is accidental or necessary. Through examples, he demonstrates the logical vulnerabilities that prevent these figures from being productive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figures
Recognize that the fourth and fifth figures are variations of the conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i). In these figures, the minor premise (al-sughra) corresponds to the antecedent or consequent in specific patterns that the author deems non-productive.
The exact pattern is not given, but the author shows that no conclusion follows.
2
Examine the accidental minor premise case (fourth figure)
When the minor premise is accidental (ittifāqiyya), the syllogism fails because the terms could be true with a connection between minor and major, yet no conclusion follows. The example: 'Whenever blackness is a color, three is a number' (true accidentally) and 'Not at all when three is odd is blackness a color' (true). One cannot infer 'Not at all when three is a number is it odd' because three being a number is always true but sometimes odd.
The connection between minor and major exists but does not guarantee the conclusion.
3
Examine the necessary minor premise case (fourth figure)
When the minor premise is necessary (luzūmiyya), the syllogism also fails because the middle term (al-awsat) could be false while the extremes are true. Example: 'Whenever man is a horse, he is an animal' (antecedent false but conditional holds) and 'Not at all when he is rational is he a horse' (true). No conclusion follows because the middle term (the proposition about being a horse) is false in the first premise.
The necessary connection in the minor does not rescue the figure.
4
Apply the same logic to the fifth figure
The fifth figure is identical to the fourth in its non-productivity. The author uses the same example with blackness and three to reinforce that both figures share the same ruling.
The author concludes with an exhortation to reflect on these findings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الضرب الرابع إذا كانت الصغرى اتفاقية فإنه لا ينتج لاحتمال صدق الحدود مع التلازم بين الأصغر والأكبر كما في قولنا كلما كان السواد لونا كانت الثلاثة عددًا، وليس البتة إذا كانت الثلاثة فردًا كان السواد لونًا. ولا يصدق قد لا يكون إذا كانت الثلاثة عددًا كانت فردا؛ وفيه من الكلام كما في الضرب الثالث. وكذلك إذا كانت الصغرى لزومية فإنه لا ينتج لاحتمال كذب الأوسط وصدق الطرفين مع التلازم بين الأصغر والأكبر كما في قولنا كلما كان الإنسان فرسًا كان حيوانا، وليس البتة إذا كان ناطقا كان فرسا. وعلى هذا في الضرب الخامس فإنه مع الرابع على السواء فيقال مثلا قد يكون إذا كان السواد لونا كانت الثلاثة عددًا، وليس البتة إذا كانت الثلاثة فردًا كان السواد لونا ولا يمكن صدق النتيجة؛ وفيه من الكلام أيضًا كما مر: فاعتبر بما عرفت
And in the fourth figure: If the minor premise is accidental, then it does not produce due to the possibility of the terms being true with the connection between the minor and major, as in our saying: Whenever blackness is a color, three is a number, and not at all when three is odd is blackness a color. And it is not true that it may not be when three is a number, it is odd; and there is discussion about it as in the third figure. Likewise, if the minor premise is necessary, then it does not produce due to the possibility of the middle being false and the two extremes true with the connection between the minor and major, as in our saying: Whenever man is a horse, he is an animal, and not at all when he is rational is he a horse. And likewise in the fifth figure: it is the same as the fourth, so it is said, for example: It may be when blackness is a color, three is a number, and not at all when three is odd is blackness a color, and it is impossible for the conclusion to be true; and there is discussion about it as has passed. So reflect on what you have learned.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage covers the fourth and fifth figures of conditional syllogisms. The author declares both to be non-productive (la yantij). For the fourth figure, he considers two cases: first, when the minor premise is accidental (ittifāqiyya) — it fails because the terms could be true while a connection holds between minor and major, but no valid conclusion follows. The example uses 'Whenever blackness is a color, three is a number' (both true accidentally) and 'Not at all when three is odd is blackness a color' (true because oddness does not imply blackness being a color). One cannot correctly infer 'Not at all when three is a number is it odd' because three being a number is always true but it is sometimes odd. The author notes that the reasoning here is similar to the discussion in the third figure. Second, when the minor premise is necessary (luzūmiyya) — it also fails due to the possibility that the middle term (the proposition shared) is false while the extremes are true, again with a connection between minor and major. Example: 'Whenever man is a horse, he is an animal' (the antecedent is false but the conditional holds) and 'Not at all when he is rational is he a horse' (true) — one cannot conclude anything about man being rational and animal? The conclusion is not given but it's impossible. Then the fifth figure is said to be identical to the fourth, sharing the same examples and rulings: for instance, the same example with blackness and three is used, and the conclusion is impossible. The passage ends with an exhortation to reflect on these rulings.

Invalid Moods in Conditional Syllogisms: Lizumiyya and Ittifaqiyya Premises
Author examines invalid moods when one premise is lizumiyya and the other ittifaqiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify invalid mood combinations when one premise is implicative (lizumiyya) and the other is accidental (ittifaqiyya) in a conditional syllogism.

Core Idea
Not all combinations of necessary and accidental premises yield a valid conclusion.

In conditional syllogisms, premises can be either necessary implications (lizumiyya) or accidental correlations (ittifaqiyya). The classical logicians examined which combinations produce valid conclusions. This passage explores several invalid moods, showing that even when both premises are true, the conclusion may not follow due to the possibility of false terms or lack of entailment.

Guided Path
1
Identify premise types
Distinguish between lizumiyya (necessary implication) and ittifaqiyya (accidental correlation) premises. Note that lizumiyya implies a necessary connection, while ittifaqiyya does not.
This distinction is critical for evaluating syllogistic validity.
2
Analyze the first invalid combination
Consider the case where the major premise is lizumiyya and the minor is ittifaqiyya. The author gives the example: major 'Not at all if man is an animal then void exists' (lizumiyya), minor 'Whenever man is sensitive then man is an animal' (ittifaqiyya). The conclusion 'Not at all if void exists then man is sensitive' does not follow.
The failure occurs because the minor term could be false while the middle and major are true, and the major entails the middle but not the minor.
3
Analyze the second invalid combination
Now consider the reverse: minor lizumiyya and major ittifaqiyya. Example: minor 'Not at all if blackness is a colour then three is a number' (lizumiyya), major 'Whenever three is odd then blackness is a colour' (ittifaqiyya). No conclusion follows because the terms could be true with entailment between middle and minor, and between minor and major, but not producing the desired conclusion.
The author states this combination 'produces nothing at all'.
4
Generalize the principle
The invalidity stems from the possibility of the terms being true without the required entailment chain from minor to major through the middle. The accidental premise lacks necessity, allowing exceptions.
Understanding these exceptions prevents erroneous inferences in theological and jurisprudential reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كاذبًا في نفسه أو كونه منافيًا للأوسط وأيهما لزم يلزم صدق الاتفاقية الخاصة؛ ولا يلزم صدق العامة لاحتمال كذب الأصغر وصدق الأوسط والأكبر مع كون الأكبر مستلزما للأوسط، وعدم كونه منافيًا للأصغر كما في قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان الإنسان حيوانا كان الخلاء موجودًا، وكلَّما كان الإنسان حساسًا كان الإنسان حيوانا فإنه لا يصدق ليس البتة إذا كان الخلاء موجودًا كان الإنسان حساسا؛ إذ الخلاء لا يكون موجودًا إلا أن يكون الإنسان حساسا لما أنه حساس في نفسه؛ ويلزم منه أن يكون حساسًا مع غيره. وإن كان بالعكس كما إذا كانت الصغرى لزومية والكبرى اتفاقية فإنه لا ينتج أصلا لاحتمال صدق الحدود مع التلازم بين الأوسط والأصغر وبين الأصغر والأكبر كما في قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان السواد لونا كانت الثلاثة عددًا، وكلما كانت الثلاثة فردًا كان السواد لونًا مع أنه لا يصدق قد لا يكون إذا كانت الثلاثة عددًا كانت فردا؛ فهذه الأحكام وإن كانت قطعية عند الأكثر من المتقدمين فقد كانت في حيز المنع لما مر من قبل. وفي الضرب الرابع إذا كانت الصغرى اتفاقية فإنه لا ينتج لاحتمال صدق الحدود مع التلازم بين الأصغر والأكبر كما في قولنا كلما كان السواد لونا كانت الثلاثة عددًا، وليس البتة إذا كانت الثلاثة فردًا كان السواد لونًا. ولا يصدق قد لا يكون إذا كانت الثلاثة عددًا كانت فردا؛ وفيه من الكلام كما في الضرب ] الثالث. وكذلك إذا كانت الصغرى لزومية فإنه لا ينتج لاحتمال كذب الأوسط وصدق الطرفين مع التلازم بين الأصغر والأكبر كما في قولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان فرسًا كان حيوانا ، وليس البتة إذا كان ناطقا كان فرسا. وعلى هذا في الضرب الخامس فإنه مع الرابع على السواء فيقال مثلا قد يكون إذا كان السواد لونا كانت الثلاثة عددًا، وليس البتة إذا كانت الثلاثة فردًا كان السواد لونا ولا يمكن صدق النتيجة ؛ وفيه من الكلام أيضًا كما مر : فاعتبر بما عرفت
False in itself or being contrary to the middle term; whichever holds, the truth of the particular accidental follows, but the truth of the general does not follow due to the possibility of the minor being false while the middle and major are true, together with the major being entailed by the middle and not being contrary to the minor—as in our saying: 'Not at all if man is an animal then void exists' and 'Whenever man is sensitive then man is an animal'—for it is not true that 'Not at all if void exists then man is sensitive', since void does not exist unless man is sensitive because he is sensitive in himself, and it would entail that he is sensitive with something else. And if it is the opposite, such as when the minor is implicative and the major is accidental, then it produces nothing at all, due to the possibility of the terms being true together with the entailment between the middle and the minor and between the minor and the major—as in our saying: 'Not at all if blackness is a colour then three is a number' and 'Whenever three is odd then blackness is a colour' while it is not true that 'Maybe not if three is a number then it is odd'. These judgments, even if they are certain according to most of the early scholars, were subject to denial for what preceded. In the fourth mode, if the minor is accidental, then it produces nothing due to the possibility of the terms being true together with the entailment between the minor and the major—as in our saying: 'Whenever blackness is a colour then three is a number' and 'Not at all if three is odd then blackness is a colour'—and it is not true that 'Maybe not if three is a number then it is odd'; and it involves discussion as in the third mode. Likewise, if the minor is implicative, then it produces nothing due to the possibility of the middle being false while both extremes are true together with the entailment between the minor and the major—as in our saying: 'Whenever man is a horse then he is an animal' and 'Not at all if he is rational then he is a horse'. And thus in the fifth mode, it is the same as the fourth; for example, one may say: 'Maybe if blackness is a colour then three is a number' and 'Not at all if three is odd then blackness is a colour', and the conclusion cannot be true; and it involves discussion as passed. So consider what you have learned.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of invalid moods in conditional syllogisms (al-muttasilat) when one premise is implicative (lizumiyya) and the other is accidental (ittifaqiyya). The author examines cases where the minor and major premises have different modalities, showing why certain combinations fail to produce a valid conclusion. He begins by considering a scenario where the major premise is implicative and the minor accidental: even if the particular accidental conclusion holds, the general does not, because of the possibility that the minor term is false while the middle and major are true, with the major entailing the middle but not being contrary to the minor. An example is given using 'man', 'animal', 'sensitive', and 'void'. Then he considers the reverse (minor implicative, major accidental): no conclusion follows because the terms could be true while there is entailment between the middle and minor, and between minor and major, as in the example with 'blackness', 'colour', 'three', and 'odd'. The author notes that these judgments, though considered certain by most earlier logicians, were contested. He then addresses the fourth mode: if the minor is accidental, no conclusion follows (example with blackness and three); and if the minor is implicative, similarly no conclusion (example with man and horse). The fifth mode is treated similarly. The passage ends with an admonition to consider the conditions in premises and use them correctly, as understanding them in implicatives suffices without needing to examine accidentals separately.

اتفاقية
الصغرى
الكبرى

A) The conclusion is always valid.
B) The conclusion is always invalid.
C) The conclusion may be valid if certain conditions are met.
D) The conclusion is impossible to determine.

Key Takeaway
When one premise is necessary and the other accidental, the conclusion does not necessarily follow; careful analysis of entailments is required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Second Division of Conditional Syllogisms: Incomplete Common Parts
Author introduces the second type of conditional syllogism where the common part is incomplete, and enumerates the possible moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of sharing of incomplete parts in conditional syllogisms and calculate the number of possible moods per figure.

Core Idea
When the common part is a term, not a proposition, conditional syllogisms have four patterns of sharing.

In the first division of conditional syllogisms, the common part was a complete proposition. The author now turns to the second division, where the shared part is an incomplete component—a term like the antecedent or consequent of a conditional. This distinction leads to four types of sharing and a combinatorial explosion of possible moods. Understanding this structure is essential for analyzing complex arguments in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two premises
Recognize that in this division, the minor premise is one of the four quantified categorical propositions, and the major is a conditional proposition (muttasila).
The minor is categorical, the major is conditional.
2
Identify the four types of sharing
The shared incomplete part can be (1) between the antecedents, (2) between the consequents, (3) between the antecedent of one and the consequent of the other, or (4) between the consequent of one and the antecedent of the other.
These correspond to the four possible pairings of the two shared terms.
3
Calculate the possible moods
Start with 4 types of categorical minors (A, E, I, O) × 4 types of conditional majors (also A, E, I, O) = 16 premise-pairs. Then each of the two shared terms also has 4 quantifications, so 16 × 16 = 256 possible moods per figure per division.
The unshared parts do not affect the count.
4
Note the conditions for productivity
Not all 256 moods are productive (yield a conclusion). Conditions include that the minor or major must be universal, and when the sharing is between consequents, the shared consequent must be positive.
The author begins to discuss these conditions but cuts off.
